ENTRANCE OF DISEASE.

no fewer than 38 different infections may be
traced with more or less directness to the
human mouth as a focal or starting point.
The mouth_ being the gateway for the en-
trance of disease, therefore it is here that the
fight must begin to keep the enemy out.

keep the mouth in a sweet, healthy condition.

The Red page
Marie Tarnowska
One of the most sensational murder trials
of recent years was that of a beautiful
Russian Countess, Marie Tarnowska, for
the murder at Venice in 1907 of Count
Kamarowsky. The Count, it will be remem-
bered, was shot by his young friend, Nau-
moff, and the wounded man himself assisted
his assailant to escape. Kamarowsky was
not fatally wounded, but the chief surgeon
of the hospital, in a sudden mental collapse
—he died in an insane asylum—ordered
the stitches in the wounds to be removed,
and thus caused his death. Kamarowsky’s
last words were a message of love to his
betrothed, Countess Tarnowska, in whose
favor he had made his will and insured his
life for £20,000. The Countess and a Rus-
sian lawyer, Donat Prilukofli, were arrested;
and after a remarkable trial these two were
found guilty of instigating the young
Naumoff to commit the murder. The
Countess was sentenced to eight years,
PrilukofE to 10 years, while Naumoff, who
had been incarcerated for two years await-
ing trial, was liberated.
The chief medical expert in the case sug-
gested to A. Vivanti Chartres, author of
The Devourers, that she should write the
story of the Countess’s life. Pie hints in
his letter to the author of Marie Tarnowska
(Heinemann) that the problem of this
extraordinary woman is mainly pathological,
and that the medical elements of the pro-
blem could not in such a work be discussed
or even clearly stated. His thesis is that:—
Moral obloquy in women is in most cases
due to pathological causes comparatively
easy .of diagnosis and of cure; that a woman-
criminal may be morally redeemed by being
physically healed; and that just as alco-
holism, typhus, pysemia or other modes of
toxic infection may result in delirium and
irresponsibility, so certain forms of disease
m women, by setting up a condition of
persistent organic poisoning, may and very
often do conduce to mental and moral aber-
ration and consequent crime.
And while the author states that this
book was not written to plead Marie Tar-
nowska’s. cause, and admits that several
deaths lie at her door, Vivanti Chartres
wields such a brilliant pSh and writes with
such. sympathetic insight that her book does
nothing but implicitly appeal to our sympa-
thies for the strange woman soon to be
released from prison. Marie Tarnowska
has found in Vivanti Chartres an advocate
more moving than even Oscar Wilde dis-
covered in himself. And though she pro-
fesses to have extenuated nothing, to have
colored nothing, her pen has, perforce,
created ah exquisite and moving piece of
fiction.
Doubtless her heroine’s attitude in
prison, in which there is to the callous
critic an element of the obvious pose, and
the medical expert’s pathological bias, have
influenced the author to stress that side of
her heroine’s character, to exhibit her,
for our pity, as a victim of an obscure
disease. And though the story is supposed
to be told in the Countess’s words, there is
too. much literary art, too brilliant a utili-
sation of the value of dramatic contrasts
and of romantic “situations” to carry veri-
similitude. If this is a human document it
is one decorated by another skilled hand.
We see the heroine first as a child of 16,
asked for in marriage by three titled Rus-
sians. She chose Vassili Tarnowska, and
ran away with him, after frightening her
mother by pretended (or real?) convul-
sions. The doctor said there were symptoms
of epilepsy, due to a fall from a swing. The
17-year-old bride was nothing but “a bore”
to her dissolute husband.
My untutored adolescence was trailed
from revelry to revelry, from banquet to
orgy; my innocence swept into the mael-
strom of a licentious life. I was forced to
look into the depths of every depravity; to
my lips was proffered every chalice of
shame.
But she had a child. In the phrase that
is surely not Tarnowska’s, “I had no
thought, I had no desire. Pale and pure
I sat enthroned in the milk-white paradise
of maternity.”
And after the birth of another child she
met Bozevsky—and Dr. Stahl, with his
morphia syringe—“the little glags viper, the
syringe of Pravaz, whose fang is a hollow
needle.” Bozevsky, “the gallant young Pole,
the handsomest officer in the Imperial
Guards,” pursues her with his passion. Her
husband negligently laughs, her aunt,
“rectilinear and asexual in her grey flannel
dressing-gown,” advises her to trust in Pro-
vidence and camomile tea.
Came an entertainment at the Tarnowska
house, where the guests for amusement shot
at bottles in the garden. Marie did not dare
fire the gun unless somebody covered her
ears. Bozevsky performed that friendly
office, and took occasion to whisp ”, “Dar-
ling, darling, darling! I love yo ” And
all were drinking, heavily. Mad revels fol-
lowed’ in the midst of which the nurse
called Marie to her child. She kissed him
good-night, and he murmured, “You smell
of many tilings—of perfume and powder
and cigarettes and wine.” A charming
dramatic touch—surely not Marie’s. Next
morning* resolved to live a virtuous life, she
chanced to meet Bozevsky in the garden.
Again she aimed at a bottle.
I was about to press the trigger when
suddenly Bozevsky, with a lightning move-
ment, put out his hand and pressed his palm
against the muzzle of my gun.
“Wait!” he cried, with a wild, extra-
vagant laugh. “Wait a moment! Before
you press the trigger I want you to say,
‘Alexis, I love you!’ ”
“You are mad!” I exclaimed. “Take
away your hand!”
He did not move.
“Take it away, I implore you!”
Still he never moved, and I could see that
hand stopping the muzzle of my gun....
and I felt almost as if I were under the
influence of some hallucination. It was
not. only his hand that I saw—l seemed in
a kind of frenzy to see the hands of all men
outstretched before me, ready to grasp me,
to crush me, to beat me down. Doubtless a
wave of madness swept over me; a con-
vulsive spasm shook my wrist—and the gun
went off. I saw .the long, lithe hand drop
like some wounded creature.
With a cry I let the rifle fall and covered
my face. But Bozevsky had sprung upon
me. “You little tigress,” he gasped. Then
he covered my mouth with his shattered
hand, and I felt the blood gush over my
face.
What distant heritage of madness broke
upon us at that moment? What primitive
frenzy lashed us together in a fierce
embrace? I cannot tell. All I know is
that from that hour I was his—tamed, van-
quished, broken in spirit, and yet glad.
Her husband’s jealousy grew frenzied.
But friends prevailed upon him to give a
supper to Bozevsky and patch up an osten-
sible reconciliation. It was a supper that
the author has made strangely dramatic,
leading to its theatrical “curtain” when
Vassili, maddened by drink and jealousy,
shot Bozevsky as he bent to kiss Marie’s
hand in a final farewell. Bozevsky did not
die. for many days, and his death was a
thrillin.gly horrible one, according to
Yivanti Chartres. Vassili was acquitted—
justifiable homicide. Stahl committed sui-
cide,. after having made Marie a morphia
maniac. Marie fled with her son, aimlessly
Accustomed to almost wasteful
luxury, soon she found herself without
money. She met Prilukoff, whom she had
known in Russia. “Leave all that to me,”
he said, and Marie soon found that she had
to leave everything to this sinister, domi-
neering personality. She drifted, submissive
to her fate. Then she met Count Kamarow-
sky, a widower with one child. Their chil-
dren charmingly made friends.
“Do you like pelicans?” Tioka inquired
suddenly.
“No,” said Grania.
“Neither do I,” said Tioka; and there
was a long silence.
“Do you like ducks?” asked Tioka.
“Yes,” replied Grania.
“So do I,” said Tioka; and their friend-
ship was sealed.
So we reach the complicated intrigue
which was to shut the prison doors upon
Prilukoff and Marie, ruin Naumoff and
kill Kamarowsky. Marie pleads madness;
but there was in her madness always a
method—a method made sordid by the need
of money. Incredibly dramatic scenes take
place, all the paraphernalia of conspiracy,
spying and intrigue are romantically utilised,
and the final tragedy is made inevitable by
Marie’s superstitions and obsessions. Prilu-
koff instilled into her unbalanced mind the
idea that the life of her child —it was seri-
ously ill—could be saved only by the death
of Kamarowsky. But the two conspirators
weakened in their hellish desire, but when
one shrank back from the crime the other
grew the more determined. Naumoff, the
youthful lover of Marie, had to be deceived,
led to believe that Kamarowsky’s death was
necessary; the method of the crime had to
be . thought out; difficulties postponed the
action; Naumoff held back; Kamarowsky
had to be tricked into insuring his life for
Marie, his betrothed.
Then began the ghastly game, the sinister
comedy with the three puppets, whose
strings. I. held in my fragile hands. I had to
tranquillise and disarm Kamarowsky; to
kindle and fan the murderous fury of Prilu-
koff, and above all to enchain and infatuate
Naumoff, so. as to impel him to the crime
.... I was indeed the modern Circe, weav-
ing her evil spell. I was fervent and
temerarious, full of exotic anomalies, eccen-
tric, unexpected.... I cut my initials in his
arm with the triangular blade of a dagger;
I pressed my lighted cigarette upon his
hand; I assumed all the absurdities, per-
versities -&nd puerilities with which since
time, immemorial woman has decoyed and
beguiled man. who, after all, is essentially
a simple-minded and ingenuous being.
No, despite her advocate’s efforts, the real
woman emerges as a weak and degenerate
criminal; her crime was mean and sordid.
She deserved all she got. But what a theme
this crime would be for a picture-show!
The U.S. Magazine Market for
Australians
In B. 12/12/’l4 Albert Dorrington gave
Australian writers some idea of the literary
market in London, and his article no doubt
has saved several local writers from wasting
time and stamps trying to get their MSS.
accepted by magazine editors in the Big
Smoke. James Francis Dwyer, another Aus-
tralian writer, who is said to have made
good in U.S.A.. during a recent visit to
Sydney advised Australian writers to sene
their stories to America, where there was
dearth of this class of copy, and every maga
zine editor in that country was asking fo
a glance at the work of new writers. Mr
Dwyer also stated . that the writer wh
could produce the goods could demand any
price. Now I want to have a say. *;
know something about American editors
and a visit to the States, 15 months bac
convinced me that the magazines through
out that country are conducted on a policy
which must of necessity prove absolutely
fatal to the aspirations of the majority o :
new writers. Every periodical of any im
portance is endeavoring to secure as many
popular writers as possible. These well
known authors, therefore, occupy the greate
portion of the available space in the maga
zines, and the obscure author can coun
himself exceedingly fortunate, indeed, if h
ever finds his MS. published along with
those of the veteran contributors. There i
no question as to the immense field for fic
tion in the U.S.A. magazine world, but thi
field is occupied so completely by the popu
lar authors as to exclude almost entirely the
less fortunate writer. And this condition
is brought about solely by the policy of the
magazine publishers. Take the case of any
one publisher of a monthly American periodi
cal. He has recently added to his list of
monthly contributors a writer who is rapidly
becoming a top-uotcher. This author he
makes exclusively his own by means of an
ironclad contract, securing to himself the
work of this individual for months or even
years to come. When the publisher has
secured a full list of contributors in this way
he proceeds to bring them prominently before
the public by lavish expenditures in wide-
spread advertising. It can readily be seen
from these facts that the publisher has
neither use nor space for the lesser-known
writer. The merit of the outsider’s work is
immaterial to him.
Under the caption, "A Famine in Short
Stories," the New Yobk Heeald recently
said editorially :
According to our contemporary, the Bookman,
there is a famine in short stories, due to different
causes, not the least of which is the fact that the
story-writers are now busying themselves with play-
building. The others, it would seem, have lost the
gift of invention, for never before have editors found
it so hard to obtain what they call “bright, read-
able stories." One of these editors says that “since
O. Henry’s death it has been utterly impossible for
him to secure for his publication a short story that
is original in any way.” When we consider that O.
Henry’s manuscript was offered for two years with-
out a buyer, we are led to suspect that the fault may
lie_ in the editors themselves rather than in the
writers, and this theory is borne out by the experience
of everyone, who has written for magazines long
enough to discover the cast-iron rules and moss-grown
traditions by which they are governed. And every
one of these writers knows that it is much easier
to sell commonplace matter than anything novel in
theme or form.
A retired publisher, in a signed letter to the
New York Sun (July 9, 1913), says in
part :■ —■
The young author has no chance other than the
chance in a lottery. His work may be good, but it
will need “the smell of the frying bacon” to be
brought across the firm’s private desk to get a hear-
ing. A leading “English best seller” is the exception
to prove the rule. A certain book was offered,
favorably endorsed by one reader in three and turned
back to the author. Two years later the favorable
reader went into business for himself, and the first
thing he did Was to look up the declined novel and
writer. He made an offer; it was accepted, and the
book published—to be the success of the year. With
our current literature in its present bizarre condi-
tion what change for the better can be expected in
the future? I retired from the publishing business
two years ago, and have a warm spot for the strug-
gling unknowns.
I may be excused from mentioning personal
experiences, because they are the same as
the experiences of hundreds of thousands of
other writers in the States. To withhold an
accepted manuscript for six months or a year
without paying for it is not just. It is
deuced mean business to keep a writer out
of his bread and butter for a long term, while
not allowing him the chance to sell to another
publisher. I submitted an article to 16 dif-
ferent American magazines and was over a
year in getting it back, much mutiliated. I
got 16 little slips of paper with the editorial
stereotype—with which every writer to the
press is familiar. Finally, after 14
months, I received £5 for this article
from the editor of one of the highest-
class publications in New York. But
Jack London’s manuscripts were returned to
him from New York publishing houses with
the regularity with which he sent them, and
Rex Beach carried the manuscript of The
Spoilers around in his pocket till it became
frayed and dog-eared before he found a pub-
lisher. Blessed are the patient!
In a recent issue of this paper I referred
to the English literary agent. I also found
him in America. I remember well my first
experience with him. I imagined, after an
almost endless number of rejections, that my
manuscripts were not available simply be-
couse I was unknown. About this time I
happened to see an advertisement to the effect
that a certain syndicate wanted a number of
short stories, articles, etc.; in fact, anything
which would put them in touch with amateur
writers. Immediately, I gathered together
all of my productions and sent them off. In
due time I received a letter irorn the concern
stating that their reader wished td examine
more of my work, with a view to publication
in book form. Of course I was delighted.
1 felt that I had arrived. A week passed.
Then a letter came. It stated that because
of my unusual talent, the publishers would
make me a special offer. For 165 dollars —
but, pshaw! you know the rest. Another
experience was with a magazine which adver-
tised for stories and poems. Upon receipt
of my manuscripts I was coolly informed
that the editor did not consider any material
for publication unless the author owned
shares in the enterprise, which were five
dollars each. The periodical was philan-
thropic in its motives, seeking to exploit the
wares of the great unknown and bring them
before the reading public.
Despite what Mr. Dwyer has said, I am
convinced that so far as America is con-
cerned the time has come for the unheralded
author to awake to the fact that his case is
hopeless just so long as the above conditions
exist in the U.S.A. magazine field.
BILL BOWYANG.
H. C. McKay joins the Brennan Society:
“I hope it is not too late to enter the great
cryptic competition, especially as the only
commentator hitherto published seems to
have out-brennaned C.J.B. himself. To me
the poem in question has a comparatively
simple explanation—it is an invocation to a
subconscious power, and the imagery is bor-
rowed from astronomy. The poet’s conscious-
ness—“the citadel wliere I would seem dis-
tinct” —his lit universe—is stirred and
vaguely influenced by the nameless impulses
beyond its bounds; his conscious thoughts,
like stars at the edge, fly off into the dark
and return infected with subconscious in-
stincts. The subconscious darkness or im-
pulse is even powerful enough to ‘intoler-
ably dilate ’ —draw out the stars (conscious
thoughts) in a tide —as if there were a huge
dark moon in space drawing them. Having
now given this version, I utterly refuse to
accept any of O. J. Brennan’s, having con-
sumed five gallons of midnight oil, 27 brain-
cells and four bedroom-jugs of beer in the
effort.
“When a student, I remember a lunatic
at Kew Asylum who tackled all visitors with
an arithmetical problem: If two horses and
nine cows plough a paddock of 12 acres in
three days, how long will 40 horses and a
cow take to plough double the area?’ When,
to placate him, you worked it out, his pity-
ing answer was: ‘Wrong. I got that at
first; but it seemed much too simple, so I
multiplied by nine cows and added in fresh
horses and a couple o’ mules, an’ took away
the number I first thought of, and shifted th’
dismal-point four places to th’ right.’ Bren-
nan is working on similar lines. He gets an
idea, and adds psychology, metaphysics and
astronomy to it ‘in case it is too simple.’ I
defy anybody to make sense of the latter part
of his poem who has not read Arrhenius’ con-
ception of all the suns, moons and planets in
the Universe aggregating at last in one giant
sphere, finally and forever. But is this poetry
or a course of lectures in atomic synthesis?
“I write in great agony, as I have just
been striving with the contortions of another
hard, E. J. Rupert Atkinson. Will any
specialist in Infinity, Chaos, Theology and
Ellipsis give me a clue to “Before the Altars”
in Wayside Poems? The poet, good fellow
as he is personally, can give me no help. ‘I
can’t reply to you,’ he writes when appealed
to. ‘But I stick to Berkely.’ It is high time
a Society for the Nailing of Poets to an
Explanation was founded, with power to cut
off their ink, beer and technological diction-
aries. ‘Bellerive’ is hereby nominated Presi-
dent. Subscription, one Disillusion.”
“John Drayman” has made the delightful
discovery that, long before the war, a well-
known cigarette manufacturing firm had
unconsciously defined the german. This firm
gives away a series of animal pictures with
a popular brand of cigarettes. No. 38, which
“John Drayman” encloses, too late, as his
entry for the german-defining competition,
shows a picture of the dachshund, and thus
defines that german animal: “A German
variety of dog which is very popular on the
Continent as a sporting dog, and is used to
draw the fox and badger. Dachshunds make
good companions, as they are as a rule very
docile, but, given the opportunity, they cause
havoc amongst poultry, destroying the birds
for the mere lust of killing. In color they
are usually black-and-tan, deep red, or liver-
colored; a white variety is also occasionally
met with, but is not popular.”

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Crazy Kate of Camperdown.
I hear all sorts of poets rave of girls,
From far Toowoomba south and west to
Hay,
Of Cornstalk figures and of rippling curls.
Broad bosoms and eyes honest as the day;
But, by the iove of Mike, I always frown
Except at crazy Kate of Camperdown.
This talk of skins of milk and roses tires me,
This skite of what she looks like makes
me wild.
It’s only homely virtue that inspires me,
I hate the “artless habits of a child” ;
But give to me, in ancient boots and gown,
The Lady Bottle-oli of Camperdown.
All through the week from Monday unto
Friday,
She gathers bottles in the old spring cart;
On Saturdays she makes herself look tidy
In her green silk she is a bonzer tart,
And a gem hat sticks saucy on her crown;
Angels, shield Crazy Kate of Camperdown!
I wait until the cart is full of bottles,
And then I go and word her for a sprat
(A coin men use to buy beer for their
throttles),
And then I up and make an end of that;
Some call me “loafer,” or some other noun,
But never Crazy Kate of Camperdown.
Heaven bless her! But for her I d have to
work ;
’Tis that old bottle-cart keeps wolves away.
The beauties of the harem of the Turk
Could never equal Kate, not any day.
The praises I do trumpet round the town
Of dear old Crazy Kate of. Camperdown.
N.S.W.
RANDOPH STOPOUT.
A Vision of Fountains.
Here where the earth is brown and barren.
Where trees are stunted and skies are pale,
Where dust from the dry plains wind-
tormented
Hangs in air like a stifling veil —
Here I am dreaming of snowy columns
That up through the wide boughs leap and
sway;
A glittering vision, fountains playing—
Fountains playing on a windy day.
The water flower swift!v budding;
Blooming, fading and still renewed.
And ever the petals falling, falling,
Till the tangled grasses are thickly dewed,
And the waving roses are jewel strewn,
And the low green boughs are hung with
pearls;
And ever the central shaft of splendor.
Wind-maddened, musical, sparkling, whirls
Broken in rainbow-misted mazes.
Scattering, flying drifts of spray.
This is my vision of fountains playing,
Splashing, swaying—
Fountains playing on a windy day.
The wind that ferries the white clouds over
Snatches the blossoms and strews them wide,
And veils itself in the fine-spun gauzes,
Laughing low like an eager bride;
And the fountains’ laughter is sweet with
singing
As they rise and fall in fantastic play;
A delicate vision that hangs displayed
In quivering plume and swift cascade,
A lovely vision of fountains playing—
Fountains playing on a windy day.
Spinning poised like joyous dancers
Tossing their slim, white arms aloft.
Bending, bowing in rhythmic measure
To the pattering crystal drumming soft
On the spread of the silver, singing pools,
While the trees a-nodding clap to time,
And wind and water twirl together
To the lilting heat of a happy rime,
Sweeping ever away, away.
This is my vision of fountains blowing,
The path of the breeze with diamonds sow-
ing i
A vision of fountains flashing, flowing—
Fountains playing on a windy day.
VICTORIA.
SYD C.
Song of the Vine.
When Springtide’s silver sandals tripped
Across the bloss’my meadows.
And purple-garbed the young Earth danced
From out the Winter’s shadows;
While gay September’s golden hair
Across the land was lying.
And overhead on sweeping wings
The wild galahs were flying ;
The new leaves clustered on the stems,
All ruddv-hued and sappy.
Yea. then the vine brought forth its bloom
While earth waxed rich and happy.
And while the languorous Summer bared
Unto the Sun her bosom,
A pulsing life rushed through the stem,
The fruit crept from the blossom.
The brown soil fed the swelling grape,
And, in the sunlight thrilling,
The honey wine-blood crept along,
The purple skin out-filling.
And, now the gracious Autumn’s come
To reap the cornfields yellow.
Upon a thousand burdened vines
The grapes hang lush and mellow.
In laughing bands the pickers move
Along the purple alleys;
The clicking of their flashing shears
Makes music in the valleys.
The wine-vat runs its crimson flow,
The must foams up for pleasure,
Gay Bacchus, lurking by the door.
Cries “Taste the vine’s mad treasure.”’
No score of years in it lies drowned,
Fresh-rough, it lacks their duty :
This sweet, wild wine is Nature’s draught,
And speaks a new land’s beauty.
N.s.w.
CHARLES FIRTH-PETTIGNER.

THURSDAY, APRIL 29, 1915.
THE FUTILITY OF BUILDING A
FENCE WITH ONE WIRE.
The idea that, if the Federal Parliament
forthwith imposes a heavy surcharge on
enemy manufactures, its obligation to the
nation in respect of tariff reform will be
sufficiently well discharged, appears to be
taking root in quite unexpected quarters.
At various meetings of manufacturers
throughout the Commonwealth recently—at
a deputation to the Minister for Customs
in Melbourne the other day—this point of
immediately jumping with both feet on Ger-
man trade was elevated into one of major
importance, • and a vast amount of breath
was expended on picturing the evils Aus-
tralia would suffer by vast quantities of
Teutonic goods being pushed over the coast
line —after the war. The reply is that the
war is neither over nor shows any definite
signs of being over soon ; that for the present
direct trade with the Plun has been cut off
at the meter, and his ships bottled up in
places where, instead of earninv subsidies,
they accumulate oysters on the bilge; and
that, anyway, the real business of the Com-
monwealth is not to discuss how on the
declaration of peace the trade of Germany
or any other country may be hampered, or
crippled, or killed outright, but by what
means manufacturing may be promoted here in
Australia —how it may best be protected dur-
ing the period of growth against the insidious
blight of foreign industry. This is the
problem Parliament has to deal with, and
the measure of the success with which it
handles its task will be determined not by
the alleged effectiveness of the bash it ad-
ministers an already-bashed von Spooken
stein, but by the actual expansion of Aus-
tralian enterprise.
*> * * *
When the war is over a weary old world
is going to find the weather very cold and
wet; all, or nearly all, its savings against a
rainy day shot off in smoke, with enormously
increased debts, an industrial position requir-
ing immediate readjustment, and a ravenous
demand for cash. A great part of
the world is going to be very poor; wages in
those places are going to be very gaunt and
the price of goods below zero, for only by
work will the team of dead horses be shifted.
And this will not be so in Germany only.
There are other countries badly affected,
are none where the pinch will not
be felt. What of Australia? Is going
then to depend on what she can borrow or
on what she can make and sell and save?
floes she propose to continue sending abroad
a steady stream of raw materials in order
that her population may be given a job with
pick and shovel and provided with a calico
shack on the outskirts of a port congested
by ships carrying away the products of sta-
tion and forest and mine, and bringing in the
output of foreign workshop and factory? Or
is she going now to get ready, even at the
eleventh hour, to do for herself what other
people do? If she is, the way is obvious.
If she is not, then it will be sufficient to
adopt a futile tariff and tack on to its knock-
kneed schedules a weapon to hit the irrelevant
German in the eye. After which the Par-
liament can give three cheers and adjourn
amidst a scene of great patriotic enthusiasm.
This frantic desire to deal with “the
menace of German imports” before sun-
down bears a strong family likeness to the
queer anxiety recently displayed to have a
conference of Premiers in London about “the
terms of peace.” What they were going to
talk about, and who they were going to talk
with, the gentle Bunyip only knows. The
conditions precedent to peace are not within
the region of human ken, nor is there any
“menace of German imports” on the horizon.
What should specially be done about this
particular menace when it begins to take
shape can be much more profitably dis-
cussed then than when it merely consists of
rumor, just as “peace” can more advantage-
ously be considered when warring parties,
having had enough stoush, are ready to bind
up their wounds. Considerations of trade
must inevitably take a prominent place in
adjustment of the broken bones. The com-
mercial relations that will eventually exist
between European countries —and between
Britain and Germany in particular—must
manifestly be a pronounced factor in deter-
mining the attitude of Australia towards her
present enemy. John Bull is a Free-
trader, or pretends to be, and may conceiv-
ably be prepared to continue his old-time
practice of securing from the German
a large volume of the things which
enter into manufacturing. That aspect
of the matter alone opens a page for some
pretty hard thinking by Australia, both
in regard to preference on British
imports (which may be half- or quarter-bred
Hun), and to schemes of to-day for making
trade in manufactures between the Common-
wealth and Germany as impossible as trade
in wheat between N. S. Wales and Victoria.
Trade with Hamburg on the basis of taking
from the German what Australians should
make for themselves is undesirable, and the
less they have of it the better. But what
about Chicago? How about Amsterdam and
Genoa and Tokio? What Australia needs
is Protection for the shirt and boot
and hat —for the axle, and horsecollar
and pair of socks —for everything her
people are capable of producing on
their own account, whether the com-
peting goods come from Tipperary or Tim-
buctoo. Economically it is all the same
whence they originate. And for Australia,
with her enormous public debt and unde-
veloped capacity for a sane mode of life, the
need is now more pressing than ever for a
tariff wall high enough to keep everyone back.
The extra row of spikes on top for the
special amusement of particular persons can
be put in position when the other 30b has
been finished according to specifications. To
stretch a piece of barbed-wire in the atmo-
phere will do nobody any good—or any
harm either.
ON THE SCRAP-HEAP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the end of the war Australia will have
to its debit 50 millions or so of new debt of
the worst and most unprofitable kind, and
the lossI—at 1 —at least temporarily—of 100,000
men. To its credit added prestige ; the ad-
vertisement of ability to put up a fight when
lawful occasion demands ; and maybe a new
Pacific empire with a million and a half
colored subjects calling for heavy expendi-
ture and careful administration before they
can be converted into machines for trade.
For a country with a practically republi-
can government, Imperialism will be no
more welcome to us than it has been to
America, and the million and a half of alien
subjects are worth less than nothing. It is
conquest with but a paper value; a Com-
monwealth with a territory as large as that
of the United States of America and a popu-
lation less than the population of New York
City, suddenly achieving 1,500,000 people
whom its religion of White Australia will
not permit it to receive as citizens. Aus-
tralia, for its spontaneous acceptance of the
duties of nationhood —an acceptance regard-
less of expense of money or the more splendid
gift of some of the best men in the world-
must be paid, and paid in men and women,
the best men and women in the world. And
that is just the payment the world doesn’t
like to make.
The Belgians accept money to feed their
starving civilians on the scrap-heap that
Junkerdom has made of Belgium—are ready
to accept money indefinitely—buj are quite
unready to let a better country welcome its
non-combatants. It has been a scrap-heap
on a small scale many times in history, and
has rebuilt itself as often; but never has it
or any other country had colossal problems
in regeneration such as the end of this war
must state. There was reason for hanging
on to a patch of ground as a matter of life
or death in the days when there were no new
countries established to offer asylums to the
vanquished, or when transport was not; but
there is no such excuse to-day.
Australia is spending men and money, and
wants to be paid in men—and write the
money off. This war has been caused by
land hunger, while Australia had room for 50
millions if but they would throw down the
shadow for the substance of comfort offered
them in this newest country. From the
beginning of wars it has been so; wars
born of land hunger, the apparently stronger
expelling the seemingly weak, and they
fleeing to newer lands to prove that the
conservative love for grandfather’s worn-
out patch of ground is a foolish thing, and
that love of country verges on the ridicu-
lous when the country loved was over-
swarmed with life as a kitchen midden.
The Australian trade lost to German manu-
facturers for ever is not to be passed on to
Japan or the United States or any other
country. That would be but a change of tax-
gatherers. Australia wants that trade for
itself, and the condition precedent to that
is skilled artisans from the countries which
supplied us before the war.
The conservatism of manufacture is as
stupid as any other. Scotland was early in
the field as a jute spinner, and jute is still
dragged from India to Scotland —to be
again reshipped to India and the East.
Dundee makes marmalade and never grew
an orange; and Australia grows wool and
bought a lot of woollen goods from Ger-
many. Yet Australia hasn’t a climate fatal
to woollen manufacture —though it used to
be stated that Australia couldn’t produce
water for brewing. Australia is simply
hard up for men—for the last word in arti-
sans and craftsmen.
The big zinc smelters of Belgium once had
French and Belgian zinc mines to draw
from ; then in the attempt to keep alive an
industry which, in the natural way, h„d
died for. want of local raw material, it
opened zinc mines in Italy and Spain and
freighted calamine and blende to Belgium.
Certainly Italy and Spain had no coal mines,
and the ore had to go somewhere to the fuel;
but Australia has coal a-plenty, and yet
Belgium and Germany got all the zinc con-
centrates which the admirable Broken Hill
Proprietary Co. could not smelt for the
small Australian demand. Germany’s
efficiency as a zinc smelter at last secured
all the trade from British works, and the
British political economists appeared to be
quite consoled by the fact that Cardiff coal
is the best fuel for zinc smelting, and that
Germany bought Welsh fuel for the treat-
ment of Australian ore. Yet all Australia
Wanted to keep an increasingly greater pro-
portion of its own zinc trade was more
people.
In Europe so much have they developed
their natural resources that they are de-
veloped out of existence. Germany and
Belgium drag even cement and concrete for
great distances out of France; and the
newer countries are keeping alive the super-
stition of nations being everlasting in a
world of change by sending meat and wheat
to overcrowded countries that cannot feed
their own people, in order to feed men to
smelt zinc from the world’s end. The actual
loss of effort in transport alone is colossal;
and its one result is the maintenance of
people in overcrowded towns of indifferent
climate, while the finest country on earth
asks vainly for men, and yet more men.
It is not as if transport were perilous, or
Australia unknown. It is too well known
to the undesirables; on one day in Mel-
bourne recently two pauper imbeciles from
England were presented for vagrancy—one,
“of good family,” said the humorless record,
had been shipped out of England by his
brother and abandoned. These were the
first dividends we had for our men and
treasure; while the people we do want will
cling to some ruined hamlet in France or
Belgium, and, after the war, will labori-
ously rebuild the place of their agony and
starvation. They managed things better in
the Middle Ages in Burgundy, principally
because they had not learned sanitary science.
To the village decimated by disease or
wasted by wars they “decanted” the sur-
plus population of an overcrowded town,
and so equalised matters. But even Bur-
gundy has stopped that not-all-foolish
interference with the rights of the subject;
and the scrap-heap is picked over and re-
cast at tremendous effort and with no
great result.
The people of the Balkan States, for
instance, are always more or less at war;
often destroying, and between times recon-
structing the unnecessary. We do not
want the Turk, and many Balkan and
Eastern Mediterranean peoples would be
similarly barred. Yet the best of them
Australia would welcome, and the worst of
Australia is better than the best their
own countries can offer. Albania is so
mountainous as to be good only for the
wildest of scenery merchants, and as the
people are mostly robbers —theft with
violence being regarded with them as re-
spectable as falsifying Customs entries is
with us —the country is especially fitted
for tourist traffic. They are foes to all the
arts of peace, and are best left alone by
Australian immigration agents. The Greeks
are just the people for the immense fisheries
Australian coasts must develop—the 80,000
square miles of the Barrier Reef, leaving
out its edible fish, can produce all the fer-
tilisers Australia foolishly drains itself of by
export. Greece could give Australia 100,000
people and never miss them. The Monte-
negrins, like most mountaineers, would be
of little value in Australia, especially as
they are still self-separated by the clan idea
and as ignorant as if they were still young.
And being very ignorant they are the most
intensely patriotic people on earth; not
with the patriotism that tries to make its
fatherland better, but believing with the
fatuousness of the criminal that anything it
does is well done because it does it. The
Roumanians—a people of Roman and Dacian
origin, its stream muddied with a strong
infusion of Tartar blood—are no good for
Australia either. Servia is a different pro-
position. Its 450 years of Turkish domina-
tion has not destroyed all its Greek blood
and feeling : its fertile country is not great
in area, being broken up by immense forests
and mountain systems and barren heaths.
Its people are some of those we want.
Yet for the millions that Australia must
have we must, consistently with the White
Australia religion and the policy of taking
only the best —which is the logical conclu-
sion of our remoteness —look to Western
Europe. In Italy there are hundreds of
scrap-heaps in the Appenines inhabited by
splendid people; fine, simple, frugal, honest
men and beautiful women, and these we
want as much for their beauty and refine-
ment as for their qualities of kindliness,
courtesy and self-abnegation. More than
any other people in the world they have
inherited the miracle of self-restraint; bet-
ter than any other people have they learned
to do without things gracefully. Villages
are there which were old 1000 years ago,
yet never do their populations grow
because the little tongues of land will feed
but so many. A man will keep a wife and
six children and the old grandmother to
mind the newest baby and stir the family
pot, and he will do it on Bs. a week and
keep his dignity. He has even a cup of red
vinegar— alias wine—and he and his wife
turn out of their bed of corncob tassels for
the honor of the guest. In one village—
Agli—-they built a church ten times that it
might be destroyed by lightning, and they
prayed and rebuilt for 200 years until a
stranger gave them a lightning conductor
for their tower. Their village is an eyrie—
rock-perched against the robber bands which
are no more —and it is an hour’s work to
descend to the “fields,” and two hours’ work
to climb home again. Yet when their mad
villages fall down in a ’quake, these hard-
working, frugal, splendid, fat-headed
wasters of effort build the lunatic structure
up again. They never waste" a penny, but
nobody shows them new methods, and so
they don’t make many pennies to waste.
They plough still with a section of forked
tree, and the motive power is a cow, or a
cow and a woman. I asked a ploughman in
Garfagnana, whose wife was leading the
drauglit cows up and down the field, why
he did not use reins, and he replied that
if he did his wife would have nothing to do.
You meet little boys and girls carrying
heavy burdens where they should be singing
in the sunlight; you see beauty taking oh
its wooden boots when it comes to stony
ways, because boots wear out and feet do
not. They capture every fallen leaf for cow
bedding; beauty and youth mix manure
with blue hands in the quite unnecessary
snow; and in the spring they pack the
manure in great baskets, and youth and
beauty carry it up unnecessary mountains
to the little tongues of impoverished soil.
And England in its refusal to adopt mod-
ern methods in manufactory is as foolishly
conservative—no more and no less.
At Santa Fiora, in Eastern Tuscany, the
death-rate is 34 per 1000, because the nurs-
ing mothers are so poorly nourished that
their milk dries early, and the toothless
child is fed on chestnuts and a little
polenta on Sundays. The chestnuts and
the summer finish off the seven-months-old
babies as a Broken Hill duststorm kills
caterpillars. But the survivors! What
world-beaters when well fed! One of the
most beautiful women in the world was on
my pay-roll at Monte Buono. She had the
figure that comes to the bearers of head
burdens, and she walked seven miles a day
to and from work—l 4 miles in all—for the
privilege of shovelling ore for 10 hours a
day at the drying floors. And if their
village falls down they build it up again
as if it were still in the tenth century when
the village was the end of the world, and
the world was flat. And at every threshing
floor the gleaners—starvation seeking alms
of poverty — many Ruths, but Boaz always
married to a watchful wife.
Earthquakes in Italy and Sicily were
repeated at intervals for 2000 years, and
yet no movement at abandonment of ruined
places, because the destruction was not
absolute, and Messina was not so fortunate
as Pompeii; Belgium, torn and riven by
the invader, rebuilding for the next invader;
England with many a sore-eyed village per-
sisted in century after century, though it
can give but poverty and sorrow to its
people; Spain growing sour wine on stones,
and keeping the Spanish virtues away from
the land of wine and oil.
Of them all, Australian character wants
most the Belgian, the Italian and the
French—-and the last are the greatest people
of the Old World; frugal, industrious,
courteous, artistic, happy-minded, cheerful
souls. We want a few millions of them
so badly that I could wish for the resurrec-
tion of that old Duke of Burgundy who
used the language of the vineyard and
forcibly “decanted” the over-full to the
empty. Europe _ must be rebuilt, of
course, but to do it on the old scale —while
rich, empty, splendid countries like Aus-
tralia wait development—would be waste-
ful and ridiculous. Pisa’s leaning tower is
interesting, but one is enough; and the
labors of Hercules contained in the making
of Venice, if repeated to-day, would entitle
the maker to a nice new asylum in rein-
forced concrete.
_ Again, let it be stated —Australia has
given men and money; let Australia begin
planning now how to be paid in men.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Australia and Fiji.
An unconfirmed rumor has been floating
around lately that the Commonwealth
would presently take over control of Fiji;
whereupon several right-thinking news-
papers have felt called upon to make the
customary ill-chosen remarks, and “gentle-
men thoroughly conversant with the posi-
tion” have aired themselves in print on
the subject. Boiled down, their joint and
several yarn consists of a whimpering
recital of the alleged chaos that would fol-
low such a transaction —of the ruin, desola-
tion and embarrassment that would take the
place of the coolie and the nigger through
the extension of the White Australia
doctrine to the island group. All of which
is the dressing-up of a shabby old scare-
crow —an attempt to throw back-handed
ridicule on a policy these unpatriotic con-
troversialists are afraid to attack openly,
but are characteristically ready to misre-
present. No Australian is ass enough to
propose a White World. To suggest that the
principles which govern the national atti-
tude on racial questions should be extended
to other countries where their application
would represent a complete reversal of the
doctrine accepted by the Australian people
for themselves is merely to call tenders for
ridicule. Yet this is what these anony-
mous wiseacres are unwittingly doing. Hav-
ing invited the community to gaze on a
laboriously manufactured travesty of Aus-
tralian sentiment, they ask their little world
to see with what derision the thing should
be jumped upon. The White Australia
principle is only converted into a White
Fiji principle by enemies. Australia has
quite enough on hand to deal with in re-
spect to her own country; and these queer
Tories who are now trying to argue that
she is bound to a policy of extending her
social theories to distant, over-sea lands are
just as fatuously incapable of understanding
obvious facts as they were 25 years
ago. Instead of, as they suppose, making
the White Australia idea appear ludicrous,
they are just proclaiming their own
banality.


The Lacking Sense of Proportion.
“0.K.” calls for a stiffer back
British casualties in the war up to April 11 num-
bered 139,347. All the casualties in the war amount
to millions, dead or disabled for life shockingly
disabled in tens of thousands of cases. And the num-
ber of persons otherwise injured within the past nine
months runs into tens of hundreds of thousands.
Calamities like the loss of the Titanic will be mere
flyspecks on the foolscap page of 20th century his-
tory. The same remark applies to earthquakes lesu
ing in vast destruction of mortals and landed pro-
perty. Probably the Moving Finger will rub them
off the page quite unconsciously. Nowadays nothing
really matters to existing civilisation, or posterity in
general, save the question whether Germany is, or
isn’t, to perpetuate the rule of truculent armed
force. The nations are all concerned about that one
great question. Yet in this very time of appalling
storm and stress there are persons pleading, for
selfish personal reasons, for benevolent treatment of
the comparatively few Germans who hate England in
English-speaking countries. From a logical national
point of view the comfort, convenience, wife, children
and future prospects of ten thousand individual
Schmidts and Kaufmanns matter not a bean in com-
parison with the stake at issue. Yet a few blind
“humanitarians” and hypocritical egotists, swayed
by commercial or social interests, cannot be per-
suaded that at the present time all civilian Germans
outside of Germany, “naturalised” or otherwise, are
out of place. In an enemy country they are “unde-
sirable aliens,” in the fullest meaning of the term.
Interned, they are a needless trouble; at liberty,
they may be a danger. What should their personal
desires re domicile matter to the millions who are
suffering from the accursed military Ilogmanism
whereof these few citizens are foreign representatives ?
The British passengers on the Falaba desired to re-
main aboard their floating domicile, but the German
submarine forced them to quit the vessel, or die —-or
both. Belgians, Flench and Russians have suffered
slaughter and horrible outrages, as well as loss of
property, -at the hands of men not more German
than Schmidt and Kaufmann. English soldier
prisoners are being victimised, and even done to
death through privations, by the enemy that refuses
an exchange of military captives lest the whole
truth about the good and bad treatment of them in
their respective places of captivity should become
known to their respective countrymen ere the war
ends. But in face of these facts a mischievous min-
ority of the world-wide British public still attaches
importance to the comfort, convenience and blessed
vested interests of a few thousand undesirable aliens,
potential spies, insidious enemies to the Cause —the
danse that cost the British forces 139,347 casualties
up to April 11, when the war, according to Kit-
chener, had not yet begrun. What Britain and the
English-speaking world wants of everybody is a
proper sense of proportion. Without it there can be
no proper sense of jus'.ice. Let the Germans who are
shirking their inherited duties to militarism remain
under British rule, if they are content to feel con-
temptibly out of place, or let them patriotically re-
turn to the dear old German Government which
wants all the food for cannon it can put on the war
market. But do not let them be matters for mawkish
sympathy or special consideration.
A New Parson and an Old Gospel.
Whenever a big Australian, question—
Defence or the working power of the white
man up North or the desirability of State
education—is . settled and put aside to
mature, someone, usually a parson, is bound
to drift along and beg the whole proposi-
tion. A new arrival in Sydney named
Kencii is the latest to run true to clerical
type. Taking the war as his text he said
lass week: —
Will it be possible to say to the Japanese and the
Indians, your gallant sons were good enough to die
to Secure our safety, but you are not good enough to
live within our borders. .. .The days when we could
keep a great continent practically empty are drawing
to a close.
What the Black Australian persists in
blandly overlooking when he advocates what
amounts to miscegenation is that the
virtues of ~ the colored man have nothing to
do with the case for bis exclusion. There is
no doubt as to the gallantry of the Indians,
and the Jans have shown themselves
to bre fine soldiers. But what sane indivi-
dual would advance proved courage as a
reason why a man must make a desirable
mate? Anyone who has the time and money
to fake a trip up the White Nile can find
between Khartoum and Gondo Koro hun-
dreds of unclothed tribesmen - brave as any
wearer of the Y.C. They have many splen-
did qualities, but they are not. and never
could be made, the sort - of folks whom
we would care ' to' have' at our tea-
parties and bowling matches. The
colored men who are fighting on our side in
Flanders and elsewhere , are incomparably
more civilised than most of the inhabitants
of Africa, but their ways will not blend with
ours either. They live, think, work and die
differently, and they always must. And that
is to set aside the tremendous race-question
which no amount of word-chopping can sur-
mount. The parson who opposes the White
Australia policy on Biblical grounds—this
same Kench, by the way, when in West-
ralia supported it on Biblical grounds—
and who winds up by appealing, in the name
of the Gospel, for just a little unoccupied
territory for the use of the heathen, is
oblivious apparently to the fact that a black
North is going to mean a polluted South.
The Australian who fights now for racial
purity is playing the altruist in a way that
will astonish the future generation which
observes bitterly how little altruism there
was about the Australian who passed on so
many debts, and so few genuinely reproduc-
tive works to pay interest with. He is
struggling for a cause that cannot benefit
him personally, though it must save his
remote descendants from tragic experiences
if it prevails. And, above all, it is one
that he will no more drop than the Britisher
will abandon the present campaign to render
Germany harmless. Why can’t the clergy
realise this and switch on to_ some new
evangel? Or else go back to their oldest one
of all, now much neglected?
—
The Men Who Went to War and Robbed
a Chinaman.
Unless the facts in relation to the four sol-
diers convicted by court martial at Rabaul
for robbery are materially different from the
statements presented in the Federal Parlia-
ment last week by Jensen, who assists
Peabce to be Minister for Defence, it looks
as if this matter has not been thrown open
for inspection any too soon. Anstey (Vic.),
who brought the matter forward, appears to
have been mainly moved by the consideration,
not that these men were innocent, but that
a number of other guilty persons had escaped
punishment. Therefore, he claimed in effect,
the four were the victims of an evil system,
since unless Justice be even-handed, the
gravest injury follows. No doubt; but in that
case the wrong is done, not to those who
are punished, but to the community which
suffers through the escape of those who are
not punished. Nevertheless, Anstey did
good service, not by throwing his allegations
and rumors about, but by directing attention
to the court-martial sentences. To the chari-
table judgment these must appear in urgent
need of revision. Sentences of three years’
and five years’ penal servitude are reserved
by the civil Courts for two classes of offend-
ers —those who have been guilty of premedi-
tated crime on the major scale, and those
who come forward for sentence on serious
charges as “repeaters.” At Babaul the court
martial seems to have taken the harshest
possible view of the offences. Pretty clearly,
this was recognised by the Crown, for the
penal servitude (most unusual nowadays) was
commuted by Governor’s warrant to hard
labor, and the men were informed that at
the expiry of six months the terms of impris-
onment would be considered with a view to
remission of part or all of the unexpired por-
tion. The circumstances were so exceptional
that the sentences should there and then
have been finally reviewed, and the men
allowed to definitely understand their fate.
Apparently they are guilty. They disgraced
their uniform and their country. It is
right that they should be adequately
punished. But when a man who at Mel-
bourne would get perhaps 12 months from a
Judge with a wig for robbing a Chinaman,
is at Rabaul given five years by Judges with
feathers in their hats, there is obvious room
for re-consideration. So far as the accusa-
tions of wholesale looting against officers
are concerned —about an orgy of “loot,
rapine and plunder” and “shiploads of spoil
sent away”—it is high time they were dis-
posed of one way or the other. If they, are
lies they are so circumstantial that, they
must do us great harm so long as they are
permitted to float around unchallenged.
Already they have been . unchallenged much
too long.

Sosh Gets Another Kick from its Own
Side.
The way not to conduct a Socialistic busi-
ness is-exemplified Jay the Sydney City Fish
Markets. The municipal concern; has. oppo-
sition in the: form of a Private Enterprise
market. With the. prestige and material
advantages attaching to a city-owned show
it might fairly be concluded that the P.E.
establishment would soon be wiped right out.
In point of fact, the reverse 'is happening.
George Black, the Chief Sec. in. the
Holman Government, visited the municipal
markets the other morning and found, four
patrons there. Passing on to the rival estab-
lishment he was hardly able to get inside
owing to the press of customers. ■ AH .the
fishermen and fish-shop proprietors can’t be
fanatical anti-Socialists determined to _ dis-
credit Sosh .at any expense or inconvenience
to themselves. There must be some other
explanation of the calamitous fact that the
municipal fish markets are costing the com-
munity £5O per week, while the other con-
cern is paying its shareholders, a dividend.
Meanwhile the committee appointed by the
City Council to inquire into the working of
the white elephant or grey shark or what-
ever it is has just found as follows: —
That there has been no neglect on the part of the
staff, either in the management or in the conduct
of the business of the markets.
That, by way of experiment, a thoroughly experi-
enced and competent officer should be appointed for
a term not exceeding six months to' systematically
canvas for business in the various fishing centres.
That the staff should be reduced from four to two,
and if extra assistance is required at any time this
should, be supplied. by the Town Clerk from the
Council’s staff.
That all private fish markets in the metropolitan
area be closed, and the exclusive right of conducting
markets for the sale of fish in such area be vested in
the City Council.
That this report be brought under the notice of the
Honorable the Premier, and that he be urged to
immediately introduce legislation to give effect to the
recommendations contained herein.
Briefly •the:-management has been admirable
- -t hough it-has neglected to get out after
business. -The ~ staff is blameless —but
requires to be reduced by one-half. And
the opposition market, since it has insisted
on flourishing in. the open, should be sti-
lettoed in,a. back lane. If the aldermen and
others who were responsible for this report
had set out expressly to discredit the
Socialistic principle they could hardly have
done better. Here is a business which
frankly admits that it is losing money; that,
though skilfully and honestly run, neither
the fishermen, the middlemen nor the public
will patronise it; and that it cannot hope to
live unless its only rival is slain. The
public—Sosh and otherwise —will certainly
respond that, if these things are true, it had
much better die. But, pending the funeral,
the Council had better appoint another
Committee to ascertain if the first com-
mittee wasn’t a fatuous ass.

Simplicity Makes Strange Bedfellows.
With the P.L.L. of N. S. Wales dexterously
side-stepping the totalisator, with the know-
ledge that the Bill to legalise the machine
in Victoria is already in troubled waters,
and with the benediction of the Church in
his ears, Ikey has decided that even if he
cannot “follow the King” he can at least
continue to venture a good cigar now and
again. Anything more fatuous than the
attitude of the parson brotherhood on this
matter is impossible to conceive. They
seem to be impressed with the notion that
if Ikey is made to work for his living there
will be an enormous increase in gambling,
“especially among women and children,” and
they view with horror the idea that if the
totalisator is legalised wagering will be
sanctioned and patronised by the State.
These black-coated obscurantists may know
a whole lot about Jonah and the whale, and
the weapon Samson demolished his enemies
with, but what they don’t know about the
racecourse and betting would fill a caravan
of pantechnicons. The yam about women and
kids, who can bet now as much as they like
with Ikey, being in peril if the tote comes
along, is obvious nonsense ; and as for “sanc-
tioning” gambling, well, isn’t it sanctioned
now? Doesn’t the State get revenue from
Ikey, and don’t the racing clubs, too? And
where is the moral turpitude in giving sanc-
tion to a machine-made wager instead of to
one concluded with a bag and a nasal voice?
The answer is, of course, that the sky pilots
don’t know what they are talking about —are
so immersed in their own Book that they
imagine there can be nothing wrong with
the Rook of McNoses. A further quaint
objection raised by the parsons is that
“experience in Maoriland and S. Aus. proves
that once the machine is introduced it is
almost impossible to get rid of it.” To every-
one except bookmakers and curates this'will
look like evidence in favor of the clean,
straight machine. It is an argument which, if
used in relation to the Christian religion by
some cultivated heathen in justification of
treating missionaries as undesirable immi-
grants, would be denounced as ignorant
sophistry by every parsonical association in
the * universe. Employed as an excuse for
the clerical toleration of Ikey, it is entitled
to even greater derision.

On the Futile Grinding of the Mills.
Melbourne University disposed of its
annual stock of degrees the other day with
dismal results. The list: 58 persons made
Bachelors of ■ Arts (24 were women), 42
Bachelors of Medicine and 16 Bachelors of
Law; three took a degree in Civil Engineer-
ing, one in Agricultural Science, one in
Music —but mot even one in Mining. This
deplorable record of misdirected individual
effort and national folly is repeated year
after year. In a country positively shriek-
ing for the application of technical and
scientific knowledge the “centres of learn-
ing,” like so many sausage-machines, are
turning out every year larger and larger
batches of lawyers and doctors —of the men
who are really wanted not so many as could
shelter beneath a 6 x 4 tent. | : ln one of the
world’s great mineral territories the Uni-
versity produces actually onei - mining en-
gineer in a year, and only three electrical
engineers! Is it any wonder that Aus-
tralia has to send abroad for men to run
departments in mefallurgical works at Port
Pirie, Queenstown and Port Waratah? And
is it any wonder that mines everywhere fail
for want of proper direction? It is the
same with agriculture. That Melbourne
can produce only an occasional scientist
in that department is of itself sufficient
evidence to damn the system. Even, if
Australia doesn’t care, about eating sausage,
there are still a thing or two in
which she can take a lesson from the Ger-
man, and one of them is in making her
system, of education reasonably approximate
to the needs of the nation.

The row over the proposal to beautify St.
Mary’s Cathedral, Sydney, by allowing the
church authorities to extend it as far as
Phillip Park reached a sort of deadlock last
week when the City Solicitor intimated that
the thing cannot legally be done. That is to
say, it cannot be done while the law remains
as it is. On the other hand, it could be if
Parliament decided to alter the law. The
real issue has been clouded by the intrusion
of the Walls of Derry. The Orange pup
can see no farther in this matter than the
fact that the acquisition by the Church of
the desired fragment of land will gratify
the priests. The pup doesn’t explain to
himself that the triumph of the clerics must
be short-lived, inasmuch as many of them
will soon be dead and none of them are
immortal; while the possession of an ill-
finished public building must be a reproach
to the city for as long as they both exist.
If England offered Sydney (say) the Elgin
marbles on condition that the city built a
gallery in Phillip Park to shelter them, the
community—Rome loathers and all —would
certainly jump at the cljance. This sug-
gests the truth that practically the whole
objection to the extension of the Cathedral
is a matter of religious prejudice—a paltry
feeling that success will mean a score for a
sect which ought only to have bricks thrown
at it. If Parliament approaches the trouble
in this spirit it will reduce itself to the rank
of a backblocks shire council.__Shorn of the
sectarian element theTfi'SiTion is this. The
cathedral occupies one of the best positions
in the city, and it 4s t and has -be’eii for' yehrs,
an eyesore. If tne proposed extension is
carried out and the business of finishing the
spire is pushed on with immediately—works
which the Council can and should insist
upon—Sydney will possess, within a very
short time, a building as imposing and almost
as beautiful as some of those which are
being destroyed in Europe by the German
guns. The solution of the difficulty is for
the Church to be -allowed to take over the
land at a fair valuation and give security
in return to have the building finished within
a stipulated time, as the London Oratory
did 20 years ago under pressure from the
L.C.C. Sydney would sacrifice in that event
what it does not greatly need in exchange
for one of the finest city ornaments in the
Southern Hemisphere.
One of the big men of the days before Fede-
ration died in Adelaide last week. Tom
Playford was big every way; in one S.A.
Ministry he sat in— John Colton’s —every
member was a six-footer. Bluff and straight-
forward, he was yet a born politician. The
S.A. Assembly knew him almost continu-
ously for 26 years, from 1868 onwards. He
was in nine of the short-lived Ministries
of those days, and twice was Premier. He
made history by standing up to Henry
Parkes, in a very early preliminary conven-
tion to discuss Federation, when that hairy
autocrat thought the rights of the “small”
States could be disregarded. In 1894 he
followed Bray as S.A. Agent-General in
London, but was back in time to become a
Senator in the first Federal Parliament.
Under Deakin, he was vice-president of
the Executive Council, and, later, Minister
for Defence. At the end of 1906 he ran
up against Party politics. A Kingstonian
democrat of the old school —he had a big
hand in giving S. Aus. Protection, land tax
and income tax —he could not see his way
to rank himself with either Labor or the
Fusion “Liberals.” So he stood on his repu-
tation, and an ungrateful public bumped
him out of politics. Still he had done his
work and left such a mark as no man need
be ashamed of.

The Minister’s explanation of why the per-
inanent instructional staff and the Gunners
are prohibited from going on active
service is quite unconvincing. To say
that the training of the forces in
Australia must proceed is true; but
surely, if . the militia officer is good
enough to lead in war, he is competent to
hold together the military machine in Aus*
tralia till the war is over. That seems to
dispose entirely of one official objection. The
other is that a ship of the German North
Sea fleet might break away. But does the
Minister. propose to use his army to beat
off a naval attack? Some permanent offi-
cers and men badly want to have the chance
to show what they are made of in a crisis
which they have been specially trained to
meet. Others have not spoken. The ques-
tion seriously agitating those who do want
to go is: “Are we being held back to save
someone’s face?”

What is called a Premiers’ Conference is
to he held, and a dozen politicians are on
their way to Sydney to talk of things of
common interest. Every such meeting is
evidence that a political fraud has been
practised on the people of Australia. Fifteen
years ago a body was established to. do
those things which called for common action ;
and when State Premiers have to meet it
simply means, even if they come to an
agreement, that .the joint action is taken
at great expense by six bodies instead of the
one that was created for the purpose. In*
variably one or more of the bodies
will not act; and then the whole
irregular business is a farce. There
is only one way out of the bog. To
make the 50 necessary amendments of the
Federal Constitution would be such a cum-
bersome proceeding that it will be far easier
and simpler and safer to put the entire com-
plicated instrument of Australian Govern-
ment in the melting pot and make a com-
pletely new start with unification. Unifica-
tion is good enough for Great Britain ;; and,
in spite of the handicaps it has put on itself,,
Great Britain, under that form of govern-
ment, is proving itself not such an impotent
old bull-ants’ nest, after all.
The Ma country is having many shocks
these days. One of the latest came through
Octavius Charles. Beale, past-president
of the Australian- Manufacturers’ Associa-
tion, getting on his legs before the Royal
Society of London and telling it to follow
Australia’s lead. Said the piano man: —
My proposal is that there should be formed in
Great Britain, in like manner to Australia, manufac-
turers’ associations solely to represent producing in-
terests, apart from those of distributors of foreign-
made goods. As things stand in Great Britain, in-
dustrial interests are affected, and possibly, on occa-
sion, overborne, by the purely commercial. Importers
and manufacturers are inextricably mixed in the
Chambers of Commerce.
Apparently some of the audience was gasp-
ing at the impertinent Beale’s idea that
England could learn anything in the matter
of trade from Australia; so he proceeded
to rub it in this way:—
From his very circumstances the manufacturer is
profoundly and energetically nationalistic. . . .He does
not admit the glory or the wisdom from the national
viewpoint of buying in the cheapest and selling in
the dearest market. A few years ago an English expert
on wheat-growing surprised his hearers in this very
rocm 1 y telling them that tilling and harvesting in
Australia were conducted at a lower cost than in any
part of the world. Yet the remuneration of labor is
happily high... .Australians invented the sturnp-
jumpirg plough and the stripper and the harvester,
and so helped to teach the world. .. .They didn't
even consult the allegedly profound thinkers of the
world as they might.
Then he went into the story of the American
attempt —foiled —to seize the agricultural-
implement market of Australia, and told his
highly incredulous but deeply interested
audience that Australian implements now
have a large sale in Argentina and in North,
South and Central Africa, to the benefit in
particular of the Empire and in general of
the world.

(An Illuminated Address to Moloch.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
God, in this hour when swings a molten
world
And Time is forged anew,
When through the quivering air Thy Word
is hurled,
Our thoughts turn back to You!
Beyond the yelling guns, beyond the smoke
That drifts in reeking palls,
Where wretches shriek and stricken heroes
choke,
Thy awful shadow falls.
Here is no martyr Chkist to salve and heal,
No piteous word of love;
Only red wrath and those dread whips of
steel
That lash men from above.
We stand aghast who have to stand and
wait, •t,.:-;.
While far and near the drum
Proclaims with pride at ev’ry brazen gate
Thy chosen victims come. ‘
To what great end Thou work’st we dare not
think ;
But we would brave the fire
And venture, shouting, to the molten brink
To find Thy dread desire.
Long have we served in peace. But now in
war,
Our chosen faith to prove,
With naked steel towards murder red and
raw
In serried ranks we move.
N.S.W.

Ip the spring campaign has not opened with
any dramatic gain or loss of ground, the
blood-letting has been considerable. After
Nelive Chapelle came St. Eloi; then fol-
lowed the gory affair at “Hill 60.” The
cables had not succeeded in making clear
what took place in connection with this
eminence when messages began to arrive
about a furious scrap along the Yser canal
in the vicinity of Ypres. Apparently the
Germans, being annoyed by the punishment
they received earlier in the month, decided
on hitting back. Great volumes of Hun
were thrown against the points of junction
between the British, French and Belgian
troops, due west of the German headquarters
at Boulers. The battle appears to have
been of the most sanguinary nature, its lead-
ing features being the effective use of poison-
ous gas by the enemy, a temporary making-
good on his part, and the ultimate success
of the Allies in heaving the invader back on
his haunches. The Canadian infantry is
reported to have borne itself with signal
gallantry in this argument. When last
heard of the German was burying big heaps
of slain, and viciously glaring at the road
to Calais. While things were looking fairly
good for Hans glad tidings went from the
front to Berlin, and the biergartens rejoiced.
What happened next day, when the popula-
tion woke up with a sore head to read of
repulse instead of victory, has not yet been
divulged. Flung off though the enemy has
again been, this latest engagement empha-
sises several points which it won’t pay us
to forget. One is that the German has still
a heavy foot on Belgium; another that he
is still ready to take the offensive and fight
with courage, endurance and skill; a third,
that no trustworthy evidence exists about his
alleged shortage of food or guns or anything
else, including iron crosses. All of which
is a reminder that the best we have to offer
Civilisation and the Empire in this struggle
is the least we can afford to give. In the
East the thaws of spring are apparently
imposing a period of relative inactivity,
though the more recent reports from Russia
indicate that Ivan is moving down the Car-
pathians into Hungary. Generally, however,
the performances in that theatre are pro-
ceeding beyond the range of the limelight.
In‘ the Dardanelles operations are being
resumed on a scale so much larger that suc-
cess looks now more probable than when the
Allies confined themselves to the water.
Troops have been landed on the Gulf of
Saros—there is reason why Australia should
be very interested in that movement —and
preparations made to engage the Turk in the
rear with a bayonet, while the warships
throw sudden death in his face. Meantime
Italy looks on with a ruminating eye, and
the Bulgars and Roumanians are still having
a smoke.
The High Court of Australia, having
thought over the matter for several days,
last week told the pneumonia-proof Chidley
that it couldn’t hear his appeal against con-
viction on a criminal charge. This was
because the Court had previously told one
Eatheb it wouldn’t hear his appeal. The
position is farcical. The Eatiier appeal
was thrown out by a majority of Judges,
among whom were two who confess they
didn’t know what they were doing. The
Chidley appeal is denied a hearing because
a majority of the Court is of opinion that
the rule laid down about Eatheii was final
—and this majority is a mixture of Judges
who either believe the Eatiier judgment to
be hopelessly erroneous in law or know that
the rule was formulated in the dark. And
the Chief Justice holds —very strongly, it
seems—that the majority is wrong and
that the judgment in re Eather, to which
he subscribed, does not govern criminal
appeals in the way the Court now says it
does. This is really dreadful. It is also
highly dangerous. The Crown should hurry
up a search party at once, and by case
stated find out where their Honors are.
They seem hopelessly bushed and bawling
for help.
Charles W. Harris, killed at Suva, Fiji,
last week, was an Australian with a promis-
ing career cut short at 36. Fie started to
draw pay from the Works Department at
Newcastle when 14 years of age; after eight
years he shifted to Sydney Harbor Trust for
11 years, and had been engineer-in-chief for
the Fiji Government for three years when
the accident happened. He was the eldest
of a family of 13 good Australians.

T. W. Robertson’s “Caste” wears well.
Its presentation by the Little Theatre Raget
company, on Saturday evening, for the final
six nights of the season, was so skilful that
hardly any moth-holes appeared in the dia-
logue, though undoubtedly the silverfish
had been at work on the sentiment. In
these days when the British aristocracy is
bred chiefly from the ballet, the Hon.
George D’Alroy’s marriage with a pretty
dancing-girl does not call for any dramatic
tears. The old play, however, is remark-
able nowadays for its long-suffering infant.
During most of the third act the baby
occupies the centre of the stage. It is the
best behaved baby on record. It is solilo-
quised to, called names, fondled, nursed—
even by its father —and never once does it
howl or do any of those unexpected but per-
fectly natural things which babies on exhi-
bition invariably do. Next to this brilliant
performance are those of Gerald Souper as
Captain Hawtree, and Kenneth’s fine study
of Sam Gerredge. These two actors had
character parts from which they extract the
utmost dramatic effect. Eccles is in the
capable hands of Arthur Cornell, though an
element of clowning was apparent on the
opening performance. But the character
remains this actor’s most finished effort,
with the exception of his part in “Fanny’s
First Play.” Victor Fitzherbert is making
rapid progress; his presentation of the hus-
band is simple and sincere. Violet Paget is
sweetly mournful, but this part gives
her little scope for acting. Lillian
Lloyd and Tempe Pigott complete a cast
that for all-round suitability shows this com-
pany at its best.
** * *

P.S. for departing troops. —The greedy
gentleman in checks struck trouble for doing this.
Jim Corbett, retired licensed vendor of
stamps, now of Sydney Tivoli, continues to
look on the humorous side of being walloped
by Fitzsimmons, and the cheerful way he
goes about it has converted The Bulletin’s
Religious Editor, an enemy hitherto of the
Stadium, into an ardent paper-weight. Gus
T. Raglus provides a particularly clever
ball-bouncing turn; and Smith and Nielson’s
mainly-musical item is another bright spot
on a very-good-all-round programme.
What you’ll see if you go in out of the
wet: —
Crystal Palace : “The Avenging Conscience, or
Thou Shalt Not Kill,” wherein the still small voice
breaks into a howl that occupies practically the
whole of the programme.
Lyric : “On the Stroke of 5,” so The Bulletin
refuses to say anything more about it unless paid
overtime.
Empress: A further consignment of “The Million
Dollar Mystery,” parts 5 and 6.

Our first imported topical war drama,
“The Man Who Stayed at Home,” is due at
Sydney Royal on Saturday night. The
likely-looking cast includes, by special
arrangement, Violet Paget, the decorative
leading lady of the Little Theatre.

Alfred Park, Sydney, Wirths’ will deflate
their elephants, dismantle the bears, powder
Lizzie’s hide and let the monkeys run down
prior to taking to the road.

The Imperial Salon de Luxe will open its
doors to rag-timers, two-steppers, high-
steppers, bunny-hiiggers, Gaby-gliders, etc.,
on Saturday evening. By special request
of the Amalgamated Curates’ Union the
opening hymn will be, “This is the Life.”
"SINBAD THE SAILOR .”
The Dardanelles situation, as the Sydney
Adelphi Panto, sees it.
“ Broadway Jones,” staged at Melbourne
Royal last Saturday, is a farcical comedy
which wants pruning of some of the patter-
allotted to Jones. Once in a way it is dull,
and more than in a way it is long
for a story where real human interest never
gets a look in. The young man that Niblo
talks for in this instance would seem a cross
between Harry Bronson (“Belle of New
Nicholas Gehde “ storming ” on the Sydney
Town Hall organ.
York”) and “The Fortune Hunter” if he
had any suggestion of a heart. Being
merely an impossible person who talks like
a machine and acts like an unprincipled
simpleton as a rule, he doesn’t suggest any
sort of character at all. The utter empti-
ness of Broadway Jones gives an air of
unreality to all the surrounding characters,
though they are conventional stage types
without exception. Farcicality is confined
to the all-pervading hero and the highly
accentuated widow presented by Mrs. Hollo-
way. The other characters seem as reason-
able as they could be, considering that they
have to accept Jones as a fact instead of a
fiction. Sydney Stirling is never foolish,
save when he falls in love with an un-
sophisticated flapper at first sight. E. P.
Bush seems an absurd boy merely because
his acting is rather crude and his inches too
many for his knickers. Cambourne, George
Bryant, Lester, Dudley, Marion Clarke and
Lacey are well placed in straight parts, and
Enid Bennett is a nice little girl with a weak
little voice, as usual. The comedy has some
telling flashes of humor and satire, and
Niblo’s mannerisms maintain their popu-
larity.
