The King’s (Melb.) is a temple of
silence at time of writing. Two nights of
“Confusion” reduced the management to
despair, apparently, and the season came to
a jagged end.
** * ❖
“Lady Godiva” is lasting a second week
at Melb. Princess’s, not because the dull
and gaudy melodrania deserves such
proud distinction, but owing to a vain ex-
pectation that the Earl of Coventry’s missus
will provide a pleasing study in the alto-
gether. _ As a matter of fact, her flowing
mane hides so much of her pink tights that
even her milk-white steed doesn’t trouble to
turn his head. The animal simply walks
slowly about, with a dejected air, as though
his lovely burden were a funeral. Next
Saturday the incentive to business will be
“From Convent to Throne,” and the prices
will range from sixpence to two “bob,” as
before.

“High Jinks” is a bubble that continues
to float on at Her Majesty’s, and the dance
of .Miss. Novotna and Victor Lauschmann
is invariably a graceful trimming to the
third act.
Hs * #
A considerable change of bill at Melb.
Tivoli has not shifted Mozart and his
long, lean auxiliary from their place in the
foreground. None of the newcomers or re-
turns are more effective than the boneless
Chinese Puzzle or the brawny Arco Bros.
Dooley and Sales, clever artists in their
way, are about the fourth- mixed double
since Christmas who have sung or con-
versed together on the same lines; West-
wood does some balancing and juggling
whilst wobbling on a unicycle ; Rose Hamel
sings tunefully to her own accompaniment
of artless charcoal sketches; Will Con-
nolly, a human marionette, needed editing
with a blue pencil last Saturday; J. Fields,
the American tramp, protests that each of
his loud efforts is going to be better than
its predecessor; and Ivanoff and Keith
describe their dismal carpet dances as “new
crazes.”
* * * *
Melbourne Savage Club promises a cap-
ital half-crown concert at the Town Hall
next Wednesday (sth) in aid of the Bel-
gian Fund. The Club rarely breaks out into
public entertainment, but never fails to do
it very well. A feature on this occasion will
be the Toy Symphony, conducted by Fritz
Hart, each toy having a leading musician
behind it. For instance, Alberto Zelman is
to play the cuckoo —and some other profe'ssor
may be expected to play the deuce. Horace
Stevens, with chorus, will sing an awful
version of the German “Hymn of Hate.”
The 2s. 6d. pasteboard admits to the Town
Hall. Holders can book their seats gratis
ac the Pianola box-office.

“BROADWAY JONES.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A waster weak was Broadway Jones,
Untroubled by regrets;
His dollars gone, he raised no loans,
But lived upon his debts,
And then, because he wanted stuff
For spending at the bar,
He mashed a widow, old enough
To be his grandmamar.
A worthless thing of flesh and bones
Was Yankee bounder Jackson Jones.
Ere Jones could wed his would-be bride
And further dissipate,
A providential uncle died
And left him much estate —
A factory with trade in hand,
A ripe commercial plum;
For uncle ran the famous brand
Of Jonesville Chewing Gum.
His wicked uncle being skyed,
Jones bolted from his promised bride.
He meant to sell the stock in trade
And fool away the cash ;
But Josie came to Jackson’s aid
And changed his purpose rash.
She praised the bizness in the past;
She told him how it grew,
How all mankind would crave at last
For Jones’s gum to chew.
’Twere cruel, said the pleading maid,
To kill a profitable trade.
The gum was grandad’s recipe,
His father’s pride and joy;
His uncle built the factoree,
And left it to the boy.
The spendthrift saw the girl was bright—
In fact, he thought her “some”—
And vowed that with his main and might
He’d push the Chewing Gum.
A thousand workers cheered when he
Took over grandad’s recipe.
O’er myriads of chewing guys
St. Peter weeps and groans,
And when a pallid chewer . dies
He scores it down to Jones.
From Noo Yark city, Boston, too,
And dark Chicago slum,
St. Peter gets the sinners who
Were killed by Jonesville Gum.
With wonder in his saintly eyes
He views the stream of chewing guys.

Louise Mack is coming to Australia in
June. The brilliant lady has decided to
tell all she knows about the pounding of
Antwerp and other scenes of excitement and
bloodshed which she recently encountered as
a penwoman in Belgium. The lectures,
which will be managed by the Taits, will be
delivered before an illustrative background
of lantern slides and motion pictures.
H: $ H* *
The Dandies, who have all summer been
entertaining outdoors at Adelaide Exhibi-
tion, have taken flight for Maoriland, which
they propose to tour thoroughly. They are
a capable lot of merrymakers, strongest in
singing and “character” work, weakest on
the humorous side. This leaves Adelaide
quiet, the Royal being again closed; also
the Tivoli, at which Dooley has been putting
in some fine acting, and Rosa Hamel some
quick caricature work, enlivened by a
moderate voice and a cynicism appalling
in one so young. At the King’s is another
sketcher, Tozart, more thorough than the
lady. Carrie Moore is in her last nights
at this house—Adelaide’s only theatre for
the moment. But the Tivoli is to reopen
at the end of the week with Cannot and his
Follies. West’s, Wondergraph, Empire,
Central and Pav. are all busy with war-
pictures and “drama.” All have a strong
family likeness, and the public rushes the
lot.
After the Federal House of Reps, had
contemplated the War Precautions Bill for
a time last week the measure was hauled
off for private inspection on the under-
standing that some pf the Government fol-
lowing could resume their hostile remarks
when the Bill was next produced. -There
are parts of this measure that will be much
improved by hostile remarks —especially
those, parts conferring on courts-martial extra
judicial rights over civilians. In this mat-
ter the Ministry seems to be playing the
part of an obedient poodle to the War
Office and to imagine that no more justifi-
cation of the measure is required than that
“it was put forward at the request of the
British Government.” That is a long, long
way from being sufficient. Parliament is
entitled to hear all that can be said in favor
of this proposal, and if the arguments are
inadequate then its duty is to pitch the
thing overboard. The conduct of the mili-
tary when they touch anything outside their
own particular business is so usually dis-
tinguished by ineptitude and uniptelligence
that the most overwhelming evidence should
be demanded in the Bill’s favor. Rabaul
and the Censor’s office are solemn warnings
against giving the soldier anything but a
gun to play with.
Thomas Scarfe, of Harris, Scarfe and Co.,
Adelaide, left an estate now sworn as just
under half a million. A dozen charities are
remembered in his will, but the most notable
feature of. it is a complete scheme of
legacies for every servant in his employ—
and he had a good many. All house, garden
afid stable hands of over 10 years’ service get
£100; and more than 20 old associates in
the warehouse get £3OO each.

“His Royal Rotundity."
Centenaries this year : Henry Parkes, May
27; explorer Edward John Eyre, August 15;
Richard Dry, one-time Speaker and Premier
of Tasmania, August 15; explorer John
McDouall Stuart, who first hoisted the
Union Jack in Central Australia, and after-
wards crossed the continent from the South-
ern to the Indian Oceans, September 7;
William Foster Stawell, Chief Justice of Vic-
toria from 1857 to 1886, June 27.
AT POVERTY POINT.
The yarn that Melba had been tempted by
an offer of 4000 dahlars a week to
sing in vaudeville came to the London Daily
Telegraph in a telegram from N’York,
though acceptance was referred to only as a
“possibility.” The Palace was mentioned as
the place that was willing to pay the 4000.
“I never will,” says Melba; but meantime
her sister prima donna, Calv 6, the finest
Carmen of the day, has gone into vaudeville
at Chicago at 3000 dollars a week.
“Mascotte”: “Upstage” (B. 15/4/T5) is
out of liis reckoning in his Pat Behan
reminiscence when he states that in 1886
Mrs. Armstrong sang under the name of
Melba for the first time. The diva did not
assume the name until her appear-
ance on the opera stage at Brussels in ’B7.
At her farewell concert in Melbourne early
in ’B6 she was billed as Mrs. Armstrong.
Lemmone was in the programme, and the
accompanists were Zerbini, Herz and
Guenett.
Misfortunes have been crowding in on
poor Bernhardt. As if it wasn’t enough to
lose her right leg by amputation, she has
also dropped nearly all her savings in the
luckless speculations of her spendthrift son.
Her great rival, Rejane, started a subscrip-
tion list, which was so successful that
Sarah’s beautiful home at Belle Isle,
threatened with seizure and compulsory sale,
has been saved for her occupation during
the rest of life.
“Ivaniva”: The arguments of “Private
Boxer” (B. 15/4/T5) against what “Avon”
had to say about the practicability of a
Shakspearian revival lose value when dis-
sected. To quote H. B. Irving’s “Hamlet”
as a sign that Shakspeare is not popular is
absurd. The first consideration for an actor
of these plays is a clear enunciation. Irving
gave an intolerable burlesque. He reminded
me of the Teutonic BandmanU. Australia
has not heard “Hamlet” spoken properly for
many years. Matheson Lang would have
drawn full houses as “Hamlet,” but he
failed to produce it. Asche may have lost
on “Antony and Cleopatra,” but he was the
wrong man to represent Antony; and in “A
Midsummer Night’s Dream” the cast was
very uneven, and not to be compared with
the Musgrove production of a decade back.
As to Waller’s “Henry V.” being a frost,
that mummer had committed the unpardon-
able crime of growing old. and his monarch
was too tame for those of us who had seen
Rignold.
“Avon”: I did not propose Sunday Shak-
speare on the magnificent scale of Oscar
Asche. My contention is that Sunday even-
ing performances of plays such as “Antony
and Cleopatra,” “As You Like It,” “Cymbe-
line,” “Hamlet,” “Julius Ciesar,” “Midsum-
mer Night’s Dream,” “Romeo and Juliet,”
“Tempest,” etc., at cheap prices, from the
existing resources of our theatres, would
draw as big crowds as picture shows, seven
6f which, in Bourke-street, Melbourne, are
filled every Sunday night. How delighted
our ambitious performers would be to enter
upon these studies ! I just leave that to the
Melbourne Greenroom Club. As for pro-
ducers, I would confidently entrust that work
to such as Niblo, Atholwood, Styan, Charley
Brown, or Muriel Starr. Nay, even Bland
Holt might emerge from retirement, and do
the job in- first-rate style. If the experi-
ment failed, I would subside, like H. G.
Turner, as a trustee of the Melbourne Public
Library, when he found that patrons wouldn’t
come on the Sabbath. Why must Germany
have the monopoly of Shakspeare?
“Fax”: Advts. of “Confusion,” in Mel-
bourne, state that the ancient farcical comedy
was “previously produced in Australia by
the famous Brough-Boucicault co.” It was
George Rignold, not B. and B-, who always
played “Confusion” hereabouts, the first pro-
duction in Australia being previous to the
B. and B. co.’s existence. When the
monotony of melodrama or the strain of
Shakspeare grew wearisome to “Handsome
George” he would stage “Confusion” for a
change. An old anecdote in point. The late
Harry Diver “pinched” the farcical comedy
and toured it through Maoriland. Seeing
that no objection was raised, Diver thought
his steal had escaped notice of the owner of
the Australasian rights in the piece. But Rig-
nold —good old cynic—was quietly saving up
a revenge for the young man. Presently he
announced a revival of “Confusion” in Syd-
ney, and invited Diver to come and study the
leading juvenile part. When Diver had
“studied” the lines which he already knew
by heart, Rignold gave him a fortnight’s re-
hearsal, still affecting ignorance of the facts
of the case. On the night of the production,
however, Rignold flummoxed Diver by play-
ing the part himself. “You had enough of
it in Maoriland,” said George to the spoofed
' one.
“THE SEVEN
KEYS TO
BALDPATE."
Maurice Dudley, as
Hayden the presi-
dent, shows signs
of anger.
“0.K.” devours a black pudding:—
I know now why the wowsers, who snort at stage
plays, are whales for picture shows. Somebody in-
duced me to go and sit among the godly at a
film production of “Cabiria,” and, O my brethren,
what an improving, what an inspiring entertain-
ment was there! A feast of murders and a flow of
gore lasting for 2$ hours. I revelled in awful
destructions of human life incidental to a story
which, to tell the truth, had more blood than body
in it. The disjointed plot seemed to be just a row
of pegs to hang gruesome tragedies upon. No
wonder the wowsers eat it as greedily as they drink
temperance beverages containing 37J per cent, of
alcohol! The story dates back to the bad old b.c.
period when all the princes of the earth were Kaiser
Bills and crimes were three a penny. It begins with
an earthquake and an eruption of Etna, after which
the little Cabiria girl is captured by pirates and
sold to a church dignitary, who intends to pop her
into the blazing interior of the great god Moloch.
Moloch, at meal times, is a feature of this charming
photo-play. The kids drop into his mouth like ripe
plums, and the audience sniggers with horror. Moloch
doesn’t get Cabiria, however. A black Jack Johnson
of the period, with his early Roman trainer in attend-
ance, rescues the dear little girl. Any number of
people are killed that she may live, all Carthage
being in pursuit of her rescuers. When the crowd
gets them cornered Johnson picks up his nearest
assailants and chucks them into cauldrons of boiling
fat, of what not. This sort of thing is always hap-
pening. The siege of Carthage is largely a procession
of Romans, who climb ladders and are hurled from
the topmost rung into eternity. The slaughter goes
on for years at one place or other. Conquerors come
and go, but Jack Johnson and his trainer and the
fair Sophy and her little lot remain as young as ever
all the time that little Miss Cabiria is growing up.
Finally, Queen Sophy poisons herself, and nearly
everybody else is destroyed except Johnson and his
trainer, who gets away with the grown-up girl for a
honeymoon on a yacht.
The cake offered annually by London
Pelican for the most popular performer in
panto., revue or musical comedy went this
year to Ivy Shilling, who is playing in
“Aladdin” at Birmingham. In view of the
fact that the runner-up, Ethel Colfer, was
900 votes behind Ivy, and that the third and
fourth places went to Gertie Millar and
Ellaline Terriss, the Australian girl has
plainly made an exceptional “hit” with a
public that does not easily abandon old
favorites —especially those with large teeth
and ducal admirers.
“C. Ockney” : Oswald Stoll, of Melbourne
and London, the man mostly responsible for
the popularity of the modern music-hall in
England, manages to earn a crust in
spite of the war. Business, in fact, on the
Stoll circuit is not only as usual, but is bet-
ter than ever. The dividends paid at the
various halls under Oswald’s direction for
the past half-year tell the tale. The 'aver-
age was 10 per cent. The Coliseum, the
huge twice-a-day house in St. Martin’s Lane,
which is under Stoll’s personal direction,
weighs in with 25 p.c. All of which goes
to show that Kaiser Bill hasn’t frightened
all the theatre-goers into the cellars. It
takes a lot to put a London playgoer off
his hunger for amusement. If bad acting
and worse plays could have done it he would
have died of starvation years ago.
“Screen” : I reckon the number of good-
class picture theatres in Melbourne and
suburbs as 34 open every night. As many
more are weekly or bi-weekly. Say over
60 altogether. It amazed us to hear a while
ago that Chicago, four times as big as Mel-
bourne, had only about 60. Perhaps Mel-
bourne’s two suburban finest are the rival
Barkly and Trocadero, in Footscray. When
one of these went up, cautious people ex-
claimed “Ruinous!” Yet up went the other.
Visiting both last week, I saw them pretty
full on off nights. Jack Cannot was at the
Trocadero. The Barkly advertised Ander-
son’s complete dramatic company in “The
Face at the Window.” So vaudeville and
what not are creeping in. The fact is the
picture supply is so short that entrepreneurs
fervently join in the prayers for peace.
There is a great loss in the French and
German contingent, and not much is coming
from England. Even the U.S.A. are not so
free producers as before, because their field
of circulation is so much diminished. Hence
the development of the “serial,” which goes
on night after night, week after week, drib-
bling out with no effort on anybody’s part.

Baron de Reuter’s suicide, in London, though
said to be due to the sudden death of h s
wife, was doubtless more or less a result o
the war. He was accused of German leai,
ings as soon as the row began, and never go.
quite clear of the hasty suspicions. A so
of the very Teutonic Reuter who founded th
telegraph business just 50 years ago, th*
late Auguste Julius Clemens was a Harrow
and Oxford johnnie. He seemingly adopted
“Herbert” as his front name when he sue
ceeded to the German title in 1899. Both he
and his co. were British enough. The direc-
tors, editorial staff and correspondents of
the agency are British, and only 20 out of
1200 shareholders belong to the Gerhog clan.
But Auguste Julius Clemens, otherwise Her-
bert, couldn’t get away from his German title,
and when the war happened he was made to
feel conscious that the business had an un-
fortunate name.
Whoever succeeds George Reid as High
Commissioner, it is time to consider the
whole question of cost. Australia has seven
Agents-General, with independent staffs.
When Australia voted for Federation it was
on the understanding that Moses’s rod was
to swallow all the rest. The. saving was to
be more than sufficient to pay for the High
Commissioner. But the State agencies,
instead of costing less, actually cost more.
The outlay on the High Commissioner’s
office in 1908-9 was £3050. In 1913-14 if
was £27,461. The new buildings, with land
and furnishing, are estimated to run away
with £650,000. Interest and maintenance
will be £40,000. Soon the whole thing will
mean about £78,000 a year. Adding the
outlay of £36.000 for the States, the agency
for this small population will be £114,000
a year, plus immigration expenses and pay-
ments to banks, etc., for doing financial
work. The Premiers’ Conference ought to
talk over the scandalous waste.
The Argus raises a chant of thanksgiving
because “Customs and excise receipts for
April are showing up so well that there is
now every reason to believe that the Federal
Treasurer will receive far more revenue
from these sources than he estimated in his
last year’s Budget statement.” In his Bud-
get speech Andrew Fisher calculated that,
owing to the war, the Customs and excise
revenue for the financial year would de-
crease £170,000. With 10 months gone the
receipts should, on Fisher’s figures, be over
£140.000 behind. Instead they are only
£87,000, and as there has been a marked
increase in trade during the last month it is
expected that the total shrinkage for the
year will be proportionately less. Obviously
a Protectionist tariff is not fulfilling its pur-
pose of building up local industries when
at a time like this the Customs revenue is
practically normal.
A case of a type thought to be dead was
heard before a Sydney magistrate a few
days ago, when Thomas Lovell Rothwell,
“Honorary Director of the Sydney General
Poor Relief Fund,” was sentenced to six
months’ gaol on a charge of false pretences.
Suspicion was aroused 12 months ago, but
it wasn’t until the Crown Solicitor received
at his private address a touching circular
asking for a subscription that things came
to a head. Inquiry discovered that about
£6OO a year was being collected for the fund
and the only disbursement traced was
through a letter, sent to a bookie —with a
cheque forwarded to Rothwell’s institution
by a philanthropic contributor!
“Bedlam”: The N.S.W. Labor Govern-
ment has proclaimed May 24 (Empire Day)
as a holiday throughout the State, “the cir-
cumstances this year being unusual.” If
Holman wanted to show a loyal spirit he
would, in accordance with the prayer of the
British Government for a speeding-up cam-
paign, have set apart the day as one on
which everyone would work harder than
usual. Presumably the people engaged on
the cruiser Brisbane, the ammunition fac-
tories, and the factories where military
equipment of all kinds is being made, will
either have a day off or be able to claim
double pay for working on. The King’s
Birthday is due the following week, and
George V. has expressed the earnest wish
that the anniversary shall be celebrated only
by the hoisting of flags. Holman’s way of
“following the King” is to proclaim a second
holiday. *
WASTED LIVES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Miss Aimee Kelly, the sweet-voiced Fairy Queen
of “Sinbad the Sailor,” is a famous equestrienne.
Miss Violet Paget’s favorite exercise is in the r6le
of equestrienne. In the West Indies Miss Paget says
she practically lived on horseback.
Miss Preston is a good horsewoman. She was well
known in Western Queensland for her exploits with
unbroken horses.
M. Sterzelly, who is appearing at the Tivoli, was
a rough-rider.
—-From one day’s theatre gossip in a Sydney paper.
The modern mummer sits his steed
Like some primeval centaur ;
The pro. is Master of the Horse,
And its accepted mentor.
When buckers have to be subdued
Our mimes can always do it ;
The prad that kicks or jibs with them
Is swiftly bound to rue it.
The lads on whom the flappers dote
(Gentiles as well as Sheenies),
For all their seeming foppishness,
Are Skuthorpes and Martinis.
The gorgeous ladies, with the clothes
That paralyse and dazzle,
At horsemanship have Mrs. Stace
Licked to the merest frazzle.
Skilled artists they on animals,
Whose habits are unlawful—
A reason why, perhaps, their Art
Is frequently so awful.

HOW TO MAKE FLESHY HIPS SLIM
AND GRACEFUL.
An excess of fatty tissue on hips or
abdomen not only prevents one from looking
well or feeling comfortable, but. it is likely
to keep increasing until all the youthful
beauty of the figure is gone. A very simph&
yet wonderfully effective .means .for quickly
and permanently reducing this ' superfluous
flesh consists in applying night and morning
a harmless herb mixture composed of one
dram of quassia chips and three ounces of
cirola 1 bark extract, which you can get at
small cost from any good chemist. Put the
quassia chips in a pan and pour over them
a teacupful of boiling water; in half
a minute strain through a cloth and add the
cirola bark extract, when it is ready feu*
use. This recipe is also very valuable for
reducing double chin, puffy neck, arms,
hands, etc. The fat shrinks away rapidly
leaving no wrinkles or flabbiness.
CAUTION.- —Be sure and apply on botH
hips or both arms, for a difference ■in one of
them would soon be noticeable if the' lotion,
were only used on one side.* "■»'
SOCIETY
Geelong has been enjoying a riotous
time. About a year ago the Citv Council,
coming to the conclusion that the town hall
wasn’t big enough to contain its dignity,
decided to pull down the old Grammar
School and have a new hall erected on the
site. A number of ratepayers objected to
thousands being “blewed” in this manner,
and when the council told them to go and
grow potatoes they rushed off to court, but
were biffed there good and hard and told
to pay the council’s bill of costs (£272), as
well as their own. This so elated the
A RIFT IN THE LUTE.
VISITOR (inspecting new portrait of hostess): “My dear, it is charming, superb! Divine!
But think of the effort of living up to it!”
councillors that they called tenders in a
hurry and let the building job. But just
after the contractor had started the whole
business fell to pieces with a crash. The
annual elections had come round, and the
ratepayers threw the council out on its
jaw. The majority of the new body being
anti-hallites, they at once pitched the plans
under the table, roasted the contract on the
grate, and paid compensation to the con-
tractor. Whilst this was going on the
council’s Gent. One billed the leader of the
objecting ratepayers for the costs. The
objectors refused to pay a bean, except £lOO
which had been previously deposited as
security. Being unable to collect the
balance, Gent. One reported the fact to the
council. The council decided to give the
objectors a release under seal of all the
costs that the Law said was due to the
council, and also to refund the £lOO held as
part payment. Thus the net result of it
all is that the council pays everybody’s costs,
while the usual unpleasant person asks the
44 winds of Heaven whether the time will
ever come when municipalities will have
sense enough to take a referendum on dis-
puted points of public policy.
* * * *
The horrible uncertainty of the beast that
men call Law was stressed a week back when
Chidley, the well-intentioned clothes re-
former, who was present in court watching
the last awful processes of his appeal, ven-
tured to say a word on his own behalf
about his own case. “Sit down!” said the
Chief Justice in rebuke. “The Court
doesn’t know you in this matter.” If
Chidley hadn’t been himself and had known
less about the facts, and if, instead of being-
clothed in light, airy garments like the
simple-living ancients, he had been disguised
in an unnecessary black cloak and had a
mass of hair belonging to a wiser and faster
animal than Man perched on the roof of
his skull, he would have been listened to
with the greatest show of respect by the
row of elderly High Court judges. But
being merely himself, the person the whole
trouble was about, and having no archaic
disguise, he was regarded by the mandarins
on the bench as an intrusive stranger.
For over 70 years one of the landmarks
in Auckland (M.L.) has been a windmill
near Symonds-street. About eight years
ago the owner, who runs a flour mill, took
down the sails and put in a suction gas
plant. He now reckons this is too expen-
sive, and has consequently added another
25ft. to the tower, bringing it up to 100 ft.,
and has brought the sails into use again.
As they are 9ft. broad by 70ft. from tip to
tip, the old whirligig is a more conspicuous
object than ever. It is the only flour mill
in Loan Land using wind for motive
power, and probably there isn’t another in
this Hemisphere.
“Callan Park” again comes to his
country’s rescue
The Rifle Club movement in Sydney would be
admirable as an evening pastime, only there are
hardly any rifles. In one affluent suburban club 60
members are trying to learn the art of war with two
rifles. It is difficult to achieve the “Kitchener
touch” on such lines. Efficiency is needed, and you
cannot make an efficient rifleman by handing him
one-thirtieth of a rifle three times per week. Faced
with this difficulty one is reminded of the fact that
there is no need to concentrate on rifles. Hand-
bomb throwers and grenadiers are doing just as
good work in Flanders to-day as the better-adver-
tised men behind the guns.
Here is an official description of the modern bom-
bardiers: “Around their middle they carry 20 or 30
bombs, little cylinders fastened on a long stick,
from which fall streamers of ribbon. Crouching
down among the barbed wire the bombers raise them-
selves a little from the earth, and seizing one of
these rocket-like bombs from their belts, grasp it
by the stick and hurl it high above the parapet.
Clouds of greenish smoke arise from the stricken
trench, through which hurtle lumps of earth and
stone and fragments of shrapnel.... After the trench
has been gained the bombers again come into play.
They help in a valuable manner to hold it. Bomb
after bomb, jampot after jampot, grenade upon gren-
ade is hurled out of the back of the trench.”
This is the very job for the amateur fighting man
of a country which has hung on to Freetrade so long
that it isn’t in a position to make enough rifles for
itself. Anyone can get the material to drill as a
bomb-thrower. All that is required is a jam tin,
a stick and some string. The hand-grenade which is
used at close quarters is an even easier weapon to
simulate. The only thing a club needs to practise
with is a half-brick per man. Youth, health and
physical strength are not nearly so essential in bom-
bardiers and grenadiers as they are in riflemen, who
must not only have keen vision, but be prepared to
use their bajmnets. Almost any elderly citizen can
throw half a brick.
And these are not the only ways in which they
could serve. For example, grave-digging. Thou-
sands of suburbanites who are now wasting their time
struggling for rifles in the name of Empire are
born gravediggers. All that they require to make
them invaluable to the allied forces is a spade
apiece and a week or so under the direction of a
competent sexton. As things are at the Front,
innumerable soldiers, skilled at their work and of
a fighting age, have to be withdrawn from the
trenches and employed to bury the dead. There might
be nothing spectacular about the training and subse-
quent departure of a brigade of expert gravediggers,
but they would be welcome in Flanders, more especi-
ally if each man brought his own tall hat, frock
coat, shovel and mattock with him.
“Pure Codde” : The Melbourne ring whose
manoeuvres have made fish a luxury in Vic-
toria struck a sort of Sedan the other day.
A “disloyalist” firm imported some M.L.
blue cod —fish which the ring has been selling
for a shilling a pound. The trust-breakers
asked 10d., whereat the combine came down
to 9d. But the public, remembering the
innumerable tuppences it had been black-
mailed out of, rallied round the “disloyal-
ists” and bought at lOd. The same thing
happened in the case of a cargo of oysters.
It almost looks as if the public is waking
up. The other day when the Governments
imposed a few fresh taxes to meet the special
conditions created by the war, the borrow-
ing policy, the drought and other calamities,
some traders immediately attempted to make
money out of the community’s ill-luck.
Tobacconists who had been selling a certain
brand of cigarette at a shilling a packet
(and making about sd. on the deal) bumped
up the price to Is. Id. The effort of the
Sydney publicans was as bad, or worse. The
smoking and drinking public took much the
same line as the fish-eaters, and with entire
success. Nobody asks Is. Id. for those
cigarettes now, and beer is, in some quarters
at least, cheaper than ever. Where the
recreant trader scores is in the fact that the
public’s memory is as brief as pleasure. The
resentment at being swindled dies almost
before the swindler has finished banking his
gains. If boycotts were not so evanescent
—if the man who has been “scaled” once
refused to put himself in a position to be
“scaled” twice—many flourishing, little
rings and combines would explode. Several
tricky tradesmen might even become honest.
It will be a hundred years on May 1
since the first sittings of the first Supreme
Court of N. S. Wales in its civil jurisdiction
were held. The Court sat in one of the
northern wards of the then newly-finished
hospital in Macquarie-street. The presiding
judge was Jeffrey Hart Bent, assisted by
two magistrates appointed for the purpose,
Alexander Riley and William Broughton.
The sitting was only a preliminary one, dur-
ing which the court was formally opened
and the members of the court sworn in;
then proceedings were adjourned till the
following Friday, May 5. At that sitting
consideration was given to but one matter
—a petition from some attorneys, who had
left their country for their country’s good,
but had been allowed to practise in the
courts prior to that date. Judge Bent at
once announced his refusal to hear them,
but the two magistrates agreed to allow
them to plead. Bent then adjourned the
court and appealed to Governor
Macquarie, who sided with the magistrates
on the grounds of expediency. This led to a
lot of correspondence, which had at last to
be referred to England. Bent, although he
remained in N. S. Wales for two years
longer, stubbornly refused to call the court
together again, and thus hung up all litiga-
tion. Finally Lord Bathurst superseded
him and appointed Judge Barron Field in
his place.
THE CAROUSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At to-day’s meeting of the Liverpool Plains
(N.S.W.) Shire Council it was resolved that during
the continuance of the war the council, instead of
adjourning for a social glass, would take up a collec-
tion for the Belgian Fund. —News item.
I met Mcl-laggis in the street,
His eye with pleasure glinted.
He seized my hand and wrung it hard.
And said, with warmth unstinted:
“Come right in here —let business be—
And have a Belgian Fund with me.”
We had it and rejoined the throng ;
McHaggis seemed contented,
Until a poster caught his eye
On which there was presented
The legend, “Socks for soldiers, please!”
He said: “We’ll do a pair of these.”
We did —and then an Ambulance,
Some Shirts, a Motor Kitchen;
My throat was parched, my face grew long
And grey as any lichen.
He said : “I hope you won't be vexed —
We join the ‘Track the King League’ next.”
I bolted from McHaggis here,
And fled for home and liquor ;
I filled a glass, I raised it high,
It looked like golden ichor.
To drown the drunk I’d had on pelf
I had five whiskies with myself.
N.S.W.

That held in place her dainty shoe,
And sent a wireless on the wing,
And wondered if she’d catch the
cue.

My wish had not been all in vain;

Joe,
So it won’t come undone again.”

* * * *
Sydney was given an object-lesson in the
difference between good soldiers and bad
ones —or rather between trained soldiers and
the other sort —during the route march last
week. The infantry- and cavalry-men, who
had been schooled to behave as soldiers
should, marched or rode like machines, their
faces serious, their eyes to tfie front.
They looked inspiring, efficient, manly, omin-
ous, resolute —all that they should. Along
came another lot, which it was hard to
believe belonged to the same race. Held
high in the air were the toy flags which they
AT THE BELGIAN FUND BAZAAR.
“Are you the girl who sells kisses for the boor
Belgians? ”
“I am; but I’m sold out ”
had collected from the crowd. From time
to time one of them broke ranks, collected
another flag, and scurried back to his place.
They talked, laughed and greeted friends and
people who were not friends. Some marched;
others walked; a number merely shuffled.
And yet the misguided lads obviously re-
garded themselves as the popular heroes of
the occasion. This type of warrior does
not understand the change which has come
over Australia in the last nine months.
Nobody has much use now for the soldier-
wag, the individual who wants to drill,
march, and, if need be, fight, along vaude-
ville lines. The occasion is too serious.
The heart of the people goes out to the other
sort- —to the soldier who is taking his job
seriously, and who shows it wherever he
makes his appearance.
* * * *
“ Jeames” : There is going to be money in
the “Follow the King” movement for some
people. For instance, Governors. One of
them told me years ago that the job would
be well worth while financially but for the
drinks. He didn’t put it in those precise
words, but that was what he meant. One
State Governor has already
“abolished intoxicating liq-
uor from Government
House during the war,” and
the rest of our imported
potentates may be relied on
to do it as soon as they
drop to the position. Enter-
taining, on an alcoholic
basis, costs the average
Governor two-thirds of his
salary. That is the usual
estimate during normal
times. In war time there
is necessarily less of it—
dances, for example, which
are the most expensive of
all festivals, are cut out. If
the few who do frequent
Government House these
black times are given soft-
drinks, the King’s represen-
tative will be on to an abso-
lute gold mine. For if it’s
soft-drinks or nothing, after
the first taste it’s invariably
nothing, unless thirst actu-
ally grips the throat.
The Argus is angry with people who seek
to disparage the poor cocky because he
keeps his children at the demnition grind
of cow so long that they are often found to
be too weary to assimilate knowledge at
school. The reasons for this child slavery,
if it exists, are given by the Argus—“pre-
posterously high wages for farm laborers,
and the blighting effects of drought.” But
the high rates of pay enjoyed by farm lab-
orers are a delusion; and the over-working
of infants is not so conspicuous an infamy
in drought time as it is in times of natural
boupteousness, when the cows are yielding
profusely. And in God’s truth the case of
the man who barbarously overworks his own
small boys and girls for profit in any cir-
cumstances is as much improved by apologies
as that of the South Sea savage who eats
his relations straight out.
One of the Kaiser’s troubles is to find
land jobs for German admirals. The latest
case is that of Admiral
von Hintze. On Janu-
ary 24 Wilhelm ap-
pointed him Envoy-
Extraordinary to
China. Speaking Eng-
lish like a native he
travelled to New
York, posing as an
Englishman. He was
unable to carry
swords, spurs and so
on through the Eng-
lish Customs, but lie
bought a brand new
outfit in New York.
The American Eagle
chaperoned him to
Honolulu. Here the
Admiral packed his
costume in a case and
consigned it as fruit
laxative to a Yankee
indent firm in Pukow.
He got himself a job
as supercargo on the
Christian Bors, a
Scandinavian tramp
steamer, and signed on
as an Australian.
Half-way across the
Japanese searched the
ship. Von Hintze’s
box was discovered
and taken away. At
Pukow he ordered
some more clot h e s.
Before they were fin-
ished he had to bolt.
Since then he has been
steadily getting into trouble. But Wil-
helm can’t recall him, as the cables
are all in the hands of the British,
and Ilintze couldn’t come if Wilhelm
did recall him, because the sea is
like the cables. Ilintze wouldn’t take
on another job as supercargo at any
price, and he couldn’t get it if he wanted it,
as the condition in which he left the
accounts of the Christian Bors will be
remembered in the China Seas for years to
come.
“Etaoin”: Enter this for the red-tape
championship. A civil servant who travels
about N. S. Wales sends telegrams from
each town he reaches advising certain per-
sons in the district of his presence. Tele-
grams cost sixpence each and are charged
for in the monthly expenses list. Recently
the civil servant found one of his clients had
a telephone, so, instead of sending a wire,
he rang up from the post office—cost, one
penny. The next ex-
pense list had a claim
for the penny, mainly
included for the rea-
son that he wanted
headquarters to know
he had been in com-
munication with his
man. A vigorous re-
ply came wanting to
know why item 13,763
was not accompanied
bv customary voucher.
So the civil servant
hiked away to the
post office to get a re-
ceipt for that brown.
The post-office clerk
couldn’t remember it.
Tire official said he
would pay again to
get the receipt. The
clerk said that was
all right, but to get a
receipt for payment
of a penny for the
use of the telephone
would cost a second
penny. So a second
penny was paid to
obtain receipt for the
other penny. Then
the civil servant
paused with a faint
feeling: What would
he do about the re-
ceipt for the penny
for the receipt for the
penny for the telephone? Would that, too,
cost a penny? With swimming head he
managed to send on three receipts, and is
now waiting for a decision as to how many
more will be wanted. He supposes the
P.L.L. will be asked to decide.
* * * *
The Holman Government has another
scheme for blewing the loan—this time
for providing families with an inex-
pensive holiday. It proposes to establish
accommodation houses in cool uplands and
health resorts, where board and lodging can
be had for next to nothing- The scheme is
to be limited, first of all, to the wives and
children of small farmers and laborers in the
Central and Western districts, and the first
shack will probably be erected at Yarrango-
billy in time for use next summer. Cheap
fares are included in the general proposi-
tion. It is a good enough scheme,
and there is not a word to be said
against it except that it costs money,
and that the people of the Western
and Central districts would very much rather
be put in a position to pay full rates for
their holidays. They could do it comfort-
ably, too, if Governments didn’t steal nearly
half the proceeds of their harvest and hire
foreign syndicates at extravagant prices to
carry out public works.
* * * *
The Burglars’ Union in Maoriland is
contemplating a strike. It declares that it
isn’t getting fair treatment from the
Supreme Court and as “Exhibit A,” offers
for inspection the following list of sen-
tences :
Shooting with intent, under great provocation
(jury recommended mercy), 15 years; ditto at
father-in-law (guilty without recommendation),
three months.
Incest (two charges), 20 years; ditto (six charges
—a shocking case), seven years.
Burglary (first offence), seven years plus seven
years’ reformative treatment; ditto (ancient offender
with a multitude of charges), 10 years’ reformative
treatment.
Pickpockets (first offenders), five years, three year;
and two years; ditto (old hands), three months, six
months and two months.
If the Judges don’t care about joining the
Burglars’ Union themselves, it looks as
though they might with advantage form one
of their own and draft some sort of a
schedule to govern their trade.
Dr. Golding-Bird, Anglican Bishop of
Ivalgoorlie, evidently thinks he didn’t lay it
on thick enough in his first appeal for
cheques in London. At any rate, he sent
out a second letter, which puts Kalgoorlie
and Coolgardie in the same district as
Sodom and Gomorrah. Thousands in his
diocese, he says, are “little better than white
heathens; they are growing up in absolute
ignorance of Christianity, and the standard
of morality is deplorably low.” But (lon]t
despair—he promises to put it all right if
the British public will part up another
thousand a year. It seems almost like
sweating to contract to shift so much sin
at that figure.

tore open a cemetery, and strewed coffins
and bones upon the surface.

A Gippsland settler is coming forward
with a patent for converting “the most
troublesome noxious weed in Australia”
into an inflammable spirit. “This weed
nearly drove me off my land,” he
says, “but I was put on the track
by accident, and now I light the
whole of my place with spirit distilled from
the growth.” Here, at any rate, is a mai
who makes light of his troubles.
THE WHITE FEATHER.
The Confessions of a Tody in Distress.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I sent a white feather to George to-night
The coward who stays behind !
Was ever a maiden in such a plight ?
My lover is sailing away to fight!
And—why is a man so blind?
Ah, me! but my lover has gone from sight,
I never shall see him more!
Alone must I mourn for my absent knight.
But George got a feather —and serves him right!
I pray that it hurts him sore !
I hope he will writhe when he sees the thing,
I hope he will guess ’twas I!
I want him to squirm at the scorn I fling ;
I’d love to be near him and see it sting,
And —I wonder if he’ll reply?
I sent a white feather to George. Ah, me !
To (his I have waved farewell —
Dear Gus, who is faring across the sea
To fight for his country, his flag—and me !
And the other —how can I tell?
Oh, how can I tell of the awful mess
I’ve made of the whole affair?
Yet how was a poor little girl to guess
The end of it all would be dire distress,
When I played with that spoony pair?
Yes, Gussie and George they were courting me,
And both of them seemed quite nice ;
For George is as handsome as he can be,
And Gussie is little, but jolly and free ;
And neither was prone to vice.
Now, wasn’t I lucky, with two such swains ?
And how could a maiden choose?
For Gussie was witty and blest with brains;
But George offered dresses and sundry gains
That prudence should not refuse.
I think, on the whole, it was George that led.
He had —oh, such splendid eyes!
But darling old Gus, with the things he said,
Would easily turn any poor maid’s head
If she wasn’t extremely wise.
So I played with them both, as a maiden will,
And smiled at their fret and fuss.
Dear George was my choice ; but I flirted till
The war came upon us. Then, prudent still,
I said: “Well, it must be Gus!”
For George seemed so handsome, so strong and
brave,
I thought he was sure to go.
One boy of the two for myself to save
Was just; so my answer I sweetly gave,
And sent him away with “No.”
Ah, me! I accepted poor Gus next day.
I had it worked out so grand !
Dear George, broken-hearted, would sail away
To bury his sorrow ; while Gus would stay.
Now, wasn’t that nicely planned?
Oh, I dreamed of it all as I sat alone.
If each had but played his part!
Poor George was to die with a love-lorn moan,
And then, ever after, would Gus atone
To my bruised, remorseful heart.
But —I sent a white feather to George to-night;
And my lover I’ve kissed good-bye.
Brave Gus, who is sailing away to fight!
And what holds the other? Mere craven fright!
Oh—l wonder if he’ll reply?

Missing: Friends.
Miss Louisa Mary Key (of Fulstow, near
Louth, England), deceased.
IF Mrs. LOUISA ALICE BUCKER
(born Louisa Alice Ewing), EMILY
CHRISTIANA EWING or HENRY WIL-
LOUGHBY EWING (who about 1882 were
residing in the Colony of Victoria. Aus-
tralia) or other the next of kin of Miss
Louisa Mary Key, of Fulstow, near Louth.
Lines., England, will communicate with Mee
& Co., Solicitors, Retford, England, they
may hear of something to their advantage.
WILLIAM BARNET WALKER, of Yackandandah,
Victoria, last heard of in KalgOorlie, West
Australia, is requested to communicate with the
undersigned, when he will hear something to his
advantage.
F. ,T. T. LYNE, Solicitor, Tallangatta, Victoria.
PERSONAL
ITEMS
BLUNDELL, S. Australia's new Minister
of Industry.
One A'ictorian Governor’s wife at any rate
did something that will be gratefully remem-
bered. Lady Talbot busily interested her-
self in the Bush nursing scheme and the
supply of good milk for infants. The death-
rate has fallen from 88 to 64 per 1000 births
in districts where the “Talbot” milk is dis-
tributed. If that isn’t the result of the
pure fodder, it is anyhow a coincidence
which it is worth while trying to reproduce
elsewhere.
Who invented that saying about ill news
travelling fast? Australian poet Adam
Lindsay Gordon shot himself at Brighton
Beach (Vic.) away back in 1870. Last
week a Yankee paper was delivered at The
Bulletin office addressed to the poor fel-
low whose funeral took place 45 years ago!
J. G. Edwards, for many years owner of
the Bendigo Independent, is dead at last,
after not feeling very well for half a genera-
tion. lie came from Tasmania and was a
compositor and more or less trained to the
printer’s job ; but during most of his days in
Bendigo he passed the control of the Inde-
pendent over to E. A. Banks, a bearded old
democrat, who ran the paper to the length
of the rope he was given, and since Edwards’s
day has made it frankly Labor. Simultane-
ously Willie Wright raked in the pennies in
the manager’s room. The proprietor was
thus able to slip out between his two assist-
ants and spend his mornings on the police-
court bench —an inconsiderate habit, since
it obliged at least one reporter to waste
many weary hours listening to the illoquence
of Sergeant Fahey and his unworthy suc-
cessors.
Passed out unobtrusively in Sydney the
other day Bro. John Wheeler, formerly an
Emperor or Doge in the local Orange move-
ment. Time was when politicians occupy-
ing doubtful seats shuddered beneath the
howls of John. Dread of hostile machina-
tions on his part drove them to Town Hall
platforms, Clifton Gardens picnics and simi-
lar functions. Yet he was no Torquemada.
A mild, hairy man, with the sodden figure of
one who had spent a lifetime eating buns,
there was no vindictiveness and a good deal
of humor in him. The only violent thing
about him was his voice. For as long as the
constituency would have him he was in the
N.S.W. Parliament as Freetrade member for
Canterbury, and for a crust he toiled 40
years for the big Wallsend Coal Co., which
he managed on safe but stodgy lines.
Died at Chislehurst (Eng.) William Wil-
lett. a notable crank who was taken seri-
ously. He caught the crankiness from Wil-
liam Morris the poet. Morris wanted every
man to sing at his work, and Willett wanted
the singing to be done in sunshine. His Day-
light Saving Bill was heavily fathered in the
Commons, and nearly got through. In the
N. S. Wales Assembly the earnest and
unhumorous Dr. Arthur took it up, but had
no better luck with it. On the more
practical side Willett was a moneymaker,
and died very wealthy. His line was specu-
lative building.
Until quite lately four Victorian drivers—
Bahl, Norman, Richmond and Sitlington—
had practically run the whole of the passen-
ger-train service on the Ararat-Portland line
from its opening nearly 40 years ago. Then
Sitlington’s eyes fell so far below the
departmental standard that .he had to climb
off the footplate, and he now passes out on
a pension. The others have still a year or
two to go before they cease watching the
prssure gauge.
The Defence Department did the right
thing and set Assistant-Minister Jensen on
to the important job of making an inquiry
into the recent change in command at the
Broadmeadows (Vic.) training camp —an
inquiry really as to whether an officer in
Australia was to be penalised for making
so conspicuous a success of his work that
the public noticed it. No time was wasted
about the inquiry either, and the result was
the wholly satisfactory return of Major
Taylor to the command.
Jack Fitzgerald, the new Vice-President
of the Executive Council of N. S. Wales,
“arrived” in politics along with Chief Sec.
George Black. He headed the poll when
four Laborites were returned for West Syd-
ney nearly a quarter of a century ago. The
other two were Andy Kelly and T. M.
Davies, both dead now. That was the occa-
sion when the hustings erected in the Town
Hall enclosure broke down, and about one
hundred persons—candidates, proposers and
seconders—fell in a heap.
Died last week, W. P. Manning, who had
been mayor of Sydney, M.L.A. for part of
it. and generally an enlightened and useful
citizen, with an extra pound behind his
shoulder when he was giving music a lift.
He belonged to the Protectionist party when
it didn’t amount to much in N. S. Wales,
and for that reason was one of J. P. Gar-
van's early friends in founding the present
Mutual Life and Citizens’ Insurance office.
A more selfish man would have made a mil-
lion for himself with his knowledge of
finance; being what he was he made some
money for himself, but more for Lord Rose-
bery and a good many other wealthy British
investors whom he represented—or in some
cases saved them from making the big
losses which early wild plunging promised
to land them in. For digging a man or a
concern out of a bog there were few better
in Australia: and he had one of the times
of his life in connection with the highly
successful reconstruction of what is now the
Australian Bank of Commerce —the old
A..T.S.
John McGuire, who died at Albury the
other day, was a brother-in-law of Ben Hall
and intimately associated with the bush-
rangers of early days. He was arrested in
connection with the attack on the gold
escort at Eugowra Rocks, and with eight
others was brought to Sydney for trial. 20
police accompanying them ; but he produced
an alibi that time. He was 91, blind and
very feeble when the end came.
Richard Pearse, Mayor of Ballarat (Vic.),
is not going to “follow the King.” George
has followed him. A little Richard took the
pledge from “Billy Bray,” a once-famous
Cousin Jack evangelist, 'way back in the
Dark Ages, and noth-
ing that has occurred
since has seemed worth
fillin' ’em up for—not
even being thrice
Mayor of the town or
residence in the same
cottage for half a cen-
tury. The old man
was 70 last week.

May birthdays; G. M. Prendergast, M.L.A.,
formerly leader of Vic. Labor party, G 1 on
the Gth; “Peter Pan” Barrie, 55 on 9th;
Queensland Chief Justice Cooper, 67 on 12th ;
Justice Chubb (Q.), 70, and Agent-
General Newton Moore, 45 on 17th;
Madame Melba, 50 on 19th; Governor
Gerald Strickland, 54 on 24th; astrono-
mer John Tebbutt, 81 on 25th; Queen
Mary, 48, and Archbishop Riley, of Perth
(W.A.), 61 on 26th; Archdeacon Gunther,
76, and Fred T. Robertson (associate-editor
Adelaide Advertiser), 57 on 28th; and poet
George Gordon McCrae, father of Hugh and
Dorothy, 82 on 29th.
BAKHAP , the whiskers champion of the
Senate, who made a very pood speech about
Defence last week.
Mrs. Jane Nicholls, a Ilawkesbury native
who died the other day, within three months
of her 93rd year, had a father who lived to
be 93, and her mother was 87 when she died.
Two sisters are living; one is 91, the other
82. She left four sons and three daugh-
ters. The eldest is 72; the youngest a
flapper of 54. To further illustrate the
degeneracy of the Bulldog in these parts, the
sons, though nuggety enough, stopped short
soon after passing 6ft., while a kid of the
third generation is a mere Gft. 4in.
Tom Playford, ex-Premier, ex-Agent-
General and ex-Senator of South Aus., was
a market-gardener—one of scores who stand
as high socially in Adelaide as any other
kind of cocky, and live in far pleasanter
surroundings than most of them. The
Eastern States owe a. large debt of ingrati-
tude to Ah Sin. There he pushed into the
cabbage-growing business, and the cabbage-
growing business soon lost caste and thou-
sands of white Australians who ought to
be using the hoe are making out bills for
imported fluff or hanging on to a precarious
job paid for with hard-cadged Government
money.
The French know how to pay a compli-
ment. When Captain Oswald Watt was
decorated by Joffre the latter ordered that
the password for the day should be “Syd-
ney.”
Dead in Adelaide, at 81, William Barlow,
doctor of law, and vice-chancellor of the
University. A brilliant Irishman, of Trinity
College, Dublin, he was nearly 40 when he
came to Australia, with a reputation for
legal oratory. Yet a lifelong shyness in-
duced him to take up solicitor’s work—the
two were always combined in South Aus.
He ran Richard Baker’s office while that
brilliant politician was doing State or
Federal business; and at the University—
to which most of his later life was devoted
—he was always willing to let Chancellor
Way do all the public appearing while he
himself did the “spade-work” in the rear.
In _ appearance he was the typical Don
Quixote, and in manner a gentle, lovable
humorous old Irishman of the Ulster variety.
From the sands of Egypt:—•
The cussedness of things! Private Reginald Arthur
Beard falls 20ft. from the Pyramid of Cheops, and
is for the rest of his life paralysed from the waist
down. Private Norman Yeness, ricochetting 11 times
in a roll of 350 ft. down the face of the same pile of
masonry, is none the worse except for a few scars.
Beard was attempting to climb at a dangerous point
when the rotten limestone gave way. He lay all
night among jackals and mosquitoes before he was
found and patched up by Dr. Fred Bird at Mena
House. He was then sent back to Australia, crip-
pled at the age of 34. Yeness, whose people live
at Manilla (N.S.W.), lost his footing at the 30th
“step,” after which he finished the journey on what-
ever limb or other excrescence bumped the blocks of
stone on his way to the ground. He was picked up
by his companions with an Bin. fracture of the top of
the skull, a 4in. fracture near the right temple, and
a third near the left eye. After a week’s unconscious-
ness he recovered, but has had to return home, a
standing advt. for Bird's surgery. Another soldier
who fell from the same height was killed, and Cheops,
except for the north-eastern side, which is regarded
as a safe thoroughfare, was then placed out of
bounds.
THE GAOLER’S LOT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Bogtown (N.S.W.) Police Station was the sub-
ject of much discussion at the last meeting of the
shire council, and one councillor characterised it as
a “dank, darksome dungeon.” It is riddled by dry
rot and ants, and when it rains the officer in charge
and his family have to shelter in a corner and go
next door to boil the kettle. —Evening 'paper.
While the cold drops pitter-patter like a rain of
gallows’ tears,
And the summons lies all sodden on the chair ;
While the gloomy gaoler sniffles through the damp
and dreary years,
In the place his stern Department won’t repair;
Lo, the criminal goes drily on his villa-breaking
round,
And he whistles very softly at his job,
As the copper, ’neath a brolly, tries to frizzle half
a pound
Of raw bacon on his spotted, spitting hob.
In the place of ants and dry rot falls the never-
ending rain,
And the parchments and blue papers fall to bits;
And the face of Law an’ Order is a mask expressing
pain
With a hopeless scatteration of the wits.
So the gaoler in his dungeon catches nothing but his
death,
While the dry and cheery criminal goes gay;
And he snuffs up eucalyptus in between each wheezing
breath,
Dreaming sadly of the cops who’ve passed away.
When he’s had his thumping Dead March, and there’s
one damp Hop the less
To inveigle merry thieves into the cells,
Will he find warm comfort elsewhere, and forget his
past distress
In pursuing bone-di-y devils through hot hells?
Oh, I see him in my day-dream lugging demons
through the flames
With his red-ghost handcuffs glowing on. their paws,
Just to punish them for playing at forbidden wowser
games
Where old Judas at his chains forever gnaws.
N.S.W.

And that is after six solid months of war.
But what about the future? Can we keep
going like this? It seems to this paper
incredible. We have only kept going like
this so far by the grace of the British Gov-
ernment, which propped up our national
finances as soon as they began to sag. by
the issue this year of Commonwealth notes
of the value of over £15,000,000, and by the
profuse expenditure of money in connection
with the fitting out of the expeditions and
the feeding, etc., of the army. Queensland,
while it did not share directly in the scatter-
ing of the £18,000,000 with which J. Bull
obliged Australia a few months ago, has
had a large share of the other fictitious
prosperity. Admittedly it has had a lot of
real prosperity as well, the inevitable result
of years of good pastoral seasons and high
prices, combined with phenomenal returns
in the sugar business. But for the future
the outlook holds these among other things:
(1) the serious consequences of a drought
in the Western district —a drought far more
acute than the papers have suggested to
the general public; (2) failure of the com-
ing season’s sugar crop in many districts
through shortage of summer rains, and the
harvest in the second season from now will
also be affected more or less; (3) not only
the stoppage of the supplies from J. Bull,
but the fact that we shall have to start pay-
ing out interest; (4) the issue of paper has
gone just about as far as it can go. so we
must stop churning out money in that easy
way. Doubtless nature may presently he
kind and send us bumper harvests and
clips, and the markets may be good, and
mining may take a turn for the better. All
these helps and more Queensland will need
if it is to jingle money in its pockets as
freely as it has done lately.
The Royal’s position compares with that
of 12 months ago this way:—
1914. ' 1915.
Bills and notes .. £18,299 £19,962 Inc. £1,663
Deposits, etc. . . 1,617,238 1,636,287 Inc. 19,049
Liquid assets . . 781,300 766,049 Dec. 15,251
Advances . . . . 1,420,735 1,456,250 Inc. 35,515
Premises . . . . 73,876 78.143 Inc. 4,267
Actually more deposits now than it had
before the war started ; and. in spite of the
drought and the hanging-up of so much wool,
the advances are only £35,515 more, from
which it is fairly safe to conclude that town
traders have been reducing their liabilities,
and that’s a good thing. Taking the figures
into account, and remembering the prices
which have lately been charged for shifting
money, it isn't surprising that the Royal of
Queensland has sustained its profits. Cer-
tainly there are banks which, expecting to
make some bad debts out of the business
now in hand, have been shipping down their
disclosed profits and putting more by to meet
contingencies. But it is quite possible that
this bank is justified in saying that what
seems to be ahead of us is no worse than it
has been steadily providing for all along.
If so there is no reason why it should make
any special provision now. The visible
reserves to protect the capital, should things
get worse than it expects, are not very sub-
stantial; but the deposits are beyond doubt.
Enough to pay nearly half of them is held
liquid; and there, is £lOO worth (book
value) of other assets to pay every £5B of
the rest of the debt.

It never seems possible to make a table to
carry a co. from its birth without decorating
it with an explanation of some sort. In this
case the prospectus was dated June 28, 1912,
and was not of course issued until a few
days later,_ whereas the profits were with cer-
tain restrictions to belong to the co. as
from Feb. 1. 1912. In the final settlement
there was £2BBl coming to the vendors as
their one-sixth share of the year’s profits.
Also £2140 profit made prior to the regis-
tration of the co. came to the co., but was
not available for dividend. The deficit with
which the co. started is made up this way:
Goodwill, £50,000; extra payment to ven-
dors as above, £2881; preliminary exes.,
£3300. The £2140 profit made prior to
registration is included with the £10.054.
The prospectus contained an auditors’
certificate which said that, excluding a very
bad year, the average profits for eight years
from 1904 onwards “show that a co. with
a capital of £320,000 as proposed would on
your present turnover be able to pay”
£21,000 in dividends and carry forward
nearly £10,.000. It seems a fair thing to
ask wlnit has happened. The present share
capital is £220,000, with £lOO,OOO of 5 p.c.
mortgage debentures. If the £5OOO interest
on tin' debentures is added to the profits of
the past two years, it will be seen that the
total averages only £17,600, which is a long
way from £31,000. What has eaten up the
other £13,400 ? Trade was certainly not
very brisk in Melbourne last year; but what
about the year before? Somebody ought to
explain something. In particular that audi-
tor ought to be given a chance to show why
very little more than half the £31,000 he
talked about has been forthcoming.
The vendors took £lOO,OOO in 5 p.c. mort-
gage debentures, £36,000 in 04 p.c. cum.
pref. shares, £30,000 in ordinary shares, and
£50,000 in deferred shares, with an option
over 100,000 ordinaries at par. For under-
writing they transferred to “Big Bill” Bail-
lieu and his friends 15,000 deferred shares
and the option over half the 100,000 shares.
The deferred shares, issued for goodwill, are
not of the malignant sort. That is to say,
they don’t confer the right to half or three-
quarters of the total profits in excess of a
given rate of div. payable to the other shares.
Under that system a deferred share might
easily be entitled to a 50 p.c. dividend if
the thing were a success, while the ordinary
and pref. dividends plodded along at any-
thing from a mere 5 to 20 p.c. In this case
the deferred shares get nothing until the
ordinaries have had S p.c., when they are
to get up to 8 p.c. if it is there. After
reserves of £30,000 have been accumulated
the deferred become ordinaries.
One of the troubles afflicting the co. is its
load of debt. Including the debentures the
liabilities on January 31 were £234,585. But
even so something is wrong. With £134,585
of floating debt, mostly no doubt cheap;
with £lOO,OOO of a debenture debt costing
only 5 p.c.; and with £lOO,OOO of a pref.
debt stationary at 04 p.c., the £70,000 of
ordinary capital ought to carry a very easy
S p.c. in good times —as times were up to a
few months ago. If they can’t do it then,
they arc going to go very short indeed in bad
times. Finally, holders of ordinaries may
profitably reflect that, since the deferred
shares lose their handicap when £30,000 has
been put to reserve, there isn’t much likeli-
hood of ordinary shares getting more than
8 p.c. for a long while to come. Any profit
beyond that will go (1) to give the deferred
their 8 p.c.; (2) to reserves in order to wipe
out the deferred’s handicap.

That is to say, after providing for all liabili-
ties there are enough assets, apart from
anything the business may be worth, to
represent the capital (£130,000) and leave
£15,027 over. But it is really very difficult
to disconnect goodwill from a trading busi-
ness of this sort: for if the time comes when
the business —that is, the goodwill—is worth
nothing at all, then it is practically certain
that the factory premises, plant, and per-
haps even some of the stock will be worth
far less than their book value. That, how-
ever, doesn’t justify a co. in leaving good-
will among the assets. It seems to be the
tendency of businesses to decay just as
assuredly as it is of human beings; and
the man who will not write down goodwill
out of profits might almost as well go a
step farther, and, backing his own immor-
tality, refuse to ever take out a life policy.
When you say, then, that Perdriaus have
£15,027 of reserves irrespective of the good-
will, you don’t say that if something quite
unforeseen happened and the co. fell to
pieces, there would be 225. Od. in the £ for
shareholders. There would be a rush
for the shares if you could have
security like that ami 10 p.c. And
somewhere about here is a point to
which the politician won’t give sufficient
attention. “Ten per cent!” he says. “Mon-
strous! Why should people get 10 p.c. for
their money? The duty which is protecting
them is too high!” Or if the concern is
paying 8 p.c. the duty is high enough. It
would be—if the capital was secure. But
it never is. The co. puts the money into
plant, etc., which on liquidation is liable
to be worth ss. in the £or less. And if it
lias borrowed money—which in desperation it
generally does before it gives the thing best
the chances are the shareholders don’t get
much of the ss.
Tlie_ co. lias developed rapidly during the
P as t n ye years, and now covers practically
all classes of rubber goods, from umbrella
rings to motor-car tyres :

ot profit _ that has been written off against

piovided for apart from the visible machinery
reserve—then it has taken 80 p.c. of the

Australian Steamships, Ltd., being
the shipping side of the Howard Smith
business: —•
Net t 0 n e . p e .

in}?’* MM 72 4 P-c. =£32,820 £7652 £7,662
IJI4. . (0,i40 8 ~ ~ 67,133 6607 14,259
‘After long and careful consideration” the
Howard Smith directors decided ‘‘for sev-
eial material reasons” to turn the shipping
assets over to a separate co., paying the
parent co. in shares. The Howard Smith
directors constituted the Board of the new
concern, and the parent co. was made man-
aging agent; so the change, though effected
tor several material reasons,” made no
practical difference—anyway, so far as
finances are concerned. All that happened
was that both the Howard Smith and the
Australian Steamships, companies are able
to tell inquisitive political commissions that
they are not combined coal-mining and
shipping cos. -and it will be remembered
there was a bit of a commotion over that
matter when the shipping combine was being
inquired into.
At the end of the co.’s first year the
capital was £820,513, all but £5 of it issued
to the Howard Smith Co., that being the
valuation of the net assets arrived at by
three experts. During the past year the
capital issued to the vendor co. has been
increased to £839,618. Of that and the
reserves, etc., £782.060 represents steamers
and plant at cost, less depreciation. What
amount of profit lias been written -off
against depreciation is not mentioned; and
without that information, or at any rate
some indication of the facts, the figures are
not worth much. The net profits claimed
for the first year were reduced by the exes,
of forming the co. At the end of the first
six months the directors said there was a
loss showing; and thereupon freights, etei;
were put up. Evidently things have been
going all right since then. For the first half
of the past year the div. was at the rate of
6 p.c. . For the second half the Board; —
which isn’t in the habit of paying away
more than it. has earned or of making too
little provision for contingencies—distri-
buted a div. at the rate of 10 p.c... making
8 p.c. for the year. The co. lias a fine fleet,
a fine connection and the knack of money-
making, and if it can’t pay at least 8 p.c. it
will no doubt be a very surprised and dis-
appointed crowd. Its financial position ■is
of course very easy. Its cash, after provid-
ing for the div., exceeds its liabilities ; but
no doubt the money in hand will bo used
up during this year in ships now building.

Elder’s Trustee and Executor Co.
(Adelaide) :—•
Towards
•Tmie, Profits. Dividends. Reserves. Deficit.

* 4ar -> Reserves.
1911.. £468 Nil £468 10
1912.. 425 Nil 425 435
1913.. 1722 Nil 1722 2157
1914.. 2435 6 p.c. =£4200 1235 3392
1915.. 1883 6 „ „ 1200 683 4075
Though only four years old this co. has the
control of estates valued at £1,405,146. It
is an astonishing performance, and suggests
at first sight that the appointment of Elder’s
Trustee Co. as executor is as fatal as an
argument with a torpedo. But it isn’t that.
In parts of S. Aus. Elder, Smith and Co. are
something like “8.P.” used to be to thousands
of Island niggers. The name stood —and still
stands—for something like omnipotence and
omniscience. Elder, Smith and Co. probably
recognised the value of interesting itself in
the affairs of the departed. Anyhow, if it
didn’t, the agents of a rival, who generally
followed on much the same lines, used to be
instructed to “look after the dead men.” So
the co. probably got into close touch with
many deceased estates; and when this separ-
ate Elder’s Trustee Co. was formed, and put
in charge of a man who had had 20 years’
training in the excellent school of the T. E.
and A. in Melbourne, numerous trustees re-
lieved themselves of their responsibilities and
thus set the co. up with a ready-made busi-
ness. The co. is in 100,000 shares of £1
paid to 4s. (£20,000). and all the money is
in Govt. 3f p.c. stock. There is thus no
question whatever about the recognisances
entered into by the co. to be of good behavior
—recognisances which no private trustee
would think of entering into. Even the
reserve fund is lodged with the Govt. So far.
shareholders have got the most gilt-edged
security for their principal; and they have
prospects also of substantial dividends. Not
more than about 10 p.c., perhaps; anything
beyond that is likely to impel the Govern-
ment to take a hand. But even 10 p.c. is
very choice on such security.

That is better, though the extra profit com-
pared with Dec., ’lo, isn’t enough to pay the
dividend on the extra capital. However, the
investor isn’t so much concerned with what
a brewery co. did last half-year as with what
it is likely to do the year after next. As
an outcome of the war some people are going
to abolish drink off the earth. There never
was a war in the course of which the public
didn’t make tremendous resolutions. And
there never was a place where it wasn’t re-
garded as more or less a public duty to break
those resolutions. The threat to never touch
it again is probably worth just about as
much as the threat never to wear a German
sock even though it should be better and
cheaper than all others offering. It is not
the war that is likely to have serious perma-
nent consequences for the Trade. It is the
women. Their vote outnumbers man’s; and
whether they care to have an opinion of then-
own about politics in a general sense or
not, the majority of them will let no man’s
hand guide their hand when it comes to
marking a ballot-paper on the liquor ques-
tion. S. Australia has just had its say;
and bit by bit privileges will almost certainly
be nibbled away from the Trade elsewhere.
llow that will affect the brewer remains to
be seen. A good deal will depend upon the
interest he has in beer pubs. It smells as
if there was a particularly cold time ahead
for the pub that is chiefly bar. It is impos-
sible to say how this co. stands in relation
to that. Of assets totalling £131,212 no less
than £109,927 is represented by a mixture of
“freehold and leasehold properties, plant and
machinery, rolling stock, goodwill, water
supply, etc.” It is pretty certain that there
is a substantial whack of-pub in that lot.
In addition to the reserves mentioned in the
table there is £17,199 of a depreciation re-
serve. On the other hand, there is an un-
known quantity of goodwill on the other side.
One way and another, considering the doubts
which pervade the co. in particular and the
Trade in general, it is a business not to be
recommended just now- to maiden aunts and
excitable persons with weak hearts.

Although practically all pastoral Queens-
land is suffering from drought, and money
is scarce out west, there still appears to be
plenty of cash in Brisbane. Further, this
year’s sugar crop has missed the monsoonal
rains and is looking very poor. So the
cheque from that business, too, will be small
compared with last year’s. Notwithstand-
ing these things, cash, as before remarked,
is plentiful in Brisbane. Twelve months
ago the City and Suburban Society had
£104,741 of deposits to play with. Now
after seven months of war and drought, it
has £103,470, or only £1271 less. But it is
unlikely that things generally can continue
like this; wherefore the directors have done
the right thing and gone back to the old
■> P.c. dividend. Advances amounting to
£46,649 were made during the year, as
compared with £44,680 in 1913. But it is
noticeable that repayments have been com-
ing in more slowly: whereas in 1913 the
total amount outstanding had increased by
only £594. this year’s figures show an in-
crease of £4540. Still the difference is
small, and the co. will be fortunate if
borrowers stand as well up to the collar as
that all along. Assets, apart from
advances, are: Freeholds, £7000; invest-
ments and cash, £17.884; and sundry
debtors, £55 —not at all excessive, though
there is. no reason whatever to expect that
there will or can be any sudden demand for
the repayment of deposits by the hundred-
weight. And no other sort of demand could
inconvenience the co. seriously, seeing that
its total assets a'mount to very nearly twice
the total liabilities.

“Well, show me,’’ were the words we heard used
by a business man to a canvasser the other day. That
is the essence of conviction —a positive proof. Let
us “show you” what “Pioneer” leather belting' can
do. We won’t place any “it's” or “buts” on the test.
Let us put a length of “Pioneer” on your stifl'est
drive. Ask our nearest office to-day, and if it doesn’t
transmit the maximum of power at the minimum of
expense we shall be prepared to abide by the test.
Make the trial now. “Pioneer” Leather Belting and
Engineering needs are manufactured only by J. 0.
Ludowici & Son, Ltd., “Pioneer Leather Works,” 117
York Street, Sydney (opp. Q. V. Markets). Inter-

ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth.*
From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard slacked, on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. Bd., as against 4s. lOd.— a saving of 2s. iid.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow i6in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2

Further extensions of plant are ahead, and
the co. proposes to issue £25,000 of deben-
tures to meet the bill and ease a temporary
loan of £17,000 incurred for recent works.
It shouldn’t have much difficulty in finding
the money. Fortunately for shareholders
there is usually a very fair margin after pay-
ing the dividend; for the capital, which is
only £60,000, will have to support the de-
bentures as well until the new works can
earn their own keep. In view of the possi-
bility of a Victorian Gas Act, probably on
much the same lines as the N.S.W. measure,
it is questionable whether an issue of deben-
tures represents the best tactics. The capi-
tal is £60,000, and all of it is paid up. Fro: i
the shareholders’ point of view a better
scheme might be to increase this
amount and call up part of it. The
co. would then be in a safer posi-
tion, should a Gas Act arrive, to transfer
its reserves to capital. Gas sales continue
to move in the right direction, and the income
from that source for the half-year (£11,098)
shows an increase of 10.7 p.c. over that for
the latter half of 1913. No allowance is
made for depreciation, but various amounts
•—shown in the table as written off —are
spent on renewals, etc., and charged to Profit
and Loss. Liabilities, including the “tem-
porary loan” of £17,000 aforementioned,
amount to £19.217 ; and liquid assets reach
£10.711. including stocks, which are only
liquid in a manner of speaking. The co. has
been making a fine lot of extensions on a
very little extra capital. Seven years ago its
capital was £48.871, and its plant brought in
a gas revenue for the six months of £4829.
Now, with a capital of not quite £12,000 more,
and nothing very serious in the way of debts,
it has a gas revenue of £11,098. Things
must be very wrong somewhere if share-
holders haven’t got something better ahead
of them than the present 6 p.c.
