Some of the figures in the last line are esti-
mates : they will have to be adjusted when
the appropriations are shown six months
hence. The co.’s capital and shareholders’
loans- —they are practically the same thing,
both being withdrawable —now amount to
£114.201. of which £54.157 is in stock and
£48,645 in property and plant. The small
balance is in book debts, cash. etc. The
basig is all wrong and improper and danger-
ous and ought to be illegal. Otherwise not
a word can be said against the highly
respectable old concern, whose whole fault
indeed springs from its struggle to be unlike
a Fatman’s money-grubbing proposition.
But that’s no reason why it should under-
take to repay, almost on demand, a large
sum-of money three-fourths of which is in
premises and stock. At least two-thirds of
the capital employed ought to be fixed. It
shareholders wanted to realise, they could
then go into the open market. As it is, they
can go to the co. and demand that it buy
the shares at par.
The most hopeful thing in the Amalga-
mated Zinc’s progress report is that
costs are set down at £18,797, and as the six
weeks’ run produced 11.617 tons of zinc
concentrates the average is only £1 12s. sd.
per ton. The assay was 49.6 p.c. zinc,
7.70 z. silver, 5.4 p.c. lead. These con-
centrates contained 5762 tons spelter, and at
the phenomenal price of £5O the value for
this alone is £288,100. But don’t reckon
on that. Zinc is worth £5O a ton only
because this and other cos. like it can’t turn
their concentrates into metal. Taking
spelter at a saner £22. the Amalgamated
reckons its profit at £8872, which is a shade
under 30s. 6d. per ton of zinc contents and
15s. 3d. per ton of concentrates. The dump
has already increased to over 60,000 tons,
and in that lot the recoverable zinc is
approximately 20,000 tons. That so great
a value should be locked up in a heap of
ore in one co.’s yard is exasperating; more-
over, it is inexcusable in a country that
owns some of the finest coal seams in the
world.
-*■**•*
Y Waterholes’ half-year ended on Janu-
ary 31; and even in a term chewed into
by war and drought a profit was. shown,
though it amounted to only £2766. The
plant worked for seven weeks. Then, a fort-
night before the half-year ended, it started
again, smelting arrangements having been
made with Mount Bisclioff, and things have
been going on well ever since. The dredging
plant is still on the eastern ground, and
though there isn’t more than about six
months’ work ahead there, what ground is
left is very good. As stated here some time
ago, the western half has been re-bored,
and has been proved worth from 0.9 to 11b.
tin per yard for 3,000,000 yards. That, of
course, is not as good as the average of the
eastern ground, which has given lately about
111 b.; but with a decent tin market and the
reconstruction of the plant —for which there
will be ample means on hand by the time
the eastern ground is worked out —Warlow
Davies and his adviser Moline (who is
nowadays tackling the tough Cock’s Pioneer
proposition at El Dorado) believe they call
promise very reasonable dividends.
Mouramba Mines, Nymagee (N.S.W.), an
English-owned property, originally controlled
by the Longworths. is tipped for another
start. The Carriers’ Union has been asked
if it is prepared to cart so many tons of
coke from the railway at a certain price,
and has said yes. The co. has had a private
survey for a railway made from the State
system at Hermidale, which is the nearest
point. Apparently it has despaired of get-
ting the Government to put the line down.
* * * *
0.5. A.. Cobar, still has hopes, that, a rail-
way will be built to assist it with its
low-grade ore. There is also some likelihood
of a furnace being put up on the mine. It
is about time something was done. For
years the co. has been rooting around and
sending the best of the ore away for treat-
ment at a small profit. Last year 1254
tons were shipped to smelters for a return
of £6426. and 678 tons of “good” copper ore
remained at grass- The loss on the year’s
operations was £1653: but the ore at grass
may wipe out that deficit, or most of it.
The output from the Vie. State coal-mine
at Wonthaggi will be about 530,000 tons for
the current financial year. It isn’t a mighty
thing, but it is a very painful matter to
the few Gippsland coal-owners who first
laughed and then snorted at the idea of the
Government having any mine. The Rail-
way Department takes over 300.000 tons of
the output. The rest is available for Pri-
vate Enterprise (which loves chear> fuel
for its own furnaces) and household pur-
poses.
* * * *
At Lake Boga, near Swan Hill (Vic.), a
start has been made to work gypsum and
salt springs, also a deposit of lime. £7OOO
is being spent on machinery, which is being-
made locally. It is expected about 100 men
will be employed when all three substances
—gypsum, lime and salt- —are being pro-
duced. All of which is a text for a sermon
about the need in Victoria of looking for
something else besides gold and tin.
# * * #
“California Gully”: A recent Wild Cat
par. about the Bank of N.S.W. anniversary
revived old memories. It was stated that
the bank’s profits in 1851 were £22.478. and
in 1853 they jumped to £178.196. And this
is the reason why. In 1851-2. approximately
2 500,0000 z. of gold were raised at Clu.nes.
Castlemaine. Bendigo and Ballarat, chiefly
alluvial, and mostly worth £4 an ounce and
over. The banks engaged in buying gold
were the Oriental, the Bank of Victoria, the
New South Wales. Union and Australasia,
and most of their gold was bought by
trusted buyers who travelled from rush to
rush and gave the diggers what they pleased.
My own father bought gold on Bendigo from
1852 to iB6O, and he reckoned he weighed
and paid for personally over one million
ounces on behalf of one of the banks,
whose deadliest rival was the Bank
of N.S.AV. The old man used to say
he never met a gold buyer who had
equalled his record. The banks paid
from £2 10s. to £6 an ounce for gold
worth £4 to £4 45., so their profits were
gigantic, after paying escort expenses, in-
surance, freight, and all shipping costs to
the London Mint. Years after, in the same
bank. I personally bought on assay Huntly
and Epsom alluvial gold running up to 22
carats, and have even paid £4 4s. for nearly
pure stuff. No wonder the Bank of N.S.W.’s
profits jumped !
* * * *
Shareholders are not likely to get much
out of the final clean-up of the liquidated
Golden Pole (W.A.). The plant, leases,
etc., which stood in the books at £23,923,
realised only £OOO, but in view of the sys-
tem of mine finance this isn’t very astonish-
ing. It is quite usual for a mine which is
worked out to have a main shaft with a
book value of many thousands, and plant
and machinery worth on paper many thou-
sands more. And yet, the property being
valueless, the machinery can hardly be sold
for old iron. As for the main shaft, it
isn’t an asset —it is an expense, since it
must be fenced in to prevent accidents. In
the case of the Golden Pole the cash in
hand on February 5 was £134; so, lumping
that with the £6OO and deducting liquida-
tion exes,, shareholders may get about Bs.
per 100 shares.
* * * *
Three Ivurnalpi prospectors turned up in
Kanowna (W.A.) the other day with £l7OO
worth of gold and gold ore on their backs.
The stuff came from shallow workings and
included a 420 z. and a 20oz. nugget. As at
most old alluvial fields there is always a
chance of striking it rich at Ivurnalpi; but
for one who does there are scores who don’t,
and beyond contributing a few hundred
ounces to the State’s yield every year or
two and giving employment to a few
battlers, the field counts for very little nowa-
days.
Edna May Deeps (W.A.) is down about
200 ft. and is sinking as fast as it can to
the point where a slice of the Edna May
shoot is expected. Meantime the market
seems to depend for support on the inter-
section of quartz veins and lode formations,
several of which have been cut. Some
trouble has been experienced with bad
ground, but that is said to have been over-
come. The way in which the Edna May’s
reef has opened un at the No. 3 level of that
property is considered to enhance the pro-
spects of the “Deeps.”
Edna May (W.A.) starts crosscutting at
No. 4 level next month. The mine is look-
ing well at No. 3, where, contrary to the
expectations of many, it has a bigger shoot
than at No. 2. It is said that one end of
the No. 3 level is in ore of much above the
average grade, and that the other has been
stopped 17ft. from the Central boundary,
with a face of 13ft. of ore ahead.

Sons of Gwalia (W.A.) held its annual
meeting in London on April 27, _ when
“everything passed off satisfactorily.” A
most hopeful view of the future was taken
by the chairman, but as the ability of the
main shoot to live much deeper is still un-
certain, the market is inclined to be cautious.
On March 31 Westralia’s State batteries
had pounded 1,027,944 tons of gold ore for
an extraction of £4,340,000, and the tin
dressing plants had treate_d 70,000 tons of
ore for a recovery of £85,000. Just now
both types of treatment plants are very
helpful. # *
“Oof Bird” (4V.A.) : The Empress is
taking no risks. Some time ago stoping of
rich ore was left off in the bottom workings,
and now, with apparently both eyes on the
market, the co. is about to drive a level at
a point only 25ft. or thereabouts below
where stoping ceased. This, combined with
the report tliat working capital is fast run-
ning out, makes one sit down and think.
* * * *
Mammoth Copper (N.Q.) expects to make
another start with its furnace early in May.
Coke and limestone are being hauled to the
works by traction engine, and a fair quan-
tity of ore from the co.’s Chieftain and
Clansman mines is in the bins. At. the
Chieftain the deepest level (No. 3) is at
230 ft. vertical. It is intended to sink the
main shaft to 400 ft., the co. being encou-
raged thereto by the big ore body
that has been opened in No. 3 north level.
The latest report was that this level was
in 145 ft. from the crosscut, and that the
ore was the full width of the face, and more,
and worth 12 p.c. copper, soz. silver and
Idwt. gold.
Austral Malay, which nosed out the
Kamunting. Ivampong. and one or two other
fine-looking dredging propositions in the
Malay States, has under consideration its
biggest thing to date. Managing-director
A. W. Freeman cables his people : “Believing
a large part of the Taiping field will pay for
dredging, have arranged options to end Sep-
tember four square miles. Scheme involves
central station generating current for elec-
trical dredges treating half a million yards
monthly. Government sympathetic.”^
“Quidam” : A curio in the way of batter-
ies stands on Campbell’s River, near Rockley
(N.S.W.). Long ago—so long that none of
the local people appear to remember any-
thing definite about it—it was worked by a
water-wheel, which served two boxes of
6-head each. Instead of solid cast-iron boxes
as nowadays, each box has only a cast-iron
bottom, with sides of riveted wrought-iron.
The dies are one solid oblong block of cast-
iron, and the heads are simply solid blocks
of cast-iron with a hole for the shank. Evi-
dently the removable shoe had not then been
devised. All the tailings went into the
river. A still older stage in batteries was
shown by Beard’s mill at Tambaroora, where
the heads and shanks were square; The
Campbell's River relic had arrived at the
round revolving stamp, and near it again is
an old 4-head mill in which the heads are
fitted with-shoes of the present pattern, and
each stamp has its separate die.

Cognac Brandy.
BOTTLED IN FRANCE.
TO THE SOLDIERS.
LORD KITCHENER’S MESSAGE.
The public of Sydney has been waiting
ever since the cables came through stating
that the British Expeditionary Force.s had
proceeded to France, for the full text of
Lord Kitchener’s advice to each member of
that historic company. It has now been
cabled out, and one of the chief of all sen-
tences in it, and fraught with great mean-
ing, reads: “Your duty cannot be done
unless your health is sound.”
Health to the soldier fighting for his
country, or to the civilian fighting the great
trade battles at home, is essential. A
healthy army should be a successful army,
and Captain H. E. U. Burke, formerly of
the “Third King’s African Rifles,” British
East Africa, states that Clements Tonic is
the ideal nerve medicine for the soldier on
active service, or recuperating after severe
illness or fever. It is the medicine that has
always been to the front throughout Aus-
tralia, and its effect upon weak or sorely-
tried nerves is little short of miraculous.
All chemists and stores sell it everywhere.*

Sydney, April 2(5, IUI3.
.My Dear Arini, —
G.-G. Munro Ferguson took the salute on
Saturday, while “Tipperary” was yelled
along the line of march by thousands of on-
looking youngsters. For an hour or so
before the first plumed hat bobbed into Mac-
quarie-street, the police troopers ambled their
satin-skinned horses up and down the lines
of the patient crowd. Part of the quarter-
million was eating its lunch in Hyde Park at
noon, so it had a long wait for the crawl of
the great brown centipede at three o’clock.
Society, with his Anglican gaiters and a
herd of politicians and their legal ladies
lined every balcony and spare inch of blue-
metalled space near the saluting base as the
magnificent examples of Australian manhood
trooped along the broad highway.
Enthusiasts who dashed across town to see
the end of the march found a bleak wind
and squally showers waiting for them at the
Town Hall. Here an intermittent mixed
choir bleated in the windy area near the
iron railings. When its bleat was over,
the Scotch pipers smashed the silence with
stupendous noises. When they took two
bars rest, a sentimental orchestra on the
other side of the steps gave “Mary of Argyle”
and similar ditties to cheer the waiting mul-
titude —which by this time had retired under
its umbrella.
On top of the wind-swept Town Hall por-
tico aldermen and their friends were packed
like damp herrings when the rain began to
splash. Then the crowd bawled a welcome
to the French • sailors who headed our in-
fantry. The Light Horse stepped out briskly
to the tunes of “Tipperary” and “The Mar-
seillaise” —a medley that echoed along the
wet streets. The great white-hooded Red
Cross waggons, the tramp of feet, the roll
of gun waggons, and the waving of tiny flags
filled in a stirring moving-picture.
(Jetting the crowd home to its evening
meal was the least satisfactory part of the
day. Thousands of women with kiddies
hanging round them were drenched by the
heavy rain that fell at six o’clock. Pater-
familias, growing desperate, scoured the
streets for trams, which were grotesquely
insufficient; yet not so grotesquely insuffi-
cient as ferries across the harbor. It was
only after two and a half hours’ jostling that
the Milson’s Point wharf was cleared. The
Mosman arrangements were almost as great
a scandal and reproach. The Ferries Co.
made many a thousand of new opponents on
Saturday. But the co. seems to thrive on
enmity.
Barrister llanbury Davies folded in the
palm of his hand a cheque for £SSO the
ether afternoon. This was the cash that
.-•oiled in from subscribers to the Artists’
War Fund exhibition. Colonel Reuter
Roth, of an army medical corps that goes
to heal the wounded, hurried round the
R.A. Society's rooms in khaki, and dropped
a word of thanksgiving for the Red Cross
comforts that follow our boys to the long,
long Front. As chairman of the Red Cross
executive, Mr. Davies quietly hugged the
artists’ cheque at this juncture, and re-
marked that it would buy a first-rate motor-
lorry. James Fairfax handed the cheque
over on behalf of the committee —with a
postscript to the effect that the Art Stu-
dents’ tableaux would add £lOO to the
funds. After which the lottery tickets were
rolled furiously in two barrels, and Oxnard
Smith read the names while a colleague—
entrenched behind the second tub—supplied
the numbers.
Subscribers’ faces stretched in horizontal
or perpendicular lines according to what
came their way. Governor Strickland’s
name was fitted with the figures that
entitled him to Salvana’s “Cattle Camp.”
John Lane Mullins’s countenance exnanded
in a satisfied smile when “In Brittanv.” a
virile water-color by Maud Kimbell, fell to
his five-guinea ticket. She is the Maori-
land girl who had been painting in France
for some. time till the war sent her this
way again. A Scot who got 50 guineas’
worth of Blamire Young’s brush-work for a
tenth of the money looked as if he’d drawn a
winner in Tatt.’s.
The A.J.C. intends to come down with a
further handsome donation to the Belgian
larder. Empire Day race-meeting receipts
will be handed over without deducting a
cent, and president Adrian Knox, K.C.. is
polishing up a sub committee to carry this
decision into effect-
Poland’s cries for help have raised a
sturdy working committee to mould various
helping entertainments into shape. Arrange-
ments for a matinee at the Little Theatre
on April 30 were knocked into form at last
week’s meeting. Premier Holman again
expressed his sympathy, which is backed by
the State’s pound-for-pound subsidy till
about the end of June. The Little Theatre
manageress, Violet Paget, is bustling along
rehearsals for the matinee, and Julius
Knight will don a suitable wardrobe for the
occasion. Tivoli and National Theatres are
also offering help to the hon. organiser, Mrs.
George Earp.
The most picturesque widow hereabouts is
the young and handsome relict of the late
Roderick Cameron. She was Miss Linde-
man, of the wine-growing family. Roder-
ick, who changed his mind about being a
confirmed bachelor when he saw the Sydney
girl, took her away to New York. Now
she is home again in wonderful draperies of
raven black. Even her baggage is said to
be something in tremendous purple, and her
car is en suite.
Still another wedding in Egypt between
a fighting man and his best girl. The bride,
Marjorie Pockley, belongs to the Antill
Pockley clan, formerly of Killara. Her next-
of-kin in sandy Egypt, Col. Jack Antill.
handed her over to her young man, George
Knowles. He is a son of Admiral Charles
Knowles, and he stood not on the order of
his going to the war, but hurried off as a
volunteer in a Ceylon lot which has been
in the Nile country for quite a while. You
have no trouble to get an Australian con-
gregation if you insist just now on having
your wedding at a place called Kasr-el-Nil.
The North Sydney Bernard Newmareh pair
were present, and Mrs. Bernard gave the
wedding spread. The Sid Christians
(Major and Mrs.) also looked on to see fair
play.
The Graham Moffats have been putting in
a sea-breezy holiday at Avoca Beach. The
author of “Bunty” astounded the seagulls
with his prowess in the breakers as he
tonicked his nerves for another theatrical
outbreak. This time it is to be “A Scrape o’
the Pen,” which starts in the Palace
Theatre on May 29.
The widow of Admiral Charles Cross —
formerly Edie Mclntosh, of Bathurst—has
two sons fighting the common enemy. One
is. or was. in the French trenches; the
other is with Jellicoe in the North Sea.
Director Maiden, of the Botanic Gardens,
had the town-planning dames buzzing
around an out-door lecture last week. It
didn't happen to be raining at the time,
so the hard-worked gamp wasn’t able to
spoil the situation. It may be that amongst
the town-planning ladies there are some
who would make the world better if they
would but curb their gigantic intellects
and plan the family dinner and clean up
the back yard. Still, the enthusiasm for
such pursuits as planting herbage in the
public streets is a craze of the moment, and
not to be easily killed. You see, my dear,
no woman ever gets her name in the papers
for attending to her own back yard.
Mrs. Kirchner, who used to be Jean
Walker, is still staying with her German
mother-in-law near Berlin. Hubby was
snatched from liis honeymoon to make him-
self useful in the Kaiser’s big city. As
little or no outside news percolates through
the enemy’s press—except, of course, tales
of the scarred ruins of England—the Aus-
tralian woman knows so little of our side
of the war pictures that she believes —or
did a few months ago —that the Common-
wealth is a German possession !
At the National Council’s executive
meeting the other day Mrs. Tom Hughes
took the best chair. Miss Rose Scott got
away with a long indictment against care-
less husbands who skip over to other States
and leave the missus to be supported by the
Government. The Federal politician will
shortly be interviewed by indignant Woman
on this rift in the domestic pianola. The
notion is to cover all Australia with one law
on the matter, so that absconding Man may
be chased with a long blue paper, even
though he does tumble through a border
fence. At this stage Mrs. Ebenezer
Vickery, who had a sheaf of correspondence
to unload, nearly exploded. Mrs. President
Hughes, believing the meeting at an end,
was about to withdraw in peace. But Mrs.
Ebenezer. in a voice trembling with emo-
tion. called the attention of the company
to her little lot of literature —still unread.
So Mrs. President got back to work. At
least, I suppose this is work.
Robert Parker’s fourth concert was illum-
inated by a magnificent vocal effort —his im-
pressive attack on the famous Tannhauser
gem, “O Star of Eve.” With Truman at the
organ and little Florence Scapini drawing
her bow tenderly across the fiddle-strings,
this Wagner item was an event. Parker
understood the Town Ilall acoustics better
at his later concerts, and his great voice,
which may be approached with respect as an
organ, drowned the echoes that lurk over the
eastern gallery. Mrs. Carmen-Harris, a
vision in rose draperies, had no corsage
worth mentioning to disarrange, so her top
notes struck the rafters in the same old place.
Why the Harris lady should be so deter-
mined to sing contralto music, when Provi-
dence fitted her up as a showy dramatic
soprano, is best known to herself. Her
frocks are for that reason much bet-
ter than her lower notes. Harold Whittle
consistently supported the company from his
usual place at the piano. Parker was handed
a floral boomerang the other night, and Mrs.
Pinchof-llarris got away with stacks of
flowers.
When Harold Whittle, who accompanies
Parker on the piano, was rehears-
ing with the Canadian, the voice floated
out of the window —no, I am not referring
to the Parker mouth —and filled the adja-
cent street. A man who stood on the near-
est kerb waited till Parker had finished.
Then he hurried up to Whittle’s room. “I’ve
been listening to that pupil of yours, Mis-
ter,” said he. “What’s your terms for
teachin’?”
Artist Bernice Edwell has gone to pitch
her camp in Melbourne for a while. She
means to find out if it’s the Promised Land
for artists that it has been cracked up to
be.
A North Sydney man, E. F. McLeod, for-
merly on the Malwa, is now navigating loo’-
tenant on H.M.S. Royal Scot.
The new recruits in camp with King
Edward’s Horse have a thick sprinkling of
Cornstalks. Campbell Lloyd, of Tamworth ;
H. Hepburn, a Cooma man; and A. W.
Mayne, from the same uplands, are included
in the latest roll. Ted Capper, of West
Maitland, is under canvas in the same park.
Sydney ’Varsity women had their annual
spread in the big sarcophagus of the Uni.
the other evening. The trencher-boards
present were, members of the Women’s
Union, which is a kind of Black Hand Soci-
ety in blue stockings. Its vendetta at present
is. against everybody who won’t help its
friends the Belgians, and it will, if neces-
sary, rob their domestic fowl-roosts for the
cause. The Black Hand made its own sac-
rifice by. munching ninepence worth of sand-
wiches instead of its customary three-and-
sixpenhy annual dinner. The other 2s. 9d.
per head fell into the Belgian hat forthwith.
President Dr. D’Arcy presented the honor
list, and specially mentioned Dr. Isabel
Ormiston and Dr. Elsie Dalzell. Both are
graduates of Sydney Uni., and their work at
the Front has been mentioned in headlines.
Dr. Elsie has now tucked up her sleeves for
duty in. a Servian field hospital—about the
stillest job any woman can look for. Gladys
Marks, an Arts graduate, not long back
from England, told stirring tales of Red
Cross toil in the wet little isle as the com-
pany washed down its sandwich with obla-
tions of coffee.
Artists Thea Proctor and Justine Kong
Sing are batching together in Chelsea. I
believe the place used to be an old inn, but
tea is the tipple nowadays.
Concerning socks, it were better for the
comfort of Tommy Kangaroo if the enthu-
siastic knitter who achieves a lumpy result
abandoned the experiment. Tommy’s feet
are much too precious to practise on. A
few shillings sent to Mrs. Keith Jopp’s sock
fund (which hides its cash-box at Elizabeth
Bay) enables the committee to pay experts
to do the business. Of course, there are
other ways-—including one which is becom-
ing the nightmare of visitors to once-peace-
ful suburban homes. The lady of the house
gives the' caller her distracted attention for
one minute. Then she faces her with three
or four balls of wool. Appended to eacli is
the blighted beginning of a sock. The
stunned visitor is commanded to go ahead.
So She writhes and falls to. Her work
may differ violently from the stitchery that
preceded hers —the work of a former
pounced-on caller. But no one seems to
mind how bad or how good the result is
likely to be. Boracic powder may comfort
the wearer.
Julius Knight’s farewell to Sydney—for
a few weeks, anyhow—found the gallery
girl in her old place; and bombs of feminine
applause exploded in the Royal when
Julius, in “The Lifeguardsman,” fell
through every obstacle, including a bad
motor smash-up, in time to claim his girl—
Princess Dorine Brown. Julius had only
half a second to spare. He won the lady
by the length of an eyelash from his hated
rival, Prince Hugo, who was too worried to
take off his busby or top boots for the State
Ball that formed the climax. Irene’s red
hair topped some handsome frocks in this
stirring melodrama. She scolded her Cabi-
net Ministers in a Futurist gown of floral
design, upholstered in a long blue tunic with
a heavily-loaded tail that banged the stage
like a flail. A shimmering white coat with
black velvet edges was a more effective color
scheme, with the sable drapery of the skirt
coming to a thin, curly end on the floor.
The spot light that found Julius on his knees
before his stage girl trembled on Irene’s
ball gown like a streak of moonlight on
phosphorescent water. A court train of
soft white bunny rounded off this stunning
pantomime-Queen effect.
Mrs. Hugliie Ward had Mrs. Freddie
Shipman and Amy Castles in the manager’s
box. At the end they looked out on a
garden of blooms which had been laid
at the feet of the company—with special
favors for Lizette Parkes, who looked like
Beauty in a florist’s shop.
The Gallery trustees recently bought
three charming etchings by ,T. B. Godson,
and Englishman who means in future to
boil his billy in Australia. He came from
the Fog about three years ago to an art-
teaching billet in Auckland. But art limps
in that commercial town, and the lure of
Sydney has brought him this way. The
new man, who has five years’ training in
the London College of Art behind him, is
now prepared to fight Syd Ure Smith fox-
picked positions on the harbor foreshores
for etching purposes.
While the enemy is smashing up famous
architecture in Europe, Sydney is on the
crest of a wave that washes town-planning
gatherings into all kinds of special corro-
borees. The women’s committee the other
day sat down in the Australia while archi-
tect Sulman took the chair. Naturalist
Froggatt then discoursed on the domestic
habits and piratical instincts of the house-
fly, and in the buzz that followed Mrs.
Burley Griffin—the U.S. lady architect who
helps hubby with town-planning—lent bel-
aid to shape a women’s executive.
The N.S.W. Institute of Architects is
already in the thick of preparations for its
biennial show in October. The Town Hall
will be packed from the cellars to the
ground floor with a model cottage competi-
tion. Just here woman ought to knock and
come in; for in the average suburban cot
built by the Inferior Creature the kitchen is
an inconvenient kennel hung on to the
hottest side of the humpy, the forage depart-
ment is as remote from the cook depart-
ment as the builder can put it, and there
are no sane ideas in the matter of cup-
boards. Anyhow, president Anderson, of
the Architects’ Institute, is promised a
lively time if the lady plumber or the lady
gasfitter does get into any of the competitive
struggles.
We have lost, for a time anyhow, the
Rajah of Pudukota. lie is an unassuming
potentate who doesn’t care for Society frills.
For the next few weeks Melbourne is to
have a chance to stalk him for its Best
entertainments. But he knows the jungle
tricks, and the hunter who captures him
must be wily.
On April 29 a garden party to help Hos-
pital Saturday collection will occur in the
Wentworth Hotel winter resort.
Mrs. Theodosia Wallace, who contributes
to the social-news items in various Sydney
papers, was given a souvenir of sovereigns
by her friends the other afternoon. Mrs.
Ilolman, as the voice of the Avomen’s com-
mittee, handed the pleasant insult over.
Mr. and Mrs. Francis Chapman and their
only chick, Evelyn, leave for London in
June. The girl is aching to get back to hex-
art studies, and the family will probably
settle for a while in ancient Chelsea. The
Sydney National Gallery, as you know,
i-ecently bemrht clever Evelyn’s picture,
“Blue and Gold.” for 75 guineas.
The Naval House tea-fights for sailors’
Avives are temporarily shorn of the presence
of Miss Henderson —the strong-minded
naval lady Avho keeps an eye on the social
Aveather boAv, so to speak, at these parties.
She is getting her second Avind on a Queens-
land holiday trip, and returns to the fray
next month.
There is likely to be a row on the sub-
ject of nurses’ uniforms before Europe
settles down to rebuild itself. Here the law
is so slack that the trained nurse begins to
jib at wearing any outdoor symbols of her
pi'ofession, because any untrained Gamp can
put on the garb of the Nightingale. The
latest squall—no bigger than a woman’s 5j
glove at present—is developing over ambu-
lance classes. Damsels Avho have lately
attended, with more or less useful results,
1.0 or 12 lectures, are floating round in the
uniform which is supposed to be the badge
of years of hospital chores. Professor Andy
Stuart, who is a first-class fighting man,
might shake things up before the genuine
sick-bedside comforter entirely discards the
saucy little bonnet of her trade.
The Inside Inn, as it is called. is. the
only tavern within the Panama Exhibition
grounds. It is built on the usual U.S. plan,
and contains, in addition to 1100 guest
rooms, a village of shops and baths, which
latter you must nay to approach. Aus-
tralians, who consider a bathroom a free
perquisite, always kick against parting with
cash for the privilege of washing them-
selves. But it seems to be the usual thing
on the other side. The huge hostel, which
is prepared to swalloAV up the tourist and
his tips, looks like a trust in large flat-
roofed iron tanks, indented with windows.
The spiky feathers of some of this sea-
son’s millinery shoot up and cross in mid-
air. They average about 10 inches in
height, and are frequently emerald-green
survivals of the barn-door fowl’s humble
tail.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MAKES THE WEAK AND THIN
STRONG AND FAT.
Weak and thin people—those who are
troubled with indigestion, nervousness, sleep-
lessness, liver trouble, etc. —can become
strong, fat and hearty, if they will only use
a little common sense. Just bear iu mind
that it’s not what you eat but what you
assimilate that makes flesh and strength;
and that the nerves directly control the pro-
cess of assimilation —the conversion of food
into flesh. Weakness, thinness, sleepless-
ness, nervousness* etc., are all signs that your
nerves are weak and must be strengthened
by a proper tonic food before they can hope
to do their work properly. For this pur-
pose there is nothing like the plain old-
fashioned nerve tonic and flesh builder the
chemists prepare by mixing iox. tincture
..Cardamoms Compound, 2oz. Sal rad o Com-
pound, 2oz. Elixir Simplex and water.
One or two tablespoonfuls of this tonic 20
niinntes-.before eating, or whenever you feel
W'd®,- tired or nervous, will freshen you up
amazingly, and if used regularly for a week
will, by building up and strengthening the
nervous ' systent,'overcome all those com-
plaints and symptoms due to weak, devital-
ised nerves. You will be full of energy and
vitality, and should put on two or three
pounds of firm, solid flesh every week. In
fact, on account of its flesh-building proper-
ties, it should not be taken by people who
do not want to put on flesh.*

MELBOURNE CHATTER
MELBOURNE, APRIL liU, 191.").
My Dear Yarrie, —
Melba’s concert will have happened by the
time this reaches you. The seats were all
gone after the plan had been exposed for
three days. Some people still have hopes
of clinging by their eyebrows to the chan-
deliers, but they are optimists. Melba has
circularised Melbourne to the effect that
she will only take £lOO bids for her special
Belgian flag. You might think that that
was a reliable method of preventing the
flag from being sold, but if so you don’t know
Melbourne and you don’t know Melba.
When she fixes her eye on the company and
says in a compelling manner, “And how
much am I offered for this flag?”—well,
someone has to spring another century or
go outside and hang himself for a skinflint.
Talking of Melba’s magetism, she hawked
her concert tickets in person. Amongst
other places she invaded the Stock Exchange.
“A guinea? Certainly,” said Mr. Bull-bear
affably, as he dived for coin. “I can get
a guinea anywhere,” was the haughty an-
swer. “These seats are tw r o guineas on
’Change.” What a bagwoman she would
have made, to be sure!
Hugh I). Mclntosh came over from Syd-
ney to say good-bye to the Tivoli Follies.
It was a braw wild night. Half the boxes
were filled with men of the arrogantly pros-
perous type which shies at the "Follow the
King v movement as though it were a tooth
extraction. A sad lot of dogs they looked.
1 was told on good authority that there
wasn’t so much as one churchwarden in the
lot. In the other boxes were yearning-
young men, who leant over the edges in a
Burne Jonesy fashion and sent burning
glances at the damsels on the stage. Take
it from me, dear, it was no place for a
wowser. To put the lid on the whole busi-
ness, as it were, the beautiful man-dancer
who revolves around Vera Pearce split his
pale-blue silk unmentionables. It was ouite
like Paris, or Home shortly before the Fall.
He was unaware of the tragedy, but the
house got considerable joy from .it, I grieve
to say. He scored the hit of his bright
young career when he appeared later, cov-
ered with self-consciousness and a vast pair
of orange-colored bloomers —understudies of
the most obvious description. All the
Follies maidens (who, whatever they may ,be
as actresses, can never be accused of over-
dressing) hung blue silk bags over their lack
of clothes and hurled from them balls of
face-pow r der. The idea would have been
quaite all right if the girls in the front of
the house had got the powder. As it was,
most of the prizes went to soul-less youths
in the orchestra stalls, and they, having no
use for t hem, employed them on the com-
edians after the fashion of vegetables or
eggs. When a lovely young manager
man stepped forward to protest, he got pow-
dered from the shining and level parting of
his hair to the iridescent tips of his shoes.
The girls flung thousands of colored ribbons
into the audience; the stout box-holders
looked balder and the alcoholic ones
more alcoholic than ever; the aesthetic ones
yearned so that they nearly set the O.P.
wings on fire. I could see that the worst
was liable to happen at any moment, and,
intoning a short hymn, I left.
It is a relief to turn from such hectic
scenes to the placid subject of the Gippsland
cow and the Stanley family. The Stanleys
have been installing bush nurses and visit-
ing the lieges down the southern branch of
the line which goes to Port Albert. The
most marked thing about them has been
their reticence. At every little railway sta-
tion the blinds of the vice-regal carriage
were carefully lowered to shut out the gaze
of gaping yokels. At one siding (given up
almost entirely to milkcans and potato-
sacks) a peripatetic motor-cyclist asked the
meaning of it all. The stationmaster, a
gentle maiden of the bush, replied. “Lord
knows; but they say that orl the Heads of
Everywhere are in there.”
A Savage Club concert has been fixed for
Wednesday next in the Melbourne Town
Hall. Object—the Belgian Fund. The
Savages have already started hanging ban-
ners across Collins-street from the Club to
the Hall. Women, ever forgiving, have put
on one side the bitter memories of the past
—none of them have ever been allowed to
put so much as a nose inside the Club —and
are working up the show. The Club reci-
procated recently by standing the workers
a tea-and-ehat party in the upper room of
the Vienna Cafe. Mrs. Arthur Woolcott
took the chair, looking impressive in fawn
color. Mrs. Percy Cook was there in navy
blue, and Mrs. Weston Pett, with the loveli-
est new veil, just reaching to her nose-tip.
and tailorings that had apparently been put
on with a slioe-horn. She is to round up
the programme-sellers. After Mrs. Gordon
Allard and Mrs. W. L. C. Search had been
made committee-women the real work of sell-
ing tickets commenced.
Mrs. Fox, the artist’s mother, aAVoke the
other morning to find herself famous.
Though well into the eighties, she designed
and worked a Avonderful tea-cloth, which
Avas raffled for the Belgian Fund. It brought
in £OO at Gd. a ticket. The old lady thinks
the drain upon public money is “simply
awful,” but is highly jubilant at her
achievement all the same.
Is there a hoodoo sign on the name Car-
negie, or what? The other Sunday a fear-
ful smash occurred betAveen a Nyora coal
train and a Gippsland goods train. The
scene of the calamity was Carnegie. Next
day Carnegie’s music Avarehouse in Mel-
bourne was burned down. The day after
that, as an aged farmer and his Avife from
Dandenong were crossing the line, a coal
train bound from Nyora to Carnegie
smashed into them. It is enough to choke
one off a free library.
Mrs. Bon, of Wappan station, near Mans-
field, the AvidoAV of one of the earliest Vic.
squatters, has sent a motor-ambulance as a
personal present to the Belgian Queen. The
old lady indulges a passion for kings and
queens, and has personal letters from half
the crowned heads of Europe. Her collec-(
tion includes one each from Edward VII.
and Queen Alexandra, as Avell as letters
from George and Mary and the long-de-
parted Victoria. She probably has a feAV
billets-doux from Kaiser Bill, but this is no
time to speak of them. She is an ardent
worker for the dispossessed Aboriginal (she
knows several tribal kings to give blankets
to), and is, furthermore, an energetic
Scawtchbyterian. When the earliest Scawtch
kirk on Eastern Hill was sold to
make a Catholic Convent she rushed
iii to buy the old pulpit and pews.
The nuns insisted on handing them
over as a present. Mrs. Bon took
them up to a small township near her house
and built a church round them.
Lady Helen Fergussssson is despatching
the Bon motor-ambulance to the Queen of
Belgium. It ought to be good for at least
one exalted autograph, perhaps two.
J. S. Horsfall gave £lO,OOO for the build-
ing of a chapel at Trinity in the University
grounds some time ago, and it is noAV nearly
finished. All that is needed are the fittings.
Who’ll give the organ? Wlm’ll give the bell?
Don’l all seize your cheque-books at once.
Good old surgeon O’Hara has offered a
stained-glass windoAV to the memory of his
son —Captain O’Hara, AA’ho Avas killed in
the war. The young man Avas an early
Trinity College student. The window is to
be designed and made by Montgomery.
They say that the Avar has thawed the
glacier which for so long divided the two
branches of one of our first families. Both
have sons in the fighting line, and thus the
reconciliation came about. One of our oldest
and most persistently inaccurate gossips
nearly swooned last Aveek at seeing Mrs.
Montague cross the Capulet threshold at
the dinner hour, and later on emerge one
vast smile and proceed to her Avaiting
chariot arm-in-arm with Mrs. Capulet her-
self.
The awful sameness of modern marriage
Avas leavened on the Riddel Stanley-Hayter
occasion by the Madden dame. Seeing that
the bridegroom’s mother (her cousin) had
been left out of the toast-list, she rose up
and gracefully filled the blank herself. It
would have been even more thrilling had she
sung a song, but it was well enough. People
are grateful for anything in the way of
diversion at a wedding.
The Parkers are going West again. The
father was given a solemn Audedictory by
his pals at the Melbourne Club the other
night, and his pretty girls are already pack-
ing in the recesses of their city flat.
Button Day has arrived, and under vice-
regal patronage too. You buy a button
for a shilling to show your loyalty,
and you are entitled to w’ear it for a
month. Then Button Day recurs, and you
demonstrate that you are still loyal by
spending another bob on another button. It
is. of course, nothing more or less than a
button tax, since no one is going to pervade
the city flaunting a time-expired ticket;
but these sacrifices must be made Avhen the
Empire calls. And if the Empire doesn’t,
there are some wonderfully good imitators
on the stage.
Sub-lieutenant Charles W. Hicks, who is
bound for the Front shortly with the First
Naval Bridging Train, is to marry Miss Doris
Grindrod before he leaves. She is a daugh-
ter of Mrs. A. Tlirelkeld, of HartAvell, Fitz-
roy.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The A.R.C. meeting occurred at A’ictoria Park on
Saturday. It wasn’t what you could fairly call a
howling success from any point of view. A few
Bowmans were there (a Bowman horse captured the
Park Trial Stakes), also Mrs. Allan McFarlane and
her daughter, the R. E. Hopes, Mr. and Mrs. AV. G.
Thomas, the Lister Colleys and Miss Jean Stirling,
Mrs. Seymour Hawker and the Claude Fishers. But
for them, and the public, the function Avould have
been a fiasco.
On the same day a Polls’ Bazaar was held at the
Kyffin Thomas home at Rose Park. It \\'as worth £64
to the Belgian Fund.
While on the subject of Funds, the Brewster-
Jones concert transpired in the evening. Belgian
Consul Herbert Phillips attended, and the hall was
well tilled at prices ranging from 7s. 6d. downwards.
Mrs. H. It. Dutton accompanied in a lovely black
garment that smacked of Paris as the latter used to
smack before Worth and all the rest of the dress-
experts joined the army.
It will be unsafe to get ill before long. Dr. Cud-
more, Dr. de Crespigny, Dr. E. A. Russell and Dr.
L. A. Hayward have all been appointed to the Double
General Hospital, England; Dr. Cunningham and Dr.
Yeatman have got their captaincies on the staff of
the No. 1 General Hospital, Egypt; Dr. Morris, of
Port Adelaide, is to leave with the Naval Bridging-
Brigade ; and there are others.
A rifle club has been formed to withstand the hos-
tile invader, with Premier Vaughan as patron, D. J.
Gordon as president, and a carefully-selected list of
vice-presidents that gives representation to all the
newspapers. ..
Before the Governor left for Sydney on Tuesday
he made a personal appeal to some rich men, who
are above the age for active service, for funds to
buy 10 or 12 motor ambulances. Ten days later he
cabled Kitchener that he could purchase seven ambu-
lances, and the funds Avould follow ; also, that there
are more of the vehicles to come. /
Errol Monk, whose father is in the Register office,
has been made a sub-lieutenant in the R.N. He was
in the merchant service when the war broke out. His
first job for the Empire Avas to act as second officer
of a crew sent to take a German prize from East
Africa to England.
The learned professor wlio has just com-
pleted bis first matrimonial experiment in-
tends shortly to make another. The subject
of it plays at concerts, and Avas recently at-
tached to the Marshall Hall staff. June is
mentioned as the month in which the new
venture will be made.
Miss Gwen Cole, the Coroner’s daughter,
is to marry Charles Naples Brown, of Gee-
long. Appropriate, isn’t it, when you come
to. thing of the sort of place that Geelong is
said to be?
An Irishman in the Melbourne Telegraph
department has struck a neAV idea in raising
money for the Belgians. He thought of a
sixpenny fete day at the Zoo, and when the
children hear of it they’ll make it gee. The
possibilities for extra attractions are im-
mense. Thus the oldest and tamest lion
might be clothed in a Union Jack and put
up to auction, the final purchaser to have
the right of presenting the beast back to the
trustees. There’s a lot of money in it.
Besides, everybody ought to be compelled
to go to the Zoo once in a way.
J. A. Lyons, the Tasmanian Treasurer,
gets himself wedded on Wednesday to a Miss
Burnell, a scholastic Burnie girl. This
event reduces the unmarried members of
Tas. Assembly to tAVo, both Labor members.
One is Martin, the smallest man in the
House, who wears a clean shave over a
County Cork upper lip; the other is Giblin,
the biggest man in the House, Avho deco-
rates himself with a black beard and the
mild expression of a pirate. Giblin is also
addicted to jiu-jitsu and mathematics.
May Grice, of the big East Melbourne
and Western Port family, has just been
married in England to one Aubrey Wilson.
No definite information about him has
reached me thus far, except that he is. going
to the war. But then Avho isn’t? lie is
alleged, by the way, to be a nepheAy of
Admiral Bridges.
The approaching matrimony of a vdry
well-favored pair is announced. Herbert
Spry is to marry Margaret Ashfold, an East
St. Kilda girl, and a wedding trip to Eng-
land is projected.
Tommy Cochrane’s brother lias got a com-
mission in the Natal Light Horse, -And
writes stirringly of his adventures in south-
west Africa. His captain is young Ggar,
also formerly of Victoria. Gear’s uneTfi
was rich old Cook, of the Upper House hiim
Toorak. Tommy Cochrane himself is toiling
at Dalgety’s in the troopship line of busi-
ness, which explains his prolonged absence
from the social firing-line. In his infre-
quent spare moments, Avhich mostly occur
at midnight when the troopships have been
tucked up and put away till morning, he.
has written a play, and the Reps, have
promised to produce it later on. lie
appeared for three nights in the recent
Pinero playlet at St. Peter’s Hall.
Melba is in great distress. Her old
butler died last Friday. He Avas faithful
but deaf, and had looked after Melba for
many years. She loved him, though she
Avas obliged to imperil her melodious voice
to make him hear, and her visitors had to
give an imitation of a thirsty man pouring
the contents of a bottle down his throat.
The sweet girls of Melbourne fail to rea-
lise the conspicuosity of the Caulfield toilet
room. xlll that the rakish bookie, his
radish clerk, and the Avicked men Avho sur-
round them on the turf below have to do if
they wish to see our noblest and best apply-
ing the poudre de riz and sundries is to .look
up (with or without binoculars) at the 'top
of the grandstand. To -say that they do it
constantly is merely to mention that they
are racing enthusiasts.
Maoriland, the country that gets up at
2 a.m. to clean the tourists’ boots, has dis-
covered that a European war is not such a
bad thing after all. The restless Australians
(there are about 5000 of them every year)
have not been able to go to the usual haunts
this year, so they have gone to M.L., Avliere
the hotel porters are used to taking tips
from trout-fishing noblemen and peers Avho
have temporarily broken loose from their
moorings.
One of our very smartest women survived
a severe strain on her concert mannahs at
this entertainment. Just as Parker began
to sing an encore she dropped her Fright-
fully Expensive ermine stole on the dusty
door. She gasped for a moment, then pulled
herself bravely together and left it. there
until the song Avas ended. The Empire is
all right Avliile Ave have women of this
resolute stamp to mother our boys.

Hannah Scott, of Tasmania —a sister of

Lipton’s famous steam yacht, the Erin, will
transport them to one of those ridiculous
Adriatic harbors, hunger for which on the
part of Seiwia Avas among the excuses for the
present war.
Nicholas Joy and an amateur company
played “The Marriage of Convenience” at
the Athenaeum Hall one night last week,
and handed over the proceeds to the Purple
Cross Service. The Purple Cross looks
after the welfare of horses, and is under the
guidance of Mrs. Monash, Miss Harvie and
Mrs. Theosophical Hunt. The somewhat
stuffy hall was packed at 3s. 6d. a time.
You can work out for yourself how many
miles of bandages for Avounded gee-gees this
represents.

It looks very much as though Viscount
Crichton, who visited Australia as a tall
soldier in attendance on the Duke of York,
died or was killed after surrendering him-
self to the Germans’ gentle care early in
November. The first report was that he
was among the prisoners; but no subse-
quent word of him reached headquarters,
and a telegram from King George to the
Kaiser, inquiring what had become of
Crichton and another lord who was under-
stood to have surrendered with him, fetched
no reply from that angry potentate. All
things considered, the official announcement
—six months later- —that the big major fell
in action is suggestive of foul play.
“Von Schmidt”: Par. (B. 18/3/Y5) re
Vic. Education Department’s concern as to
which of its officers it should style “Mr.”
and which “Esq.” recalls an incident of
years ago in connection Avith the same De-
partment. In replying to a correspondent
trading under the dignified family title of
Smyth, the Department inadvertently ad-
dressed the -writer as “Mr. .T. Smith.” He
promptly resented this, and indignantly
called attention in another letter to the fact
that he was not “J. Smith” ; but, notwith-
standing the protest, a subsequent communi-
cation from “your obedient servant, J.
W. Venables” —the then Secretary of
the Department—actually repeated the
offence. Swift retribution followed: next
day the culprit received one in the
solar plexus in the shape of a mis-
sive directed to “J. W. Vegetables, Esq.”
It had the desired effect. The local postman
soon delivered something very clearly ad-
dressed to “Justinian Smyth, Esq.,” and he
lived happy ever after.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Cleveland”: Burrowing in tlie ground
for protection is neither new nor confined to the
armies in Europe. The sand whiting of North
Queensland are expert in the art of digging them-
selves in. I was at a N.Q. resort recently, and
fancying a fish breakfast, borrowed a net. Shoals
of whiting abounded. At the first cast I got a splen-
did catch, and started to drag the net ashore; but
when I got there I found’ a haul of two jam-tins, an
old boot and a soft-shelled crab. Tried three more
casts. Each time I saw the net full of fish until I
got it near the beach, but didn’t land even a scale.
Hat down to search myself as to my condition,
physical and mental. Had abstained from fermented
liquors for about four hours; Had not heard a tele-
phone or any war news for a week; the last military
and naval expert had been smothered two days pre-
viously.' While yet a prey to deep and bitter
reflection I saw dozens of pairs of black-ringed e.ves
appear out of the sand at the water’s edge, and
immediately afterwards there was a flurry in the
water, and a shoal of whiting appeared and dis-
appeared, none the worse for its submarine mining.
Scored next timed Spread one net just at the
water's edge, and dragged the other on to it, and
the surprise of the whiting I hauled in was like that
of the politician who finds himself at the bottom of
the poll.
“Yancy”: Could any of you tell me liow
to pasture cows on lucerne? In the Yanco (N.S.W.)
irrigation district the milk manufacturer has a fool
of a habit of trying to stow her hold in record time.
The result is an abdominal distension of such mag-
nitude that if you are not prompt to let out a hole
in her belt the lucerne, to say nothing of the cow,
is wasted. I have heard that if you put a bit in
the beast’s mouth it acts as a brake on her chewing
capacity. What reliance can a bloke put in that
tale?

“Bad Australian” (from the Andes)
J hope I am in time to say two words about the
modern revolver. As one who has used the plaything
for many years for other purposes than perforating
the amorous tom cat, I say the Colt lays over all
kinds of automatic pistols in that it is practically
a non-jamber, and even on the rare occasions when
a shell does jamb the next pull brings the cylinder
round and looses off another projectile, whereas an
automatic which jambs through any cause whatever
is not worth a plumber’s conscience until it has been
to the armory ; and meantime even a cave-man with
merely a club would have no trouble in extinguishing
the innocent coot who had stacked his chips on the
automatic. .1 have tried most of the automatic con-
traptions, and the almost invariable result has been
jambing, occasionally by the mechanism going wrong,
and frequently through a swollen or fractured shell
sticking in the barrel. The absolute necessity for
ejecting the whole shell immediately after each shot
is what makes the automatic an uncertain proposi-
tion. When playing for keeps with a Colt a jambed
shell is of little importance; but with the auto—-
Que (Hallo? As to accuracy and range, honors are
fairly even between the Colt and the best automatics;
but that is about all that can be fairly said in favor
of the auto. South America holds a very heavy
percentage of the world’s gunmen, in the strictly
business, sense of the term (not the flickergraph
gunman; but|:the out-and-out “bad man”), and in
my nine years’-- wanderings over this continent 1
have found this type of tough going nap on the
Colt almost without exception. The rare exception
was usually in favor of the Smith and AVesson of
exactly similar type. The same must be said of
the South American .Tolm Hop, who is about the
handiest Hop in existence when it comes to the
short-gun.
* * * *
“Barbwire”: How far inland have you
seen seagulls? On the Salt Lakes at Dynevor Downs,
near Thargomindah (Q.), we’ve had mobs of both the
small and large variety; and during rough weather
on the coast I have noted the small variety along
the bore drains in the Warrego District. In the
latter locality, however, the birds never settled, and
after dipping into the water for a day or two dis-
appeared.
* * * *
Two Bananaland fishermen had hard
morale lately on their north coast. They collected
in their net a groper weighing, they estimated,
2501 b. The struggle to secure him was stupendous,
but eventually the beast was tied up to the landing
place with the only piece of rope at hand. AVhile
he raged and used all the fish curses he could recol-
lect his captors went oif for more rope, and their
return drove him to extra fury. AVith a. mighty
wrench he broke his tether .and dashed oft’ in a
torrent of foam and language from all concerned.
The fishermen reckoned he was worth £3 at Chatters
Towers.
“Tekelo”: The law in Hie Islands runs:
“Boys must he paid in cash.” One boss who had
nine boys and only one pound squared his account
of £9 with them this way. Boy No. 1 was handed
the pound, and simultaneously the glories of the
trade-room were revealed to him. He spent the
quid. Boy two was then invested with the sove- .
reign, and the trade store scored again. Seven
more times the coin went out, only to be engulfed
in turn by the store. And everybody seemed quite
satisfied. I know the boss was, because 1 happen to
be him.
* * «
“Blacklip”: “Boronia’s” poisonous “<-ob-
bler”-fish (B. 4/3/’l5) is mentioned by Seville Kent
in his work on the Great Barrier Reef. Up there it
is known as the “stone”-fisli, and amongst some abo.
tribes as the “bullerow” (won’t
swear to the spelling). Its average
length is Gin. ; color, liver and
dirty drab; piebald marked; shape,
not unlike the common eatable flat-
head ; head, hideously corrugated
and indented; eyes, small and
deeply inset. One hand-like fin on
each side is spiked. On the spine
are six spikes, finer than any needle,
about fin. long, erectible at will,
enclosed in a filament sack and
containing a transparent, viscid
fluid. When alarmed the stone-fish
works itself down into tlie sand
until its body disappears and then
onlimbers its poisonous spikes.
Should the unfortunate intruder be
a barefooted human the contact is
electrical and atrocious. The
agony adds interest to all of 24
hours at intervals. I’ve had to hold
a black boy down while his
paroxysms of pain-madness lasted;
and, although I can’t vouch for it,
the story goes ihat a child pad-
dling in Brisbane Itiver died after
being perforated. The only cure
(if available) knowil to -me is used
by northern aborigines. The injured part is" scarified
and common wood-gum dropped or placed burning
hot, or a piece set fire to, on the wound.

“Outcast”: I don’t know if goitre is a
commpxy complaint with native mountaineers all the
world* over, bu|, jt is frequent in several hill dis-
tricts fhatsTve yisited in Papua. In., the Upper Vetapu
regions I haVe seen swellings as large as yams, and
the complaint is very prevalent. The name of one
of the local villages is itself a vernacular word in-
dicating goitre. In the hill country on the Upper
Kemp Welch I found goitre to be exceedingly com-
mon, especially among the women. Native thera-
peutics ascribe the complaint to “something in the
water,” and I believe there is some foundation for
this. The Swiss peasant women, who are great
sufferers, blame the carrying of heavy weights on
the head in their country.

“Yarrum”: The old engine-driver ex-
plained what he did when something got into his
eye. lie; rubs the oilier eye and blows his trunk to
dislodge the irritant. I use castor-oil. Once, camped
at La Perouse, I was? taking my company down on
the flat for parade, when a shell, thrown from Manly
or somewhere, struck near the monument, and sent
•lewt. of sandy soil into my left telescope. The
cadets got none of it. I sped off to the German
doctor. lie produced a silk handkerchief, and pro-
spected for the earth, hut couldn't get a color. Then
he told me of the oil ; but he hadn’t any about him.
However, I got the lubricant, which did the' trick,
and I’ve used nothing else since.
* * *
“Lagani-Namo”: All Papuan drums are
more or less shaped like an hour-glass, but they vary
considerably in size. The mountaineers make' the
best dancing drums. These are seldom more than
12in. x 3in., and weigh about lOoz. I have heard
them tum-tumming at a distance of several miles.
The finest native drums, though, that I have seen
are made by the cannibals of the Fly and Bamu
Rivers. These are of some dense blackwood (not
ebony), about 40in. long by 16in. at end. The
deep booming throb seems to jerk out from the
mouth of these drums as though it were a pro-
jectile. Here’s the recipe for making them: A block
of wood is first roughly tomahawked into shape,
(hen stood on end so that the maker can keep a
tiny hardwood coal aglow by blowing through a
narrow reed or bamboo tube. When one end has
been charred down as far as the narrowed middle,
the other is turned up and sit|ijlavly .dealt, with..
The re onancy of 'the (Irani“depends, of’S’ccWrse, on
the smoothness and proportioning of its interior,
l.vrn a chip too much out of the neck, between the
chambers, makes a noticeable, difference in ihe tone.
Inside and outside are made smooth with a piece of
shell or glass. The cured skin of a gohanna or
snake is then damped, stretched, over one,, end, and
cemented around with a mixture of rubber-tree sap
and gum. A very ordinary drum will take 50 or
60 hours in ihe making.

“Boronla”: To what height has maize
been known to sprout? Often the splendid cow-furni-
ture surveys the Westralian landscape from an alti-
tude of 14ft., and the other day I saw a crop on
one of the rich swamps on the fringe of the Donelly
plains, defiantly waving its whiskers 19ft. 6in. in
the atmosphere. Is this a record?
‘‘.1.8.5.”: “C.F.E.” (B. l/4/’l5) asks lif
the betel-nut and betel-leaf are related. The t etel-
leaf plant is a i limber, and is related to the pepper
plant. The arcca nut and the betel nut are
-different species of a palm. I have seen tooth paste
made from the areca nut, but can’t say if it has any
other virtue than as a deodoriser.

AN EAGLE FLEW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An eagle flew
Where the fading blue
Of the sky sloped into a sunlit west,
With a slow, strong flight,
As the coming night
Cast a purple pall o’er the mountain’s crest.
And a fluttering, clattering,
Squeaking, chattering
.Bat burst out of the glooming bush;
In furious rout,
It chased about,
And insects caught in frenzied rush.
And it seemed, as I sat,
That the noisy bat
Saw naught but the bush, and the gnats, and
me;
But the eagle, high
- In the western sky,
Saw a darkening earth and a golden sea.
N.S.W.
J. H. RAMSAY.

HOW TO HAVE LONG,
BEAUTIFUL HAIR.
By a Hair Specialist.
It is not hard to stop the hair from falling
out and promote its growth if the right
means are used. There is no hope for the
scalp where the hair-roots are dead and the
scalp is shiny. However, to those who have
not reached this condition, immediate steps
should be taken to stop it before it is too
late. Here is a simple recipe which you can
make at home, that will stop the hair from
falling out, promote its growth and eradicate
scalp eruptions and scalp humors. To a
half-pint of water add loz. bay rum, a small
box of Orlex Compound and -]oz. of glycer-
ine, and apply to the hair, rubbing with the
finger-tips, two or three times a week. These
ingredients can be obtained at any chemist’s
at very litttle cost and mixed at home. This
recipe not only promotes the growth of the
hair, but darkens streaked, faded grey hair
and makes it soft and glossy. C 9

X.P.D.”: The advocacy by “Vigilans” (B.
l/4/’l5) of Beckett's idea to train northern Binghis
for home defence prompts me to offer further evi-
dence of the general handiness <jf these part-Malav
people. _ On Murray Island, Torres Strait, the school
for native boys and girls is self-supporting. The
youngsters peg away at ordinary school subjects for
half the day, and put in the other half at growing
paw-paws, cocoanuts, etc., the marketing of which
pays for the teacher and school material. One of
the main sources of income of Yarrabah mission
station, near Cairns, is—or was—the beche-de-mer
and other sea-harvests gathered by about half a
dozen of the younger men and bigger boys, with the
able assistance of a small sailing craft. *
* * *
“Frank”: I was going through the heart
of the drought-stricken Malice the other dav and
when near Curvo, on the big level stretch, I saw one
of the “travelling farms” approaching—a caravan a
mob of horses as poor as wood, a sleepy
dog, and the boss, a young fellow. The
horses were just able to crawl along. It
put a lump in your throat. But not for long.
Coming level, you saw that the side of the cart
bore the inscription: “Starved out—God bless the
Malice’; and underneath that: “Housemaid wanted—
apply within.” You simply can’t crush some of
these chaps.

“Levuka”: If it’s rock-rod on a large scale
that anybody’s looking for let him take his tackle
to Fiji. In the native market at Suva the other
day one was on view that has my backing as the
record. It measured just on 7ft. long, was about
6ft. in girth and weighed 3361 b. And if anybody
calls me a German diplomat when I sav the marine
bullock was caught on a line, don’t let him do it in
my hearing.

SHE: “They issued 150 wedding invitations."

Quill : Visited a town in the droughty
part of Victoria the other day, and found on the
wall of the bedroom allotted mo at the pub an
illustrated placard: “Instructions for restoration of
the apparently drowned,” Had a bath in a pannikin
of water, and got a restorative over the bar.

“Devilfish” : According to a yarn I heard
at Burnie (Tas.) the other day, a Bass Strait octopus
played a part very like that attributed by Victor
Hugo to the English Channel variety. Thev arc
building a breakwater at Burnie,
and divers have the job of going
down in from 40 to 50 feet of
water to scratch a bole in the
bottom for the 60-ton blocks of
concrete to rest in. A diver in
full working- rig is such an in-
edible-looking- object that the
most ferocious of octopi might be
expected to hesitate before attack-
ing him. But his hands and
wrists are unprotected. While a
diver was busy near the pump
used to clear the sand away from
the foundations, an octopus got him
by both hands and twined its ten-
tacles tightly round them. The
diver was in a pretty fix, for they
didn’t expect him on top for a
long time, and lie could not pull
the communication cord to let
them know he wanted to come up
in a hurry. With the fish getting
a firmer and yet firmer grip of
his hands he had an idea, and
pushed the brute up against the pump. The pump
21 2 ? SOt the oct °P us out of the
shaft they found its arms measured 7ft. 6in. from
tip to tip.

“N.”: The usual nose ornament in Papua
is a piece of pearl or tortoiseshell or a short cylin-
der of bamboo, but in the ears great bunches of
colored shells ( sapi-sapi), tails of pigs, or cus-cus,
are worn. The Solomon Islanders beat the Papuans
out of sight with the variety of their decorations.
It is quite common to see a boy’s earlobes tied
together beneath his chin when he is engaged in
anything strenuous, or is travelling through the
scrub. I have seen lobes that could be stretched
around articles up to 44in. in diameter. Tortoiseshell
or wooden discs exquisitely inlaid with mother-of-
peail aie the usual furniture for ears, but pannikins,
bottles, candles or any old thing are used. One
artistic native whom I had just paid for six months’
work invested in two alarm clocks, and hung one
in each flapper.
Ilie Reefer ’: In Papua the natives’ fancy
lightly turns to murder. For instance, a boy
•rained his tottering grandmother with a stone
club simply because a child crying in the adjoining
hut had annoyed him. It relieved his feelings, and
he had no use for a grandmother, anvway. Another
murderer excused himself to the Judge this way:
I had to kill this feller for all-e time he talk,
talk, talk, talk too much.” Out of 187 arraign-
ments dealt with by the Papuan Criminal Court last
year 97 were for murder, 11 for manslaughter and
nine for rape. The balance ranged from forgery to
e stealing of corpses for sorcery purposes. Since
then three white men have been butchered. Some
years since one of the high Administrative panjan-
i rums opined that “a white man can now go from
ore end of the Territory to the other with a walk-
ing-stick.' My walking-stick is going to be a .32
magazine rifle for a long time to come.
Paddy-M.’ : “Cambridge’s” par (B.
25/3/’l5) about some horse disease in Tasmania
being identical with the Birdsville disease is all
mush His verdict is that the disease is caused bv
ie bot-fly. Kittens! I’ve humped my knot up

‘Wal”: The Papuan highlanders are not
unlike their cousins of Scotia in their great faith
in charms and dreams. Rambling in the Owen
Stanley Range recently I dropped across a youth who
lad been shot through the knee some years before
and oil asking him how he treated the puncture lie
in ormec me that he tattooed a charm-sign around
the wound, and the gods made it well again. He
was hit in the centre of the cap, but with all his
rough surgery the knee was only slightly stiff, and
beyond a limp he was as good a tramper as any-
body. He informed me that the same pattern was
tattooed on a man’s head if he was sunstruck, and
it always acted like a white man’s medicine.
as'ani-N.” : livery new division opened
np in Papua has something quite unique to show us
When Beaver explored the Girara swamps between
the Fly and Bamu rivers last year, he was trans-
ported in a canoe 70ft. long and 3ft. 9in. in beam
hewn out of solid cedar. The prow was carved to
resemble a crocodile, and other carvings extended
from end to end along the outside. In one of the
genemas (married men’s lodges) there, he saw huge
figures of crocodiles and iguanas depicted as swallow-
ing humans, and in the part hollowed out for the
belly lay four human heads. These effigies were
carved out of wood, and have some ceremonial use.
the last magistrate who ventured thereabouts with
a squad of armed native police was attacked while
rafting along a waterway. One arrow went through
his hat another into his left shoulder, one into his
oot, and another through his right forearm. There
were 57 arrows stuck into the raft. The return
civilities were the death of 10 attackers.
“Broombus.h” : Carrying coals to Newcastle
seems no less absurd than carrying water to Murray
Bridge; yet in this dreadful year it has been a
matter of every-day occurrence. The river has ceased
to flow, and the sea water lias encroached inwards
as ar as Mannum. Water for railway engines at
Murray Bridge, on the Adelaide-Melbourne line, is
supplied from a fresh patch in the river above
Mannum. A barge, fitted with valves in the bot-
tom, is towed to the fresh patch; the valves are
then opened, and the freight is allowed to run in
up to within a foot of the top. The barge is then
towed down to Murray Bridge, where the cargo is
pumped out, and so the weary business goes on. The
strange thing about the fresh-water patch is that it
has salt water on the up-stream side as well as the
down-stream side. Can any potamologists explain?

jhe Wanderer ’: “Hare’s” recipe for dam-
pei (B. 1 /4/’ 15) lacks the raising ingredients. The
old hand takes what he considers sufficient flour for
the job, a small quantity of cream of tartar, half
a small quantity of baking-soda and a pinch of salt.
-Mix the lot thoroughly (dry) ; then add sufficient
water to make a doughy mixture that won’t gallop
while you're raking out the coarse ashes. The fine
art is to get the concoction, after damping, into the
ashes as quickly as possible. Cremate for about one
hour, and if the product isn’t satisfactory blame the
flour. line gidyea or box ashes are easy winners for
open-air cookery.

) em U P again, old man,” I said, as he backhanded

wife lately,” he added. He gulped down the rem-
nant and slouched moodily out to the dray. It
was tme, too and both losses had been replaced in
the order of their importance.

THE QUARREL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Annie King was a little bit “on,”
Mrs. 801 l was a little bit “off,”
And the latter when partially “gone”
Had a mean disposition to scoff.
So she tackled her hostess, poor thing—■
In the vehement course of her tale
She declared Mrs. King
Had no right to her ring,
And had lots of relations in gaol.
Then arose Annie King in her pride,
And she squarely confronted her guest.
Setting lady-like weakness aside,
She assailed her with eloquent zest.
Maiw 801 l she declared was a thief
Whose pa had been properly lynched;
She asserted, in brief,
How it passed her belief
That the dame had escaped being “pinched.”
Mary 8011, with alarm in her eyes,
Fled the verbal assault, but in vain;
lor a full fifteen minutes the cries
_ of her neighbor infected the lane.
From no epithet sinners might use,
From no crime Mrs. B. was exempt.
Then Ann made her adieus:
“I despise such as yous—•
I jest treats yell with silent contempt!”

Business, Bobbery, etc.
“Moana” : The pirate who says lie is mak-
ing £r>ooo a year when lie is really making
£.'171 19s. Id., and on that representation
succeeds in soiling out lo the usual mug for
£S0()0, got it in the neck at Wellington
(ALL.) the other day. The gentleman who
received the axe sold for £1750 a business
whose average profits had, he said, been
£l5OO per annum. When the simpleton paid
his money and climbed inside he quickly saw
he’d been had, and when he stumbled across
copies of the income-tax returns for the last
two years and found that one gave the profit
as £415 and the other as £530, he heaved in
a writ for £2500 damages for piracy on the
high seas. The pirate, the Lord knows why,
defended the action and struck trouble. Ilis
income-tax returns and the Income Tax
officials were brought into court. The latter
stated that they had delved into the books
and assessed the pirate’s income for the
previous two years at £415 and £530. and
that the pirate had protested violently and
there had been a big controversy because he
considered these assessments too high. That
landed him between the Devil and the cold
ocean ; for if he proved that his income had
been £l5OO the Income Tax peonle would
fine him heavily for sending in a false re-
turn and smite him with treble tax, and if
he couldn’t prove it the simpleton whom he
had taken in would get damages. It was a
rotten position, and the Judge ended it by
awarding the simpleton £1750.
Maoriland is going to have a shot at
making paper from wood pulp. A company
with plenty of confidence in the future has
just obtained a lease of 30,000 acres of bush
at Otira. It has also obtained water rights
and has acquired a suitable site for power-
house, mill and other buildings. Recent
experiments have proved the suita-
bility of the Otira timber for making pulp,
so there is every prospect of Maori land being
able to print the terms of peace on paper
made out of local chips.

“Bill Bowyang”: “Gator” in B. 1/4/T5
wants to know if there is a market for alli-
gator skins in Australia. There is not.
Some years back Lee and Sons, who used
to follow the unique profession of capturing
alligators in the Johnstone and Tully
rivers (N.Q.), cured several fine skins and
sent them to Sydney and Melbourne, where
they hoped to sell them to trunk makers,
etc. No one bought, and the skins were
returned. Later on, the Lees tried to find a
market in Germany, but were informed
there was more demand for the imitation
article, and that when Sauerkraut did use
the real thing he procured it from America.
There is now and then an opportunity in
Australia to sell the great lizards for show
purposes. The Lees have received as much
as £7O for a large specimen landed in Mel-
bourne.
“Chug”: The outlook for the canary is
gloomier than ever. Seed supplies usually
come from Turkey, Morocco, Spain, Hol-
land and the Argentine. Germany has used
up all the Dutch seed. France, England
and other countries have depleted the
Spanish and Moroccan stocks. The small
Argentine crop keeps the birds singing in
the United States, and is all absorbed.
Turkey alone remains as a source of supply,
and Turkey isn’t feeling like feeding canaries
just now. So the Gerhog may be said to
have brought down another victim —the
little bunch of fluff and feathers that sings
in the cage.
“The Old Man”: After months of war,
there are still German toys for sale in Lon-
don. A little clockwork clown has just
arrived in our household from the darkened
city by the Thames. The German who
wrote the “Directions for Using” did vio-
lence to the hated language in this fashion:
When the figure stops standing upright turn, for
putting it in the cartoon, only the key of the clock-
work, mind pushing the upper part of the body hv
force down.
Still, though the grammar may be rocky,
the Huns have collected the money, and the
translator probably makes himself under-
stood with the cartridges he bought with the
proceeds.

“Gas Escape”: What with the war and
the drought and the price of beer going up,
kapok costs about three times what it did a
few months ago. Now there is a local beast
called physiantlius by those who can pro-
nounce it—l call it moth plant myself—
which yields an exact imitation. Moth
plant is a creeper, and rather an ornament.
It has a sweet-scented white flower, whose
principal occupation is catching moths. The
flowers are succeeded by large gourd-like
pods, which burst when ripe and display
a creamy white fluff exactly resembling
kapok, and equally good for pillow stuffing.
I have tested it. The small black seeds
inside tlie gourd can be separated from the
fluff without much trouble. The plant is
easy to cultivate. It seems to like stony
ground as much as anything else. I have
it growing wild in m,y fowlyard. It seems
to me that there is a chance of a local indus-
try here. Machines used for cleaning seeds
from cotton could probably be adapted. I
don't know whether the fibre can be spun,
but there would be a pretty large demand
even for the fluff.

small Melbourne jeweller who is making a

wards. Propriety is the mother of inven-
tion.
* * *
South Aus. has now reckoned up its last
wheat-harvest. It reaped under a bushel
and a half per acre, 1.41 to be exact. This
is the worst since records began to be- taken,
GO years back. Very nearly half of the
land sown with wheat was not reaped at
all. Areas far north and far west mostly
averaged under a bushel. Mount Gambier
district, on the other hand, surprised itself
by going over 10. Others were astonish-
ingly varied and patchy. The total yield
was 34 million bushels, as against 17 the
year before, and over 20 previously. This
directly reflected the rainfall, under 114
inches in Adelaide for the year, and a
trifle over seven (or half the average) all
over the agricultural areas. Still, the
finest April rains for years having directly
followed the election of a Labor Ministry
-—which fact makes pious South Aus. very
thoughtful—there will be a great scramble
for seed-wheat and another gamble, with
Providence.

“A.H.J.”: A furnishing house makes a
special window show of Oriental carpets.
Good ! One of these is ticketed 40 guineas,
and in case the carpet ‘ fails to strike the
taste of Mrs. Money-Bagges a show card
presents this diffusive information
It takes six natives six months to make one of
these carpets. They are carried 200 miles by
caravan or camel to the coast arid are taken by
surf boat to the agent's steamers. Eight in evert/
10 carpets are damaged or lost in these small surf
boats. They are then taken to headquarters at
Smyrna and thence resliipped to us.
I don’t know anything about the cost of
dragging carpets across a desert by camel
team, nor of the cost of the various tran-
shipments, nor of fishing damaged carpets
out of the surf, but I have a vague idea
that in this case the customer is informed
that 40 guineas represents not the value of
the carpet but the cost of making and haul-
ing over a sandy waste of 200 miles on the
hump of a smellful quadruped four other
carpets which get lost on the way. The
view that it were better to give employment
to white men in Australia than to other
colored persons and camels in some remote
corner of the earth may not appeal to Mrs.
Money-Bagges, but paying for four lost car-
pets in order to get one may strike her hus-
band as being rather dubious business.

In Berlin to-day no man is accused of neglecting
bis duty to Germany who puts in his time voicing
bitter hatred for the English.
—„l mar lean correspond ent.
He leant upon the spacious wall
Of Berlin’s greatest bank,
A beery cit. in view of all.
They took him for a crank —
lie did not talk, he never howled,
But leaned just so, and darkly scowled,
And goodly Germans paused to vow

lie still was on the job.
A local peeler in his might

“Ach, ged a moot', you ’beat!” lie cried.

”1 ain'd vos loafing,” said the sot —
