VICTORIA.
BILLY T.
MYSKLE AT THE BOOKSELLER’S.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ido not drink or gamble—l only dissipate with
books.
Every now and then the hunger comes upon me;
And I hurry along to the bookseller s.
There are books heaped on tables and more books
piled to the ceiling;
Books by old men and young men and fools and
flappers; }
Books on philosophy and engineering and exploration
and love —
Especially books t on love.
Men climb up long ladders and peer at books through
spectacles;
Other men go down on their knees and grope in dark
cupboards for books
Books large and dusty and very small books with
gilt edges.
I go amongst the books and turn their pages and
peer at their backs;
It gives me a strange pleasure just to take them up
and put them down again.
1 would not be allowed to do the same thing in a
butcher’s shop,
Or a grocer’s,
Or a chemist’s.
Someone would linger round me, and ask me if I
wanted to buy something.
But everything is different in a bookseller’s.
I am allowed to wander where I will, and do what I
like.
I turn over the stock and put it back again.
I wander from exploration to civil engineering,
From electricity to poetry, and from fiction to
theology.
I look at new books and second-hand books, at pub-
lishers’ remainders and dead men’s libraries.
I do not know what I want, or if I really want it.
I do not even know if I can afford it.
I have books at home that 1 have no time to read.
I also have books at my office that I have no time to
read.
But I have an idea that I want more books.
And more books.
My hands are busy amongst the books, but my
mind is a blank.
I love the books piled to the ceilings!
I love the books heaped on the floor!
1 love the books that I cannot see !
They are all like beautiful, mysterious women!
I probably would not understand half of them !
The other half would probably bore me!
Already I spend too much time inside reading!
And too much'time writing!....
I look round at the books —the heavy, unrecessary
books!
How disgusting!. How superfluous thee are!
What blatant brutes their authors must be!
The philosophers are probably mere buzzers!
Lots of them are dead, and lots of them ought to
be dead!
I think I would like to hang a few authors!
And a few publishers!
I would like to hang them closely so they would
obscure their shelves of rotten books !
It dawns -upon me that 1 should not have come into
this charnel-house.
I leave the place in disgust, and walk quickly away
down the street.
I have bought nothing.
1 have stolen nothing.
But I have had my debauch of books !
Is it not immoral!
M. KODAK (Paris).

Ex-Premier Jimmy McGowen's elevation
to the N.S.W. Leg. Council, along with the
job of handling Government business in that
Chamber, was a strong tip as soon as it was
known that Fred Flowers was to follow
Suttor as President. But McGowen s health
is still bad, and the family doctor put his foot
down. So someone else must argue the point
with the Old Men and move the frequent
adjournment.
“X.P.D.”: That par. (B. 1/4/T5) about
Robert McNab’s drivings for the record
buried by Du Fresne after he bad taken pos-
session of Maoriland as a French colony in
1772 reminds me of a similar quest on Pos-
session Island, in Torres Strait. According
to Cook’s journals, it was here that be, a
few weeks after the Endeavor’s close call
on tlie reef outside Cooktown, went ashore
and took possession of all territory lying
between latitude 38deg. south and lOdeg.
30min. south, lie. being then satisfied, after
passing .through the narrow waters at the
top of Gape York Peninsula, that Australia
was an island. The records refer to a cop-
per cylinder containing papers having been
buried on the island. The disinterment was
financed by a syndicate of Thursday Island
people, and every part of the place to which
Cook’s description could apply was dug over.
But the cylinder was never found, and the
presumption is that it was taken away by
Malays or fell to pieces in the campfire
when Binghi tried to cook shellfish in it.
About bores of one kind and another:
The “City of the Plains,” at the Far Western end
of Queensland’s Northern line, has the hottest Mow-
ing bore in Australia, if not the world. 'the
temperature at the surface is 210deg\, or only two
below boiling point. It is likewise the deepest ser-
viceable spouter. The town is reticulated from this
supply, which comes up from a depth of nearly
5000 ft. The bagmen make the neighborhood their camp-
ing ground. There tliey don’t have to worry about
: fuel. They attach their corned junk to a wire
hook, suspend it under the overflow pipe, and the
fodder soon is cooked. The making of the tea is
simpler. Glenariff bore, on Rockwood station, in the
same Western country, is well over 4000 ft. deep, and
took longer to complete than any other bore the same
depth, viz., over 10 years. The “drillers” were fresh
hands on deep holes, having been brought over from
the comparatively shallow oil wells in Petrolia, Can-
ada. They had to learn as they burrowed, and during
tlie process everything except the engine fell to the
bottom. It is easily the most expensive hole of its
size in this continent. It has a small flow of gaseous
water, and by applying a match to the pipe above
the overflow a steady blue flame can be got six to
eight inches in diameter. The water is just like
pure rain water after being allowed to stand some
hours, tlie gases escaping when exposed to the air.
The late Frank Sutter, for so long pre-
sident of the N.S.W. Council, was once
expelled from Parliament House. With his
eldest brother, William Henry, ho had come
to Sydney on a visit. His father was then
a member of the “mixed” —or single—
House. The brothers were accommodated
with seats in the gallery, and \V. 11. induced
F. B. to fire a paper pellet at the bald head
of some legislator sitting below. YV. 11.
arrested the uplifted hand with a laugh
which shocked the Sergeant, who promptly
instructed an attendant to remove the
offenders, and the pair were fired out into
Macquarie-street. The brothers in 1875
obtained seats in the Chamber, though they
always sat on different sides.
One of Tasmania’s best-known con-
tractors got bis start this way. In the ‘7o‘s,
before the Government had an up-to-date
Works Department and a staff that can write
B.C.E. after its name, a carpenter much
addicted to forcible language was employed
as a sort of handy man to do anything the
Minister of Lands required. A bridge was
wanted, and the handy man spent, the best
part of two days making a plan. He com-
pleted it entirely to bis own satisfaction and
showed it with pride to his Minister, a feeble
gentleman, who had been educated for the
Church, but whose health had driven him to
agriculture. The Minister, who was just
beginning to feel his feet, sat back in his
chair and said leisurely, “I don’t think much
of your plan!” This was too much for the
carpenter. “Why, you old potato-digger.”
he said, “you are looking at it upside down.”
The speech got him the sack, and sent him
out to seek and make a fortune in the build-
ing line.

tific purposes.”
A new comet is reported to be roaming
round, about 800,000,000 miles from Can-
berra; but its tail has so far not been
focussed by Australian observers. Also a
public debt of about £800.000,000 is hanging
over the country: and no one lias photo-
graphed the tip of that monkey’s tail either.
From time to time loud complaints are
made about the slowness., of the. Federal
Arbitration Court: yet the Court is greased
lightning compared with some of the parties
who say they want to come before it. The
other day, when it was looking fur work,
none of the parties in a long list were pre-
pared to go on, although the cases were filed
from six months to two years ago. And
nine times out of 10 it is the most dilatory
party that makes the loudest howl" it the
Court doesn’t get to grips with the job the
moment the ease is ready.

Remover.
Those who suffer from corns and hard skin
on the soles of the feet will find it a great
boon to apply a little Atla Resin to the corn,

and a little covering of cotton wool on the

Atla Resin seems to act by getting thor-
oughly into complete contact with the corn,

to act on the surface only. It is certainly
the latest cure for these troublesome and
painful structures, and, according to all re-
ports by those who have used it, one of the
best.* [2

CHEMIST TELLS HOW TO STOP
INDIGESTION.
SAYS USE OF PEPSIN LEADS TO
CHRONIC DYSPEPSIA.
“It is a wonder some of ns have stomachs
left,” remarked a well-known chemist
recently. “While all chemists sell a score or
more of stomach remedies for which there
is a wide demand, most of them are just
pepsin pills, which aid digestion of the food
that is in the stomach at the moment. They
have no curative or strengthening effect on
the stomach at all, and, of course, do not
reach or cure the cause. So the same people
keep on coming here and buying and using
them until they are real chronic dyspeptics.
When anyone really asks my advice I swear
by and recommend ordinary bisurated mag?
nesia, which doesn’t digest the food at all,
but acts as an antacid and sweetens the sour,
fermenting contents of the stomach. That
stops the pain, heartburn, sour rising, wind,
floating, fulness, etc., in just a few minutes,
and the stomach digests its food without help
or trouble, which is the proper way. jt
“Doctors make mistakes sometimes, too.
he continued. “My own aunt had all kinds
of trouble with her stomach for years. She
bought and used several styles of digestive
pills, but got worse right along, as naturally
she would. Finally she went to a doctor,
who nearly scared her to death by telling
her she had cancer of the . stomach. She
came to me with his prescription, and told
me what he said. I thought it was non-
sense. I sent her to another doctor whom I
knew T very well, and he didn’t tell her any-
thing, but just gave her this same thing,
bisurated magnesia. She took it for two
weeks, and never has had any stomach
trouble since, and that’s three years ago.
She’s my own aunt, and I know this for a
fact. Yes, a lot of bisurated magnesia
note the name carefully, as other kinds are
lacking in its peculiarly valuable properties
—is sold in Sydney. All the chemists have
it, I suppose, and all you take is half a tea-
spoonful in a little water after every meal.
It’s all right.”*
POLITICAL
POINTS
Premier Holman explained that the job
of N. S. Wales Agent-General is not pro
tom. —that the lucky winner will be
appointed for two whole years. All the
same, there will be little in it. Coghlan
joined the service as a boy and comes under
the old N.S.W. Superannuation Act. This
gave to every person who kept up his con-
tributions the right to retire at 60 on two-
thirds of his salary. But no other A.-G.
will get this. The N.S.W. Agent-General
(as Agent-General) shares with Federal
Judges and Commonwealth military officers
the disability _of getting no pension—and
nothing excessive in the way of salary.
It is never safe for Davy Hall, the
N.S.W. Attorney-General, to tell his own
story. The other day, after announcing
that he had made £17,000 out of the mostly
drought-stricken farmers whose poor little
wheat harvest he had grabbed at about half
the present market price, he said proudly
that this money belonged to the general
public and would probably be used to
cheapen the mice of the loaf later on. Of
course the wilier Holman, who represents a
farming constituency, saw that that wouldn’t
do; so a new story is to be told presently—
the money is to go to the farmers if there is
enough to give them an extra penny a bushel.
If there isn’t, it is to go to reduce the price
of the seed-wheat and fodder which the State
has been supplying.
The author of “Undiscovered Russia” in-
sists that the black race will eventually
dominate the United States. “What then?”
the Age asks. Uncle Sambo then.
It looks as if the frowsy shirt of sec-
tarianism is to get an airing at the Legis-
lative Council election for Hobart on May
4. Father Travers, of St. Joseph’s, has
been urging his flock to forget the name of
Dwyer Gray on polling day. Dwyer Gray,
one of the Labor candidates, editor of the
local Labor paper, and an R.C. himself,
declines to conform to the wishes of the
Catholic Federation in the matter of a State
subsidy to denominational schools. Hence
the unpleasantness. Archbish. Delany seems
to have called Father Travers over the coals
for mentioning a political candidate’s name.
Next day his Riverence wrote to the papers
admitting hist error, but repeating his pulpit
sentiments with additions. The Mercury
published the letter, and has been writted
by Dwyer Gray for £2OOO for libel. These
happenings are adding some interest to the
election, which is due for May 4, and
threatened to be a dull affair. The candi-
dates are Bond, M.L.C.; Dr. Bottrill, who
lost his deposit at last Assembly elections ;
Crooke (another deposit-dropper) ; and the
two Labor candidates, Dwyer Gray, editor
of the Post, and Martin Ryan, solicitor.
The latter, by the way, was secretary of the
N.S.W. Farmers’ Union 20 years ago. Bot-
trill, Crooke and Ryan are in favor of the
subsidy to denominational schools. The
£3O franchise, plus the sectarian shindy, will
probably make the retention of the seat by
bark-merchant Frank Bond an easy thing.
Premier Peacock having promised to re-
enact liis Price of Goods and Prices of Food-
stuffs Acts before they expire on April 30,
and also to re-appoint his Price of Goods
Board, it is only a fair thing that he should
first explain what use the foolish things
are. Although the Board fixed the whole-
sale price of butter at Is. 2d., and has not
officially raised it. the combined whole-
salers have gradually pushed the price up
to Is. 3d., Is. 4d., Is. 5d., and now to Is.
7d. per lb. The rises may be justified, but
where does the Board come in?
The usual monthly foreign parson arrived
in time to throw mud at the White Aus-
tralia religion before April was 16 days
old
Rev. A. G. Fraser, principal of Trinity College,
Kandy, Ceylon, speaking in the Church Missionary
Society meeting at Melbourne, said that the White
Australia policy would have to be modified largely.
The people of Asia were developing a great national
spirit. .. .and it would be only a very short time
before they began dictating terms. They had many
resources, and were developing them strorgly, ard it
would be a very serious thing if they had to assert
their rights.
Their rights! The rights of a black man
to a home in White Australia! No such
right ever existed, and Fraser of Kandy in
saying it is an impudent guest in the house
of an all-too-tolerant host.
GOOD CAUSE FOR OFFENCE.
THE OLD LADY: “I see the British and
French is startin' to get offensive now, and
I don't blame ’em either, after the way
them Germans ’as been carry in' on."
“Ramassis” : According to James Marion,
the Cold-Tea party in N. S. Wales is going
to follow the lead of South Aus. and have
the beer turned off at six o’clock. A much
more sensible proposal would be to have the
beer restrained during business hours and
turned on for a while in the evening.
Liquor during working hours is a curse.
If there is anything at all in alcohol
—and most people think there is—its busi-
ness is to knit up the ravelled sleeve of
care, and it can get its stitches in best when
the day’s work is done. The .South Aus.
vote suggests that men should be compelled
to neglect their work if they want to drink.
Therefore it is a fool vote.

MRS. BINKS (relieved): “Thank 'Eaven! I
thought it was an ’awk aƒter me chickens."
Various church bodies in the Eastern
States have just been denouncing the
totalisator on the ground that it produces
national demoralisation. The same people
have been sounding the loud timbrel over S.
Australia’s magnificent forward movement
on the liquor question. If the totalisator
is a national demoraliser, what is S. Aus.
—which must be the most demoralised State
of all—doing with a great forward move-
ment? Which is wrong—the parsons’ yarn
about the totalisator or their yarn about S.
Australia’s forward movement? They can’r
have it both ways.
Finlayson, M.11.R., of Brisbane, attacking
the. Federal Government’s wise policy of
nationalising the liquor business in the
Northern Territory, demanded to be told
whether it was for this the great Common-
wealth was formed —to paint its coat-of-
arms on an hotel? While he is contemplat-
ing the degradation of a great agency being
used for such a trivial purpose as the reduc-
tion of crime and suffering, Finlayson might
inquire whether it was for this that the
great British Empire was founded —to put
its coat-of-arms on a “Black Maria”? Also
the idea of Finlayson’s great City of Bris-
bane being interested in a slop-cart no doubt
fills him with unspeakable grief. Still the
vehicle is not likely to be abolished on that
account.
The acuteness of Andrew Fisher’s disap-
pointment over the inability of Bill Massey,
who reigns over Maoriland by
a brown and insecure majority
of one, to attend the Premiers’
Conference, causes The Bulle-
tin to suspect that something
in the way of a joint p.n. may
have been in the incubator.
Massey seems to have reckoned
it safest to keep tight grip on his
elusive savior Henare and do his
own pawning.
“Rags” : I have just finished
opening up a load of stuff from
_■ Sydney warehouse. Included
in it is a line in great demand
among Australian unionists’
wives, because it is cheap—and
nasty. A little label bearing in
very small type “Made in Ger-
many,” formerly adorned the
packet. Now the same article,
in the same packet, bears the
words “Made in Holland.”
Everybody in the trade knows
that goods of this description
were never brought from Hol-
land before the war. It is also
public knowledge that Holland
has been purchasing from Ger-
many and re-labelling all the
Cotton goods it could handle,
and our patriotic country has
been taking its full share per
medium of intensely patriotic
York-street and Flinders-lane.
Those resolutions to neffermore trate mit
Ilans ought to be flung into the Pacific.
They merely, in a manner of speaking, side-
track that tariff wall.
Though Andrew Fisher doesn’t take any-
thing with his soda, unless he pours it out
of the milk-jug, he would be a sadder man
than he is if all Australia followed the
King, Stimulants and narcotics are return-
ing an extra £lO,OOO a month under the
increased excise, otherwise . the revenue
would take a big drop. And that is the
drop the temperance Treasurer is most con-
cerned about at present.
For some mysterious reason, possibly con-
nected with herrings, Premier Peacock is
introducing into his Bill to assimilate the
State and Commonwealth electoral methods
a clause making voting compulsory. Why
imperil a commonsense economy by tacking
on to it a doubtful and quite irrelevant pro-
posal—a proposal so doubtful, in fact, that
Peacock wouldn’t dare to apply it to his
own mining companies?
The Parliamentary programme for this
session in Victoria looks more promising
than gaudy, though some of the items at
purely ornamental. Bills dealing with the
Price of Foods, the Murray Waters, the
proposed Greater Melbourne Council, Pub-
lic Charities, the Price of Gas. Combines
and Monopolies, and Public Health are the
main subjects for consideration. Elmslie
starts off with the usual no-confidence
motion; but this amounts to nothing. The
real opposition will come from the Rich
Landlords, who won’t say half as much as
the nominal Opposition, but will destroy
Peacock’s work far more effectively. And
as long as Peacock leaves Fat in its trench
the assumption is that he wants to see his
bills bayoneted.

What sort of a reception would Dry-
berra receive on a vote of the present Senate
if the Capital Sites battle could be re-
fought? Of the 16 Senators who, in the
final vote five and a half years ago, sold the
Cotter pup to Australia, only Gould and
Millen remain on deck; but T-renwith, of
Victoria, is alone missing from the band
of 14 Nationalists who refused to be bluffed
by the Ma State-Frighters into accepting a
barren piece of Sydney’s backyard as the
site for the capital of a continent.
A DIRGE OF SUNDRY DEBTORS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The latest figures show that the debt of the Aus-
tralian States is £6l 6s. sd. per head of the popula-
tion.
Is it not wonderful? Can it be true! All of this
money we owe—me and you! Ev’ry young
fellow and bald-headed coot; every saint, and
each sinner to boot; ev'ry dear girl, be she
eighty or eight; the children of Fat, and the
wards of the State; each innocent babe, though
it cannot as yet observe more than “Goo’ ” owes
a mountain of debt; each infant, in fact, in
this country alive owes sixty-one quidlets six
shillings and fivel
And this, be it understood, is merely in its capa-
city as a State infant. As a Federal infant the un-
fortunate child must be held responsible for its share
of something like another seven and a half millions.
Jones —forty, and bald—lives down Jolimont way,
but journeys by tram to the city each day. He
“saves” on a fiver, and suffers from corns; the
Flesh and the Devil he righteously scorns. Ilis
stipend he snares in the drapery trade, and sees
that his rent and insurance are paid. lie gardens
a little, and drinks rather less; his only real
vice is a passion for chess; and no one who
noticed him toddling with care (for his corns
always keep him informed that they're there),
with well-rolled umbrella and business-like bag,
and, fixed in his coat fold, a small Belgian flag,
would think for an instant that Jones is in debt
for something like sixty-two quidlets; and yet
this is the plain truth! So, in cold, steely
tones, I ask: “What think you of a blighter like
Jones ?”
And Smith, whose employment is sorting out hides;
and Smythe, who to town in a motor-car rides ;
the good man whose job ’tis the Gospel to
preach, the bad men who write for the Yarra-
side Screech ; McTavish the German, O’Leary
the Jew, Ike Cohen the Scotsman, the “softies”
at Kew, the “heads” out at Caulfield, the “nuts”
on the Block, who tilleth the soil, and who tend-
eth his flock, the Fatman who lives on an int’rest
in tin, and Flossie who lives on the wages of
sin ; the cute fiend who cannons in style off the
“cush,” the fencer who toils in the heart of the
bush, the butcher, the baker, the candlestick-
maker, the doctor, the nurse, and the brave
undertaker ; the flapper who flaps half her time
on the Block, the vagrant who drifts ’twixt the
gaol and the dock, the woman with children,
the woman with “views,” the sweater who
sweats, and the student who stews, the blue-
blooded exile who lives on a pension, and others
too humorous even to mention, eat, drink and
make merry, what time they contrive to owe
sixty-one pounds six shillings and five!
But fill we our pewters—the cash we can borrow—
and care not a d—-n for what happens to-
morrow! The int’rest is growing, the outlook is
blue; afar sounds the rumble of loans falling
due; but trip we a measure and merrily sing:
“Where, Jew, is thy victory; where, Debt, is
thy sting?”
VICTORIA.
HARRISON OWE.
THE VERGERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Dago’s eyeballs roll and flash,
His hands are black as ink ;
I buy a bream for sixpence cash,
He says his comrades think
That Italy is on the Verge
And Greece is on the Brink.
A leaderette enwraps the 1 ream,
Familiar words emerge—
Greasy, and odorous and dim,
They read: “The cables urge
That Italy is on the Brink,
And Greece is on the Verge.”
And daily from those wastes of mud
Where twenty millions surge
Through hells of murder, fire and blood,
Comes drifting back the dirge
That Italy is on the Brink,
And Greece is on the Verge.
When half the world is dead with age,
And weariness and drink,
Still will the mighty conflict rage,
Whilst future glasses clink
To Italy upon the Verge,
And Greece upon the Brink.
N.S.W.
W. R. D.

INCORRECT DIAGNOSIS.
TONIC PRESCRIBED FOR WELL-
KNOWN SYDNEY MAN WHO HAD
LOST THIRTY POUNDS IN WEIGHT.
—WORMS THE CAUSE.
The striking case of Mr. G. B. Renton,
of 14 Sir John Young’s Crescent, Sydney.
N.S.W., and connected with a well-known
George. Street wallpaper house, illustrates
two. things: One, that doctors are not in-
fallible ; and the other that adults are
troubled with worms just as children are.
and that Comstock’s “Dead Shot’’ Worm
Pellets are a most effective remedy for this
most unpleasant complaint. The letter of
Mr. Renton is purely voluntary and freely
given, and simply states the facts, which he
will at any time confirm.
“Some time ago I began losing weight in
an alarming manner,” writes Mr. Renton,
“so much so that I went to see a doctor,
who examined me and came to the conclu-
sion, it was due to overwork and worry... I
continued to lose weight and also suffer from
peculiar internal pains. I became very
anxious at the continued loss of weight, and
thinking I might be going into a decline
went to a second doctor, who examined me
thoroughly and gave about the same opinion
as the former one had done, and prescribed
a tonic. One night going home in the train
I read one of your advertisements giving
symptoms of people who have worms. The
symptoms given strongly coincided with my
own, and I determined to test the question.
I accordingly procured a packet of your
Comstock’s ‘Dead Shot’ Worm Fellets, and
took eight tablets, and in the morning four
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, and to my
intense satisfaction and relief passed two
very large stomach worms. I took them
and showed them to the doctor who
had last examined me, and who had pre-
scribed the tonic, and it is needless to say
he was surprised at the result. Altogether
I lost 80 pounds in two months, due to these
worms, and you may believe me I was
thoroughly alarmed, thinking I might have
consumption. I regained all this _ lost
weight immediately I rid my system of the
worms, and am in splendid health at pre-
sent. These are the simple facts which
speak for themselves, and I shall be only too
pleased to confirm them to any person, who
doubts their truth. You have my permission
to use this for publication.”
Comstock’s “Dead Shot”. Worm Pellets
are a purely vegetable medicinal preparation
for the eradication of worms in children and
adults. For sale by all chemists and store-
keepers, price 3s. Gd. per packet; or
six packets for 205.; or will be sent
post paid at the same price by The
W. 11. Comstock Co.. Ltd., 23 Lang Street,
Church Hill, Sydney.*

v PROBLEM.
[For The Bulletin.]
A boxing crowd chased Sam Langford out of the
ring in Montreal last week, hooting and hissing him on
the grounds that lie was not trying.— Gable item.
The angry crowd blasphemed at Sam and chased him
through the ropes,
It clamored loudly for his gore and that of all
black “hopes” ;
it threatened him enragedly as through the doors he
hopped—•
Hut what would those who made the row have done
if he had stopped?
N.s.w. WILLIE.
The tennis championship tournament was
more or less washed away in Sydney last week, and
instead of the finals being played on Saturday the
games have overflowed well into this week. At the
time of writing there’s nothing decisive. The only
championship finally nailed down is the Ladies’,
which went to Miss Stewart, of Victoria, who turned
the tables on Mrs. Ford (6-4, 6-2) after the N.S.W.
lady had beaten her in the inter-State match 6-1, 8-6.
The other championships are being narrowed down,
and on present appearances only the old hands will
see the finals. ■ '
Judging by the “gates” at the opening
of the football season at Melbourne on Saturday, war
and drought are things of no account. The 15,000
crowd at the big match, Carlton v. ’Roy, was well up
to the opening-day average. The game was fast and
skilful, and when, after being seemingly out-played
in the earlier quarters, the old Blues got going in
the last, and wiped off the deficit, excitement rocked
the crowd like a gale. For 15 minutes both sides
strove all they knew for the point that meant victory ;
but ’Roy’s defence was too sound for Carlton’s attack.
The bell rang, with the tale 41 points all. At Vic-
toria Park ’Wood made a better start than usual by
walking over Essendon to the tune of 52 points to
26. Last year’s runners-up, South, got a shock at
St. Kilda. The seasiders have discarded the red,
white and black colors of llogmany, and adopted
those of'Belgium, and the change has apparently in-
fused into them some of the latter’s spirit; anyhow,
they snatched the lead from the Southerners before
half-time,, and gave them no rest thereafter, winning
by 64 points to 37. Melbourne, having captured most
of the disbanded ’Varsity team’s stars, now possesses
a strong agglomeration of ’bailers, but individual
brilliance is of no avail against training and combina-
tion, arid unless the team practises it will go down
as persistently as aforetime. It managed to beat
Richmond by 82 points 73, but that was nothing
to brag about.
*, * * *
“Goal”: There was only a week’s inter-
lude in Melbourne between the chief summer and
winter games, but in that time the Vic. Hoofball
League grasped the opportunity to settle its attitude
towards the new all-Australian game, whose ultimate
eventuation is now practically assured, though much
still depends on the public’s attitude when the exhibi-
tion games are played, towards the close of this
season. Chief points of variation are, on the Rugby
League side, the abolition of the scrum and the throw
in; and, on the other, the adoption of the Rugby
method of tackling, the bar across the goals, and
the abolition of the right to throw or knock the
ball forward as well as backward —in other words,
the imposition of “off-side” on the Australian game.
Seven of the component nine Vic. League clubs
accepted the alterations, and though this writer
approves of an all-Australian game if it can be accom-
plished and popularised, he has doubts about the
wisdom of adopting the “off-side” rule. The Aus-
tralian game has been attractive and fast without
any offside rule —-why make it slower and less exciting
by adopting it ?
* * * *
Senior Victorian football club officials
have been worrying as to whether it was compulsory
for their players to be insured under the Workers’
Compensation Act. The State Insurance Commis-
sioner, on application, said “Yes,” and added that a
premium of £2 a head would be charged. Still
unsatisfied, the Association took the opinion of 11. E.
Starke, who advised as follows: “An Association foot-
ball player is not, in my opinion, a ‘worker’ within
the meaning of the Workers’ Compensation Act.
Therefore, in my opinion, neither the Association
itself, nor the clubs, paying the respective players
a fee for playing are under any obligation to insure
the players pursuant to the Act. As the compulsory
clauses of the Act come actively into force on May 7,
when football will be in full swing, it will be inter-
esting to see Whether barrister Starke is right of the
Insurance Commissioner.
* * *
“Scrum” : The N.S.W. Rugby Union was
well advised not to make arrangements for an active
football 'season. The manly Club reports that 33
of its members have volunteered for war service.
East Sydney and Armidale, with 31, are next on a
long list. Of the many members of the old brigade
who have obtained commissions in the Expeditionary
Force are J. F. Macnanemy and B. I. Swannel. The
proportion of League members who have followed the
example of secretary E. R. Larkin, M.L.A., who was
an early recruit, compares badly with the Union.
On this subject Headmaster Adamson, of Wesley
College (Vic.), made the grimly humorous statement
that if he were a- German living in Australia he
would assist Germany by patronising League football,
thus encouraging the semi-professional ’bailers to
keep out of the trenches.
“Makaire”: Chanced to wander into a
paddock attached to a Maori pa a day or two ago.
A. football match was in progress, and I stopped to
watch. Football, billiards and horse-racing’ are part
of a Maori’s religion. The game proceeded, to the
accompaniment of a medley of shrieks, groans, hoots
and waUyells from the spectators, until the hall
worked out at the side of the scrum. A big fellow,
clad a bit different to the rest, appeared, seemingly
from the clouds, grabbed the leather and bounded off
with a good start. His fellow-players emitted a yell
that senf a queer chill up my spine and gave chase.
The whole of the spectators and about 53 dogs of
various shapes and breeds insisted on joining it. But
the man with the ball had reached the goal-posts, and
there lie grounded it. A try! But before he could
arise the crowd was on him, men pummelling, the
waliines tearing his hair out, and the dogs con-
ducting well-organised rushes at his unprotected
legs. I spoke to a large and ponderous youth who
stood by watching matters with ill-concealed amuse-
ment. “What’s the matter?” I said, pointing to the
dilapidated and dishevelled try-getter. “Oh, him,”
was the contemptuous retort, “he no plurry good!”
“But what’s he done?” 1 persisted. “Done?” in-
dignantly shouted Henars. “Why, he te plurry
ref’ree! !”
The Demlen-Loughrey disturbance at
Sydney Stadium on Saturday night was a rotten
show, regarded as a fight. The parties seemed only
really happy when they were in clinches. They
were as slow as dinosaurs, and the clean hitting
which betrays the championship event was mostly
absent. As a psychological study, however, the
scuffle was worth the money. Loughrey is an Irish-
American, and as full of emotionalism as a dime
novel. Towards the end of the row he hit Demlen low.
The latter protested, whereupon the other promptly
landed him again in the same place. The referee
grabbed the offender and severely cautioned him; in
fact, it looked as if he severely cursed him. Lough-
rey thought, apparently, that he had been overtaken
by the fate which he had assuredly earned, and he
proceeded to give a representation of a strong Yank
in anguish. Twice he leapt in the air, with his feet
close together; and twice he raised his hands in
unison and dashed them to liis side again. Then he
sobbed. At this stage his mentor, A 1 Lippe, con-
vinced him that all was well ; his sobs ceased; he
lay back in the arms of his friends like a tired child,
and had his nose wiped and his brow valeted.
Demlen is the stolidest proposition imaginable. He
tottered to what looked like certain doom at the
beginning of the next round with an impassive coun-
tenance. Howls from the enemy’s corner of “Git to
his hardy, Bo!” “Hit him in the bal-ly, son!” and
so forth, left him calm. Half-way through the round
he was recovering; by the end of it lie was pasting
the young fellow from the U.S. all over the ring.
It was a fine study in Belgium ruggedness. At the
finish Ilarald Baker rightly gave the decision to
Demlen, who accepted the same, with the unconcern
of a brass image. When the Yankee youth grasped
the position he bounded four times in the air, as
before, semaphored violently with his arms, as on the
previous occasion, and bowing his head upon the ropes
wept bitterly on to the writer. He then went else-
where and wept on to another pressman. Loughrey
is a game fighter; in fact, it is not too much to
say that he is a Yankee Demlen as regards endur-
ance. But he requires to get a grip on himself, and
cut the nursery business out.
A. B. JONES, a Tennis Champion.
Hughie Mehegan and Fred Kay fought as
welters in Perth last Friday. The old lightweight
champion tried to knock his elusive opponent out,
and with a motor-car or even a pair of skates he
might have done it. On a pedestrian basis Kay
was too shifty. After 20 rounds of stern effort on
the part of Hughie, and stern evasion on that of
Frederick, the referee gave it a draw,., and for once
the house was satisfied.

N.S.W. amateur boxing champions were
drafted out last week. The final conflicts were waged
with great fury. In the heavyweight division R. I).
Manning (12st. 61b.) and S. Baker (14st.) smashed
one another with heavy, meaty swipes, the big fellow
going down four times in the second round. By
virtue of his right and a couple of blows unnoticed
by the referee, Manning took the jam-dish. 11.
Heaney triumphed over the other middleweights,
clinching the matter by dropping E. White in his
tracks for the necessary 10; but he was himself
beaten in the welter by R. Finlay, the winner last
year. F. Brownlee captured.the lightweight prize from
V. Lawson, who was badly punished. P. Jones was the
best of the feathers, and IV. Bell was the only
bantam entitled to crow at the finish. ,On the whole,
a moderate lot. -t

Hostile meetings for Saturday— at Syd-
ney Stadium, Harold Hardwick and Jeff Smith; at
Melbourne, Les Darcy and Fritz Holland. The first
two are strangers to one another: Darcy and Holland
have swapped punches thrice before. Holland, much
the best boxer among the middle-weights, had the
worst of the last encounter. Hardwick miy find
that Smith hurts more than King, who argued the
point with him over 20 rounds a few weeks ago.
Jimmy Hill and Geike at Brisbane the same night.
McGoorty, the U.S. left-hook specialist, is
coming back to Australia. Also Jack Dillon,
a pugnacious person who has been raging from Noo
Yark to ’Frisco at the rate of a battle every fort-
night the last couple of years. With this pair to
operate on the middleweight division should he able
to sort itself out fairly well.
From Buenos Ayres: The racecourse at
Palermo (near this city of sin and beauty) gives
Flemington and Randwick a point or two. Fleming-
ton was made by nature, in a sense; Palermo has
'been made by money. The upper floors of the
grandstand are reached by electric lifts. The flat,
as we Australians know it, isn’t in the picture. In
its place there are lakes and ponds, islands and
rustic bridges, flowers and shrubs and swans. That is
what you gaze on from the stands instead of contem-
plating a hot crowd of people wolfing sandwiches and
beer. Then there is a wide .tree-shaded space 'with
chairs and tables and waiters, where one can sit
and have a drink in peace. There are no bookmakers
to bellow. All betting is through totes, one for a
win and the other for places. Parsifal 11., a grand-
son of old Carbine, ran here a few months ago.
He’s a game finisher, and I would like to take him
to Vic. and have a cut at the two Cups.
* * * *
Earlier in the year good judges were say-
ing that the Bedwell-bred colt Reputation, which is
to be sent across from Maoriland to winter on the
spot before .being tried against Australia’s best at
Randwick in the spring, was pounds better than
War.step. But as Warstep has beaten the
colt at w.f.a.., dead-heated in the N.Z. Cup,
won the Great Easter Handicap, and the
Dunedin Cup and set up a new two-miles
record (3min. 24sec.) in the Trentham Cup
at Wellington, and as Reputation is on the
small side, the good judges may merely have
been showing once more how wrong the judgment
of good judges may be.
War clouds have not frightened the A.J.C.
into being sparing with the prize-money for big
fixtures ahead. For the 1915 spring meeting the
Metrop. (£5000), Epsom (£2000) and Breeders’
Plate (£2000) will be as heretofore; also the A.J.C.
Derby ( £5000) and Sires Produce Stakes ( £3000)
in 1916, and Sires Produce Stakes (£3000) and St.
Leger ( £1500) in 1917 remain intact.
Pony-racing in Sydney is a thriving mid-
week business for McWowser’s partner who carries
the bag. It took 17 races (in heats and run-offs)
to dispose of last week’s programme at Ascot, and
only seven favorites were successful. It was the
longest helping of horse at one day’s meet that Tins
Bulletin knows' of.
It is remarkable how often the M.L.
Great Autumn Handicap has been won by a mare. A
start was made by Lady Emma in 1881, who rushed
home first carrying sst. 131 b., the lowest weight ever
put on a winner of the race. Rubina won in 1885,
Ravenswing ip 1889, Hazel in 1891, Merganser in
1893, Lady Zetland in 1894, Bellicent in 1896, Gold
Leaf in 1898, Pitch and Toss in 1899, Palaver in 1901
and Lady Lilian in 1903. Then there was a break
until last year, when Bronze won.
Polo is another branch of sport that has
heard The war gong. So many players have volun-
teered that the country clubs won’t be able to turn
out, and the big Sydney annual gymkhana has been
abandoned, including the yearly contest for the
Countess of Dudley’s Cup.
After a lot of barging Charlie Towns and
Felton have signed up for a race on June 26. The
stake is £250 a side; and the course, as insisted
upon by Felton, is (o.jie Middle Harbor, Port Jack-
son. Towns was eager “to row on the old Parramatta,
having a natural desire, to win back, if possible, the
honors he lost there when he last met Felton. But
Felton wasn't letting sentiment get in the road of
cash returns; and, when the precariousness of the
sculling game these days is considered, there’s small
blame attachable to him.
Something definite, too, appears to have come
upon the negotiations of Webb and Hannan in
Maoriland. The champion is reported to have planked
a covering wad on Hannan’s £lOO, and has declared
for September as the time for concluding the long
and wearisome argument. The challenger’s anxiety
was to race in June, and on this point, or any other
old point, another hitch may occur. It’s more in the
nature of a skulling match than the other thing.
Incidentally, there’s nothing doing for Arnst with
Webb—at least, that’s the latest , bulletin from head-
quarters.
Peter Kemp, who once signed himself
champion sculler of the world, passed some of his
oarsmanship on to his family. Hence it was, partly,
that son Syd and daughter Fern relieved Lily and
Arthur Pearce of the double-scull championship of
N. S. Wales on Saturday at Middle Harbor. Bad
weather made for gruelling work, but the girls
in both boats showed fine grit and sea-craft. The
Ivemps, having won the toss, were able to take advan-
tage of some leeshore, and consequently had not the
same solid fight against the punching southerly as
the Pearce delegates. One and a half lengths separ-
ated the canoes when they passed the judge, and the
losers promptly separated themselves from a side-
stake of £4O.

Sydney Church of England Grammar
School’s wiri in the race for the Great Public School
eight-oar championship last week, at Sydney added to
its ornamental record in the event. Since 1893 this
school has dragged its proa to the home line first
on 12 occasions. In the four-oar championship St.
Joseph’s gave its friends an excuse to maffick by
scoring a maiden victory.
Lacrosse is becoming animated at the
approach of Sydney’s energising season of winter.
Inter-State visits are listed between Queensland and
N.S.W., at Brisbane; and Tasmania, and Federals
(Sydney), at Tasmania. The N.S.W. Association’s
finances are sound, and there are plenty of playing
grounds, so the 35th season will be opened on Satur-
day without any anxiety, thank you.
* * * *
The very first test-match at lacrosse
between Britain and Australia has come off, in the
shadow of the Pyramids. But it wasn’t a real Test.
The English team —Lancashire Territorials, mostly—
admitted (or claimed) that they were practically a
representative XII, whereas our side was merely what-
ever talent was available, half S. Aus., the rest
Queensland and Vic. The Englishmen provided all
the materials for the game. They also won easily.
*■**•*
N.S.W. Cricket Association “reform”
proposals are getting short shrift. On top of the
refusal of inter-State Associations to swallow the
“eight-balls-to-the-over” pill, the State delegates in
council last week threw out the recommendation to
cut down first-grade clubs from 13 to 12 —at one
time it was proposed to reduce them to 10—and also
decided not to concede second-grade clubs representa-
tion on the Association. It took the reform com-
mittee three months to think out that little lot, too!
“Midchannel”: A word about resuscitat-
ing interest in big cricket. Hitherto, the batsmen
have had all the concessions and the bowlers have
suffered all the disadvantages. For instance, bats-
men' are allowed to guard their wicket with their
pads after the ball has beaten the hat so long as
the hall is pitched clear of the stumps. The
absurdity is obvious in the ease of googly bowlers,
who, bowling with a fair break, must pitch the ball
clear of the wicket. The rule for l.b.w. needs amend-
ing. Then, no batsman should he allowed to remain
at his wicket (in perfect condition) unless he makes
50 runs each hour. This would mean that every
partnership lasting an hour would yield 100 runs,
which is good scoring. If he should fail to make
50 ho ought to be counted out. This would weed
out Bardsleys and Ivelleways who study their figures
on paper and not the feelings of the crowd. “Oh,”
someone says, “the bowlers will take mighty good
care they don’t score off them at that rate, because
they’ll pitch nearly every ball outside the reach of
the batsman.” There is a counter for this. Am
bowler who pitches two balls clear of the batsman’s
reach in any over will have four runs given against
him. As for the suggestion to have eight balls in
an over, it is obvious the men who make it have
never played when the glass was 95deg. in the shade.
Fancy men like Richardson, Brearley, Cotter or
Lockwood bowling eight balls to an over 1
* * * *
“Moana” : The suggested eight balls to an
over isn’t going to put new life into cricket, but, all
the same, it is a step towards smiting tradition out
of the ground, and that is something. My pet schenn
is the bowling of two overs in succession from each
end. It seems remarkable that this very simple and
entirely reasonable solution of the troublesome chang-
ing- over difficulty and the consequent delays has no.
been tried before. Here is how it would work.
Smith bowls six balls from the pavilion end ami
Brown, the other bowler, does ditto. Then the field
changes over, and Smith bowls six balls from tin
other end, and Brown again does ditto, and so on.
This would at once cut down the changing over and
the delays by one-half without putting any extra
strain on the bowlers.

* * * *
W. J. Stack, the long, loose-limbed Sydney
’Varsity slow bowler, has joined the Army Medical
Corps, with the rank of Captain. “Tib” Cotter, the
N.S.W. express, is with the Light Horse. R. ,1. A.
Massie, the two Minnetts, Gregg and other leading
first-graders are also with the colors. There wouldn’t
be any difficulty in raising a first-class team to plav
(lie Germans cricket—-if they “played cricket,” which
they don’t.
* * * *
Adelaide Y.M.C.A., which believes in
getting the under-hold when wrestling with the Devil,
has installed two billiard-tables in its new building
in the local Expeditionaries’ camp at Mitcham"
Alcock and Co. gave one table, and the Good Young
Men of the city, who number some very warm
Players among them, found the other and the build-
ing which houses them.
* * * *
From “Rosna” (London) : The war—if
1 may mention it-—has brought out An irregular
army of crack she-motorists and motor-cyclists. The
Women’s Emergency Corps, which Leiia Ashwell,
Decima Moore and other stage damsels started in
August, was in two months 500 strong—all women
motorists or cyclists, many of them not only expert
drivers but qualified mechanics also. One dame, a
Mrs. Knocker, of Salisbury, jumped on her motor-
cycle when war was declared and dashed off to the War
Office to offer her services as a despatch-rider. The
M .0. nearly fell over its own red-tape at the impu-
dence, and refused. The lady rode sadly home to
Salisbury, and on the way had the pleasure of repair-
ing the motor-cycles of three male despatch riders
on Government business, who were incapable of
mending their own broken-down machines. Mrs.
Knocker afterwards took herself off to Belgium,
where she has just received the Order of Leopold
from Albert the Good-Looking, for services rendered.
The Bulletin is sorry to hear that one
of Australia’s oldest manufacturing indus-
tries is suffering from the suspicion that it
is “made in Germany.” Zevenboom might
he some sort of a Deutscher; but, as a mat-
ter of fact, the man of that name who landed
in Victoria 59 years ago and started to
make paint and other brushes came from
Holland, and his successors in the business,
which has been carried on ever since, are
partly Scotch and partly English and wholly
Australian.

“Did I have a good time at the Show?
You bet I did. Rained like blazes most
of the time, but I didn’t mind that when
I heard it was doing a hit back here.
“I met a few of the old boys —ran
against George Eland, the champion whip-
maker, and Saltbush Bill, the champion
whip-cracker. Old Saltbush had the pin
the King gave him in England—l’d like
to own it.
“George Eland took me over to Walther
and Stevenson’s stand on the Show-
ground, and I saw some saddles, harness,
whips and things that made me mouth
water. Eland only makes for Walther
and Stevenson.
“Well, I bought one of Eland’s whips
for thirty boh —this one here —and I
couldn’t resist this saddle. It’s the
Wien eke Pattern Stock Saddle, and a beauty
at that. It’ll stand rough work all right, and has a jolly comfortable seat. The waist
is narrow and the tij.ee is heavily steel-plated over the head and along the bars. You
can see the panel lfas extended leather facings, and has leather points down the flaps,
and this gives a chap a great ‘grip.’ The flaps are long, and the long girth straps
buckle below the flaps away from a feller’s legs. The fittings thrown in were short,
folded leather girth (two buckles each end), bevelled stirrup leathers and solid nickel
stirrups. I paid £O/15/- and got a bargain. The firm send this saddle freight free,
for cask, to any port or railway station in N.S.W., or any direct port in the other
States, New Zealand or Fiji. Write for their catalogue, Bill, they’ll send it to you
post free.” ____________

A Noted
Hair Specialist.
There is a clever young English scientist, Margaret
Windeyer, practising her profession of hair and skin
specialist in Sydney, who is going to cause a stir
in this sunny land of ours by the extraordinary things
that she is doing for the hair. She practises what is
known as the Thorl system of hair culture, under
which falling hair, even if it is coming out in hand-
fuls, as it often does, is stopped within a week, or so,
and made firm in the scalp. The dandruff germ is
entirely cleared away so that it cannot return.
Thin, weak, short, broken, decayed hair is made to
spring into life and grow thick, long, strong and
beautiful. It is worthy of note, too, that this re-
markable system of hair culture has the power of
growing thick, healthy, firm hair upon scalps that
have been bald and bare of hair for many years.
The most amazing feature about it is that it entirely
cures grey hair, that is, it brings the natural
original color back to the greyest, whitest hair,
irrespective of whether the hair has been grey since
one was -in one's teens, or whether it is absolutely
all grey,’ or 'half or three-parts grey, or just going
grey. “To have the lost color brought back to one’s
grey hair so that never again in the coming years
of one’s life can a single thread go grey, to be cured
of our baldness, falling hair, dandruff, etc-., to have
our hair given such a start that it’ll just go on
growing thicker and longer year after year without
a bit of bother, worry or expense—that seems to us
to be the kind of relief that is worth seeking and
obtaining with all speed. Nearly the whole of the
first floor of the Bank of N.S.W., 228 Pitt Street
(opposite Palace Theatre), has been taken for this
young Englishwoman’s laboratories, consulting rooms,
etc., and it is pleasing to know that consultations
with her are free. There are private separate and
exclusive waiting-rooms for men folk and for women.
Country dwellers who send a few combings to Miss
Windeyer to be examined under the microscope will
be written to. free of charge, explaining what is
wrong with their hair and the cost of complete per-
manent cure. Her address: Margaret Windeyer, Hair
and Skin Specialist, Bank of N.S.W. (first floor), 228
Pitt Street, Sydney.*

The infamy of robbing the Blind Man of
liis dorg has been surpassed. At Bathurst
(N.S.W.) last week someone stole £8 belong-
ing to the local Belgian Fund.
A Melbourne paper asks for a new name
for the motor-’bus, in view of the many
accidents that have resulted since its appear-
ance on the streets. What about blunder-
bus?
Spurr, the anti-Defenee crank, imported
for the Collins-street Baptist Church in
Melbourne, anti now pulpiteering at Regent's
Park in London, has published “Five Years
Under the Southern Cross.” The Times
calls it a “pleasant and lively volume,” cal-
culated to make everybody pack up and
make tracks for Australia, without delay, to
enjoy a gorgeous climate, delightful fruits,
spacious cities and splendid opportunities—
especially for manual workers, who draw
the highest wages in creation. Incidentally,
The Times expresses the hope that many
will read the book “as an introduction to a
serious study of a vast region which, great
as its advance has been, still offers a multi-
tude of untried opportunities to enterprising
Englishmen.”
Thomas Holden and his wife, of Geelong
(Vic.), discovered a week ago that 50 years
had passed since their partnership com-
menced, and they rejoiced accordingly. They
and their families go back to the beginnings
of Australia. Mrs. Holden’s father was
Seth Hague, one of Melbourne’s first wool-
brokers. Her husband was the son of a sol-
dier in the 17tli Regiment which landed in
Sydney nearly 00 years ago, and had served
over 10 years when he received his discharge
from Colonel Despart in 1836. He was born
at Camden (N.S.W.), but went to Geelong
when the world was 68 years younger, and
found the place agree with him so well that
he stayed on and became a trustee of the
Methodist Church.
Tablets in commemoration of Commis-
sioner Coombs and Colonel Kilbey, in front
of the Salvation Army Temple, in Mel-
bourne, have just been painted out. Yet it
is a simple fact that the acquisition of this
magnificent property was due to the energy
of those two men —in 1895 and thereabouts.
The property cost the Y.M.C.A. £55,000 in
the palmy and balmy time when Mat Davies
was the gorgeous president. He and the
other magnificoes had paid £29,000 off the
debt when the slump came. Coombs and
Kilbey got the lot for. £19.500. How they
raised the money just then nobody knows;
but, anyway, they secured a gigantic plum
for General Booth. The property is now
worth much more than £30,000. Yet
Coombs and Kilbey, who received Booth’s
warmest thanks, have to get out. In the
like spirit the name of Herbert Booth was
scraped off the very fine buildings he
erected, the Training College and Printing
Office. The official histories of the herring-
brained crowd omit all mention of such
valuable workers as Coombs and Kilbey, or
of Pollard and Wright, the pair who pio-
neered the business in Maoriland ; even the
whilom favorites Catherine Booth, the Gen-
eral’s daughter, and Ballington Booth, the
son. But Time will have its revenges, and
we may yet see the name of William Booth
scored through.
From the annual report of Victoria’s
Education Department. concerning the
Little Slaves of the Cow: —
In some parts of the State, particularly in dairy-
ing districts, many children are overworked at home,
and, when they attend school, are unfit to profit I).''
the instruction. .. .In the case of one school the dis-
trict inspector reported as follows on one locality:
“Child-labor is rampant. Of the 31 children in
attendance, 12 milk cows before comma- to school.
One boy eight years of age milks six cows, and one
girl of six years five cows before leaving for school.
Ten of the pupils rise at daylight, summer and
winter. These circumstances operate against the
school, and, as a consequence, only six children have
a chance of reaching grade eight. The details of the
work done by the 12 overworked children are: Child
eight years old milks six cows; child six years old
milks five cows; child 13 years old milks eight
cows; child 11 years old milks five cows; child 13
years old milks six cows and feeds calves; child 10
years old milks six cows and feeds pigs; child 12
years old milks nine cows ; child 10 years old milks
six cows; child 14 years old milks 12 cows; child 12
years of age milks 12 cows ; child nine years of age
milks eight cows ; child 14 years old milks nine cows
and feeds calves.”
The cows!
“Chasuble”: Depledge Sykes, from Stow
Church, Adelaide, is nominated for the Col-
lins-street Independent Church. He has been
a good deal there, and knows that it is a
cold tomb, so he will not be taken in as Ley-
ton Richards, the Englishman, was. Dr.
£. £. Bevan toiled at it for 20 years, but
could not warm it. To be sure, his big
money was safe, for the rents make this
tabernacle quite independent of the plate—-
like those cobwebby London proprietary
chapels, such a one as Bellew’s father had.
However, money will not satisfy absolutely
every soul, and Sykes is credited with a hope
that he can get warmth into the vault. The
only man hitherto able to do it was Tom
.Tones, the Web b poet-preacher.
A FASHION NOTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The rhymester’s heart is glad to note
The some-time vanished petticoat
Returns again to fashion ;
For petticoats were all along
A theme to grace a simple song—
With me they were a passion!
Whatever Isabel has worn
Could in no way a verse adorn,
And what it was I know not.
Of asking questions I’ve a fear ;
She won’t display what she may wear,
It may be naught—l trow not.
I hear the modern maids all say
The petticoat is “out.” But, nay!
It is no ugly bustle.
Though girls some newer fashions quote,
Man loves his lady’s petticoat
And its bewitching rustle.
Whatever Isabel may wear
She is—at least, part of the year —
My petticoat, my passion.
Though fashions change and twist about,
The petticoat is never out:
It always is the fashion !
x.s.w.

Tenders for Boots.
TENDERS will be received, subject to
the conditions of tendering, until
twelve noon on Monday, 10th May, 1915,
for the supply of Boots for the Royal Aus-
tralian Navy.
Sealed samples may be seen at the office
of the Director of Navy Contracts, Navy
Office, Melbourne; the Victualling Store
Officer, Sydney; and the Naval Staff Office,
Brisbane, Largs Bay (S.A.), Fremantle
and Ilobart, where tender forms can also be
obtained.
Tenders to be endorsed “Tender for
Boots” ‘in top left-hand corner of the
envelope, and addressed (if by post, prepaid)
to the Director of Navy Contracts, Navy
Office, Melbourne ; or the Director of Navy
Contracts, care of the Victualling Store
Officer, Sydney; Naval Staff Office, Bris-
bane, Largs Bay (S.A.), Fremantle or
Hobart, as the case may be.
The lowest or any tender will not neces-
sarily be accepted.
Tenders are to be in all cases on the
printed forms, which can bt ontained as
above.
G. F. PEARCE.

THE BANK OF APELAIPE.
FORTY-NINTH ANNUAL REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS TO THE
SHAREHOLDERS.
The Directors have pleasure in submitting- herewith, for the information of Shareholders, the Balance-
Sheet of the Bank as on March 29, 1915, also Statements of Profit and Loss Account and the Reserve Fund
on that date.
The balance to credit of Profit and Loss is .. .. .. .. .. £53,190 11 5
which it is proposed to appropriate as follows:
To Dividend at the rate of 8 per cent, per annum .. .. .. 20,000 0 0
leaving a balance of .. .. .. .. .. .. .. .. £33,190 11 5
to be carried forward.
An Interim Dividend at the rate of 8 per cent, per annum was paid in November last.
Since the last Report agencies have been opened at Booborowie and Mypolonga, and the agencies at
Bowhill, Callington, Eastern Well, Monarto South, Purnong and Wilkawatt have been closed.
The General Annual Meeting will be held in the Board Room, at the Head Office of the Bank, King-
William Street, Adelaide, on Wednesday, May 5, 1915. at 12 noon, at which the Shareholders will be called
upon to elect two Directors in place of James Harvey, Esquire, and George Brookman, Esquire, who retire
by rotation, and, being eligible, offer themselves for re-election.
The Shareholders will also have to elect Two Auditors for the ensuing year, and to fix the remuner-
ation of the retiring Auditors.

SYMPTOMS of KIDNEY TROUBLE
ARE YOU IN DANGER THROUGH
IGNORANCEV
Nearly all of our readers suffer from
aches and pains of some kind at one time
or another. Aches and pains very often
mean kidney troubles. Such symptoms as
constipation, backache, improper action of
the bowels, pains in muscles and joints,
nasty taste in the mouth first thing in the
morning, swelling of the feet, cloudy urine,
a general feeling of weakness and irrita-
bility, backache, all these are symptoms of
kidney trouble.
They are caused by the poisonous acid in
the blood which the kidneys should remove,
but they do not owing to their being in a
weak state.
To cure kidney trouble of any kind you
must positively get rid of the cause—the
poisonous uric acid. Remember, to do this
a real remedy must pass through the kid-
neys and the bladder, and not through the
bowels, as most kidney pills do. When you
see the urine change to a muddy bluish color
—a feature which distinguishes De Witt’s
Pills from all others —you know beyond all
doubt that they have put their healing
touch on the right spot—the kidneys and
the bladder. A wonderful pill—straight to
the kidneys—that is the reason why I)e
Witt’s give such amazingly quick relief
every time. A permanent cure follows in
almost every case.
DE WITT’S KIDNEY and BLADDER PILLS
can be obtained at any of the branches of
Washington H. Soul Pattinson & Co., Ltd.,
Sydney; Longmore’s The Melbourne Phar-
macy, Bourke Street, Melbourne, and all
leading chemists at 2/9 and 5/6 per large
box. The 5/6 size is two and a half times
as large as the 2/9 ’size, and contains
enough doses to last you three weeks. Try
your nearest chemist, but if you have any
difficulty in obtaining De Witt’s Pills send
the price of the package required to either
of the firms mentioned above, and the goods
will be sent by return, post free.*

For Public Information only. PROSPECTUS of
Boyds Molybdenite Yetkolme Mines No Liability
Yetholme, New South Wales.
CAPITAL. —£20,000 divided into 20,000 shares of £1 each.
7200 Fully Paid-up Shares to Vendors.
2000 Fully Paid-up Shares to Promoters.
5800 Contributing Shares, payable 5/- per share on application, 5/- per share on allotment, and the
balance in calls not to exceed 1/- per share per month.
5000 Shares are held in reserve.
PROVISIONAL DIRECTORS:
PETER LESLIE, Bondi. J. G. PURVES, Glebe. W. A. -T. MARSHALL, Hill End.
R. H. WARREN, Burwood. D. M. MARSHALL, Hill Eend. EDWIN NICHOLS, Paddington.
It. J. BOYD, Yetholme. ®
LEGAL MANAGER: OLIVE A. COOPER, Gibbs Chambers, 7 Moore Street, Sydney.
BANKERS: THE AUSTRALIAN BANK OF COMMERCE LIMITED, Head Office, Sydney,
SOLICITORS: Messrs. WEBSTER & MACLEAN, 7 Moore Street, Sydney.
Boyds Molybdenite Yetholme Mines, No Liability, is being formed to acquire from Boyds \ etholme
Syndicate their Molybdenite Mine, situated four miles from Yetholme and about the same distance from
Gemalla or Locksley Railway Stations, comprising an area of 45 acres. .
The discovery of Molybdenite at Yetholme was made by Messrs. Kirk and Wade during’ 1914— 1
Boyd’s mine was discovered later, during the latter part of the year, and is about one mile distant front
Kirk and Wade’s leases. Open cuts have proved the lode to run through the whole length of Boyd’s Lease
and the outcrop shows the width to be 100 yards and upwards.
Sufficient development work has not yet been done to enable anyone to estimate the value of a lode
of such magnitude, but already an unlimited supply of good grade ore has been exposed assaying up to
47 per cent.
A suitable treatment plant will be erected as speedily as possible, and vigorous development work
proceeded with at the mine.
It is safe to say that the present price of Molybdenite—over £7OO per ton —cap fall a long way
and this property still produce the metal at a big profit.
In purchase of the property the Vendors receive 7200 Fully Paid-up Shares and £BOO in cash. The
Promoters receive 2000 Fully Paid-up Shares.
The only contracts entered into on behalf of the Company are those of which particulars are stated
hereunder.
(«) March 22, 1915. Agreement, Frederick Buckridge, Thomas Maccabec Boyd, Henry John Boyd,
Hugh Turner, Eliza Boyd, Mary Boyd and Emily Turner, with William Alexander John Marshall creat-
ing an option over mining properties.
(b) March 23, 1915. Agreement, William Alexander John Marshall with Clive Ashley Cooper (on
behalf of the Company) for the sale and purchase of the options created by the above-mentioned agreement.
Dated at Sydney, March 23, 1915.

From “The Oldest Inhabitant'': —.
“Monkbarns” (B. 15/4/’lf>) is wrong in stating
that John Thomas Smith, seven times Mayor of Mel-
bourne, was- in partnership with Mark Last King as
a timber merchant in Sydney. John Thomas Smith
started life in Sydney, where his father was a shoe-
maker, as a clerk in the Bank of Australasia. He
was just out of his ’teens when he went to Mel-
bourne as assistant teacher at the Aboriginal Mission.
Then he became clerk to John Hodgson, who advised
him to start in business for himself. About where
Temple Court is now John Thomas Smith, in IS3S,
kept a little tobacco shop. Later, he married a
daughter of Mick Pender, licensee of the Shamrock
Inn in Flinders-lane. Mick set up his three sons-iu-
law in pubs, John Thomas being established in the
Adelphi, Flinders-lane. He had a unique public
career. He was an alderman from the birth of the
Melbourne corporation until his death, and he was a
member of the first Legislative Assembly, and held
that seat, too, until he was too dead to hold any-
thing.
A bit of Emden wreckage from Singapore :
Seen anything of a gentleman named Diehn? lie
was the manager of Behn, Meyer and Co., one of the
largest Hun firms in these parts. When ship
after ship was so easily found and scuttled
by the Kmden, and when the pirate sank a
Russian cruiser and French destroyer at Penang
while they were getting cleaned, the Government
smelt a rat as large as a whale, and made investiga-
tions. The result was that they unearthed a powerful
wireless installation and two German operators at
Diehn’s home. Diehn was tried and sentenced to 15
years’ imprisonment. The Emden did practically
nothing afterwards. When the sth Light Infantry
mutinied and opened the gates of the German en-
campment, Diehn was One of the few who escaped,
and he succeeded in getting aVvay to Sumatra. Now
the Government is trying to put its hand on him,
and is working with an assiduity which must be
making Herr Diehn very uncomfortable.
LINKS ON AN OLD NEGLECTED BURIAL
GROUND, HOBART, TAS.
Upon the hardened turf,
Above human bones,
Shattered and wrecked,
Lay old' tombstones.
Around the paths and beds
Where once flowers did bloom
Wild weeds did twine in
The yard of gloom.
Privations and trials brave
Proud wealth can leave
But cannot defy the
Dark wormy grave.
I realised the power
And work of fate
As I turned away
From the rickety gate.

HOW Of It GRANDMOTHERS
MANAGED TO ROOK YOUNG
AT SIXTY.
“It is strange,” remarked an old chemist the
other day, “that so many women nowadays
persist in trying all sorts of injurious patent
systems and _ complicated treatments, hoping
to restore ruined complexions or improve the
.appearance of their skins, when thoy might
easily do so by the simple home remedies of
their grandmothers. In olden times a woman
looked comparatively young at 60, while now
most women have blemished complexions long
before they are 30. Yet nothing new has
been invented anything like so effective as
the old-time lotion used by famous French
Court beauties, such as Recamier and Du
Barry, to preserve their youthful appear-
ance. The recipe was so simple: just 2oz.
rosewater, 1 dram tincture of benzoin, and
2oz. ordinary flowers of oxzoin, which any
chemist will supply. Mix the lotion well,
then apply with a soft cloth. If you apply
it regularly it will not only keep the skin
free from- wrinkles, but will quickly restore
the dullest complexion. My own daughter is
nearly 50 years old, and in this way she
keeps her face looking so young and fresh
that most'people think she has not reached
the age of 35.”
. The above advice'indicates such a simple,
harmless means to rejuvenate the appear-
ance, that* it is published- for the benefit of
lady, readers.*
