IN
BANANA LAND
Only one Brisbane daily, the Telegraph,
supports Denham wholeheartedly. The
Mail uses the stiletto ; the Courier damns
with faint praise; the small fry pray and
scoff by turns.
The irony of things. Scarcely has Rob-
erts, the: Yankee chemist, successfully demon-
strated that his arsenious chloride, if not
capable ; of absolutely destroying pear in
immense areas, is yet an infallible means of
checking, its spread, than Western graziers
come along testifying that pear is their sal-
vation in drought time. Unless rain comes
shortly, pastoralists in some parts will be
taking out an injunction against arsenious
chloride and denouncing Oliver Cromwell
Roberts as a public enemy.
Queensland Ministers are prattling every-
where : “Our railways pay their way.” The
State has just renewed a loan for £1i,728,000
at a price which makes the nominal 4\ per
cent, nearly 4f, while official statistics show
that the railway system is returning a net
£3 7s. 6d. per cent. Practically the whole
of the loan renewed was spent on line con-
struction, so that Bananaland has money
borrowed at 4| per cent, bringing in some-
what less than 3} per cent. That is how
the Queensland railways “pay their way.”
“Opus”: I have just returned from a
critical inspection of tropical Queensland in
time to tell two recent Bulletin inquirers
that I don’t think much of the cocoanut-
growing prospects up there. Reasons
(amongst others) : Scale, exorbitant ship-
ping freights and erratic, facilities for trade.
Incidentally, I noted mangoes, flyblown, are
wasted in hundreds of tons. What’s wrong
with the freer manufacturing of chutney to
eat with the salt horse and cold cow so
extensively devoured these times ? Cascara
bean that grows so luxuriantly is of no com-
mercial use—it is not sagrada. Tamarinds
are wasted in shiploads, despite their many
uses. Strychnine bean flourishes ungar-
nered in scrubs. Wasted also are “wild”
pumpkins, tomatoes, marrows and sweet-
bucks sufficient to feed expeditions. In the
canning factories tanks of what ought to be
syrup are discarded in the guise of pine-
apple peelings. But why worry? There
might be a surplus of foodstuffs on the
market, and that would perchance mean
HALF-PRICE.
THE McT AVISH: Threepence, ye say? Have ye no half-price for a man wi' one eye?”
fewer cigars for the struggling middleman.
Australia ought to cut £lO,OOO off the cost
of its representation through the Pie Com-
missioner’s office in London and spend it in
a cold-blooded inquiry into the reasons why
tropical products are wasted and the means
of stopping the sin.
Queensland’s new Governor told the
local Historical Society that he didn’t know
the country had any history. What! No
history? iJjas he been three weeks in the
State and no yet met the Macintosh, M.L.A.,
and heard the story o’ his kilts?
Queensland has £344,000 of short-dated
4 per cent, debentures coming home to roost
on July 1: and though the holders can have
the cash if they want it, they can also con-
vert to 4i Per cent, debentures if they like
that better. The new per cents" will
mature in 1925>. but will be redeemable at
the Government’s option- any time after
1920.
The desperate efforts made by Brisbane
hospitals to keep-afloat are really a public
scandal. Judging by the reports of the
General Hospital it seems that every visitor
to the city is., badgered for aid, or asked- to
do something to raise the wind. The con-
cerns simply live from hand to mouth. Yet
when Arthur Morgan, as Premier, introduced
a Bill to compel the landowners in the city
and suburbs to provide for the sick poor, a
noise went up in protest loud enough to be
heard in the Gulf, and the Upper House fired
out the measure with great gusto. They
liked the present policy of cadge a hundred
times better.
The luck of the seasons will be against
Labor-leader Ryan, even if he gets a majority
in the current general election. The mon-
soonal rains have done very little for most
of the big sugar districts this summer, and
it is upon the monsoons that the cane de-
pends. On present appearances the next
sugar harvest will be the poorest for many
years, and the one following will be affected
by the drought which has blocked planting
operations this season.
It is 50 years ago this month since Samuel
Griffith, now Chief Justice of Australia, was
awarded the Mort travelling scholarship by
the Sydney University, having won the
highest honors up to graduation. He was
a great student, a great lawyer, and he is
greater than all as a Judge. But probably
because he was made to be a great Judge
he was one of the worst politicians that ever
won to the Premiership of a State which has
suffered from many poor ones.
“P.”: Has Bishop Frodsham, once of
Townsville, got a- press agent too? A
recent number of the World’s 'WORK re-
marks concerning him: —
They were wont to call him the Citizen Bishop in
North Queensland on accou-nt of his wise leading: of
public affairs.
I know Townsville and North Queensland
well, yet I never once heard of a “Citizen
Bishop” or of the personally popular Frod-
sham’s alleged “wise leading in public
affairs.” I know he could drive a team of
horses, and that counted for something; but
as for “wise leading in public affairs”—
'rubbish!
Little Warra, on the Western line, is
wearing a bigger hat these times. Hitherto
it has been a bit proud of itself because
Jerry Jerome was a local product. Now it
is prouder, because it has discovered a
second black proposition. Coal has been
struck alongside the railway station, and
the find is being vigorously exploited. As
W ayra is about 80 miles out from Too-
woomba, on the Charleville line, the rail-
ways are watching it closely. Commissioner
Evans saw the mine the other day and then
gave the fuel a preliminary trial, with satis-
factory results.
Root, Hog—and Die!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Owing to a shortage of fodder nearly 1,000,000
German pigs are billetted in Belgium.
—London Daily Mail.
When Flanders late was overrun by swine
Hungry from Wilhelm’s land across the
Rhine,
The fields were rooted out
By many a greedy snout
That sought the hoggish wherewithal to
dine—
When Flanders late was overrun by swine.
The Belgian burgher trembled in the frost;
To hogs lie saw his daughter’s virtue tossed ;
The nose of foreign boar
AVas in at every door
And grunting was the password and the
sign—■
When Flanders late was overrun by swine.
The women rooted out from cot to street;
The children driven the road with wearv
feet;
The weak and lame , and old —
Sans hope, sans bread, sans gold—
Rooted from hearth and patriarchal vine
AA'hen Flanders late was overrun by swine!
The church was littered as a noisome sty,
The priest was rooted from his flock to fly
Before the anguished cry—
“ The Hogs are coming by !”
The flats of Flanders shivered to the brine —
When late the land was overrun by swine.
The tusks were deeply dyed a bloody red ;
They rooted even among the murdered dead ;
And. when the maw was full
And teeth were getting dull
They wallowed in the still undrunken wine—
When Flanders late was overrun by swine.
And now the Lord Hog sits aloft in state
And raises up his anthem all elate:
“ ’Tis I that wear the crown, .
The rest of you are down—-
Hear, hear me squeal in triumph o’er the
Rhine—-
This land is now the Province of the
Swine !”
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, April 24, 1915.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
Yice-royalty has been fairly occupied this
week. It visited the Southport flower show,
attended a fete at the Junction Park State
School, and on Friday night the head serang
was at the St. George dinner. To-day both
Exes, are due at a skating carnival in
aid of a patriotic fund, and Suburbia is
impatient to know if lie or She
intends to glide. When the Governor
and his missus engage with an un-
crowned Mayor and Mayoress in a rifle-
shooting contest, one never knows what to
expect. By the bye, in the rifle match, his
Ex. and Lady Goold-Adams each scored 63
out of a possible 70. Some spiteful people
hint that they addressed the marker as
“Charlie.” Mrs. Davey, the little Mayoress
of South Brisbane, notched 26, which was
three better than his Worship.
The Red Cross fete at the Junction Park
School was a social, artistic and financial
success. That was'the general result. As
for the details, Premier Denham made an
impassioned speech, urging all and sundry
to do their best for “our husbands” at the
Front. Lady Goold-Adams, whose voice is
really too gentle for public speaking, made a
pleasant little speech, and to the great joy
of the neighborhood managed to remember
exactly the number of garments made and
the amount of cash gathered by the
Stephens Shire for the boys at the Front.
The Ambulance Sports took place on the
same afternoon and drew crowds to the
South Brisbane Cricket Ground. Governor
Goold-Adams was again on deck, and masked
any sign of boredom that might have been
inwardly consuming him.
On Monday night one of Brisbane’s most
popular daughters, Miss Nerene Qualtrough.
was given over to Renton McSwaine, a
son of the late Rev. Dr. McSwaine,
of New Farm. She looked very charming in
a coat and skirt of white crfipe de chine and
a white sailor hat with a water-lily on the
brim. Miss Io Qualtrough was bridesmaid,
and brightened adjacent man’s outlook in
white chiffon taffetas and a black velvet hat.
The wedding was a guiet one, the directly-
affected families only being invited to the
feast, which was spread in the hospitable
Qualtrough dwelling. None the less, the
church was besieged by the girl friends of
the bride, who showered much confetti in
the aisle, to the boiling annoyance of an
elderly gent, in a black nightie.
Another wedding during the week was
that of Dr. Eric Cowlishaw, youngest son
of James Cowlishaw, M.L.C., to Miss May
Thurlow, second daughter of the late R. W.
Thurlow. The event was got off in the
drawing-room of the Thurlow homestead.
J- V. Black, a student at the Teachers’
Training College, has swapped his text-books
for a uniform and an assortment of iron-
mongery. He leaves for some part of the
Front with the next batch of Light Horse.
Tlie Brisbane Women’s Club met on
Thursday night, and listened to Mrs. Cliffe
Mackie’s postponed presidential address.
I learned that the club has added 75 mem-
bers since the beginning of the year. The
information was interspersed with music and
light refreshments.
The committee which has the matter of a
recreation room for soldiers in hand is still
looking for premises—which is annoying,
but not so annoying as it would be to have
to look for funds. In this case there is no
necessity to scratch for cash.
1 heard a woman at a picture-show", where
the fighting men of the Empire were being
screened, shrill out. as an ambulance clat-
tered down a hill. “Fancy anyone being in
there with a headache.” Sometimes, Thar-
gommdah, I wonder if the imagination of
our se_x ever reaches further than headaches
and afternoon tea !
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIAN JANE.
Further evidence that the North is no
good without colored labor in the fields was
available the other day when a couple of
Sll £. ar farms in the Mackay district were
sold at over £65 per acre —and that with a
poor year ahead, too. In spite of the
steady rise in the cost of white field-labor,
running to about 50 per cent, in the past
odvn n n^a ai ’ S^) he value of land I,as steadily
advanced. Of course the .country is no good
and the climate is rotten, and the owners
S oa and clemand these higher prices
simply to prevent other people buying in
and mining themselves.

“Mr; Chairman and Men of Australia, —
You ask me how Australia can help the | British Empire* Well, you
have already done your share in your noble sacrifice of men and equipment)
to help to right the Great Wrong—to uphold the integrity of that great)
Empire to which we are all proud to belong, and I can tell you that in the
records of these historic days, when millions of men meet in the clash of arms,
and the fate of Empires trembles in the balance, Australia’s part will be written
in imperishable history.
This much you have done, but there is still more to do. My thoughts are of
the mother country and of Australia. You can help both by buying Australian
products. Particularly can you'help Australia. Remember that, gentlemen,
and live up to it. It is your duty.
The article before us to-night is Chateau Tanunda Three Star Brandy,
distilled wholly from the fresh grape in South Australia, and bottled under the
supervision of the Customs officials of the Commonwealth Government, whose
seal guarantees its purity and age. It is one of the finest brandies in the
world. For medicinal purposes it is without a peer; as a beverage it is a superb
old spirit. . , <-,
Now, here is a brandy that is a credit to Australian skill, enterprise, labor
and capital. When you buy it you help Australia in a score of ways. You
keep the money in your own country. You help Australian labor. You assist
in the development of the Commonwealth. Your money spent here means
more wealth —more people—more general prosperity for Australia. There is
not a single, solitary reason why, when you ask for brandy, you should not
demand ypur own country’s product in preference to any other.
Mr. Chairman and Gentlemen, I ask you to lift your glasses to the Empire
and to Australia. The coming years are pregnant with great things. There is
a great, industrial expansion looming up. Your share will largely depend on
the amount of your patriotic purchases of Australian products. Develop the
resources of your land —call forth its powers—build up its institutions —promote
all its great interests —this is Patriotism. “Advance Australia!”
“Since Eighty-nine from Pure Grape Wine.”

An English Divorce Court the other day
granted Jessie Hannah Porter’s petition that
her matrimonial partnership with Caleb, in
Australia with Asche, should be wound up.
Caleb is still with Oscar, who is doing a
potted edition of “Kismet” in the ’alls.
“Melb”.: The Naval Battle at the Glaci-
arium, with real water and model ships, is
creditable so far as it goes, but does not go
far enough. Glance back nearly 60 years, to
“The Siege of Sebastopol” at Cremorne.
W. J. Wilson, afterwards of Sydney, painted
the enormous canvases, displayed in the
open air, behind the lake, with its ships. The
affair was really impressive. The guns
were heard, and the rockets were visible, for
a radius of miles around. Afterwards came
“The Eruption of Vesuvius,” equally well
managed. Then a lapse of many years, and
we had Pain’s “Destruction of Pompeii,”
behind another lake, in the Friendly Socie-
ties’ Gardens. If Australia’s interest over
the present war had been as intense as it
should be, surely there would be great open-
air military spectacles. We don’t even have
military or naval plays at the theatres.
“Samuel N.”: Do dramatic critics ever
read plays? I want to know. Thus the
S. M. Herald (15/3/’l5), referring to the
second curtain of “Bought and Paid For”:
A dramatic situation quite as audacious as this has
not hitherto occupied the modern stage of any coun-
try, so far as accessible records reveal.
Does Brieux’s “Maternity” come into the
category of “accessible records”? Broad-
hurst has simply “lifted” the curtain of
Act II of “Maternity,” using a situation of
tremendous dramatic force to provide a
“thrill” in an ugly story. No doubt about
it, there is audacity in tjie Yankee play—
in the barefaced plagiarism.
Samuel Wagner, who had a queer asso-
ciation with the very often parodied hymn,
“Hold the Fort,” was dug in the other day
at Illinois, U.S.A. Wagner, while a soldier
in the CiviP war, had on one occasion to
flag-signal al message from Sherman to
Corse, who was holding a pass against Con-
federate troops under great difficulties. He
flapped “Plold the fort —I am coming.”
Subsequently Wagner related the incident
to Sankey, who piously turned the words
into the chorus to a new hymn. That’s the
yarn anyhow.
“H. 0.”: Melbourne Repertory Theatre is
starting a play agency, which will read
dramatic masterpieces, at prices ranging
from 21s. to 525. 6d.; and it announces with
pride that it is “Under the patronage of J.
C. Williamson, Ltd.” When a repertory
theatre boasts of the “patronage” of a
frankly commercial concern it seems to me
that it has for all practical repertory pur-
poses put up the shutters.
“Kaniva”: Answered the last call, aged
60. Mrs. Bernard Beere, who made her Aus-
tralian debut at Melbourne Opera House
23 years ago in “As in a Looking Glass,”
supported by Marius, apd, among others,
that great actress, Henrietta Watson. Her
repertoire included a big range of old comedy,
Shakspeare and modern drama—she was ex-
cellent in Sardou’s works. Her last appear-
ance was at the London Coliseum in 1905 in
“Tim Spy.” Mrs. Beere was three times
married—first to Captain E. C. Derring;
then to Bernard Beere; finally, in 1899, to
A. C. Olivier.
Died the other week in London the violinist
w;ho was known to Australians of yore as
Jan Pechotsch, the infant prodigy. He
achieved a reputation in England under the
name of Rudenyi, and his death, at the age
of 28, is reckoned a real loss to the musical
world.
“Stargazer”: She felt hurt about it at
first, but soon was as cheerful as usual. Her
source of success lies in certain physical
qualities—an overplus embonpoint, a
marked shortness of leg, with a calf that
might outshine Rockefeller if it were golden ;
also a mouth that is in excess of all reason-
able requirements. When she seeks to shine
without stressing these points of hers she
goes for nothing;. but when she utilises her
physique for all it is worth in low-comedy
antics, the audiences vote her “immense.”
She was offered a 50 per cent, rise on her
present screw to join an American film co.
“With us you can let yourself go,” said the
agent. The actress was inquisitive, but it
appeared the agent only meant that she could
let. Imi’self grow. “They’re buying me by
weight.” moaned the artist; “but I’m
going!”
“E.T.”: Re the remarks of “Cockaigne”
concerning Wilson Barrett’s very up-and-
down career. The suggestion that “one
Kelly financed him at the Princess’s,” though
undoubtedly , warranted, applies to a much
later period than “Cockaigne” imagines.
Kelly was unknown in England when Bar-
lett produced “Lishts o’ London,” and the
later gold-mine, “The Silver King.” It was
long after he had “blewed” the "profits from
these two bonanzas that he attempted “Ham-
let.” Then it was that W. W. Kelly came
on the scene. Kelly had landed in London
P'om America with a few dollars and a lead-
ing actress, Grace Hawthorne. After several
flutters, to which the London public declined
to respond, Kelly produced the worst piece
«»f his repertory, “A 'Royal Divorce”—and
lived happily ever after. That’s how he got
the money to help Barrett. There seems to
be a moral attached to this story somewhere.
C. Ockney” : “Too Much Johnson,” now
being pkyed at the Palace, Sydney, by the
indefatigable Allen Doone, was, strange to
say, one .of the first big successes of the
lanky Yankee, actor-author, William Gillette.
-He adapted it from a French original, and
starred in America in the leading part for
years—long before he rose to fame as the
author of the “Sherlock Holmes” drama.
Also he played a season in the piece at the
Garrick, London; but as Hawtrey, had
already appeared in a comedy which came
from the same source, Gillette did not score
cither his “Secret Service” or “Held by the
Enemy” success. Why, by the way, does
not the enterprising J.C.W. firm let us have
another look at the latter piece—the best
war-drama of its kind? Brought up to date
“Held by the Enemy” should pull them in
as of yore.

IN DIVORCE.
In the Supreme Court of New Zealand,
Northern District.
No. 809.
BETWEEN LILLIAN FRANCES EMMA
CAMERON, Petitioner,
And HENRY ELLIOTT AUBREY CAM-
ERON, Respondent.
To HENRY ELLIOTT AUBREY CAM-
ERON, formerly of Auckland, but now
of parts unkown.
riAAKE NOTICE that a Citation bearing
JL date the 30th October 1914 has issued
out of this Court citing you to appear and
answer the Petition of Lillian Frances Emma
Cameron of Auckland praying for a dissolu-
tion of her marriage with you on the ground
that you have deserted her for five years and
upwards without just cause: TAKE
NOTICE that by an Order of this Court
dated 30th March 1915 it was ordered that
personal service of the Petition and Citation
herein be dispensed with and that service
be effected by advertising an Abstract to be
settled by the Registrar at Auckland in
The Bulletin once and in the Sydney
Morning Herald twice at an interval of a
week also twice at an interval of a week in
some daily newspaper published in San Fran-
cisco. and that appearance and answer be filed
within 90 days after the appearance of the
ast advertisement and that the trial be at
the first Civil Sittings of this Honorable
Court to be held at Auckland after the
expiration of 120 days from the appearance
of such last advertisement aforesaid. If
you wish to enter an appearance you must
within the time above mentioned attend in
person or by solicitor at the Registry of this
Court at Auckland and there enter an ap-
pearance in a book provided for the purpose,
in default of your so doing the Court will
proceed to hear the said Petition and you will
not be allowed to address the Court.
Dated at April this 10th day of April,
1915.
(Sgd.) R. G. THOMAS,
Registrar.
This Abstract is issued by Ralph Lionel
Ziman of Shortland Street Auckland Solici-
tor for the Petitioner. A copy of the
Petition may be inspected at the office of the
said. Ralph Lionel Ziinen or at the Auckland
Registry of the Court. Friends of the
Respondent are requested to forward this
advertisement to him.

“The beginning of last year my baby’s face
caught the sun and formed a dry rash; it
was red and ugly looking and looked bad.
She was getting her teeth so I put it down
at first to them, but it looked worse every
day. It used to irritate her and she would
scratch it and for the time it caused dis-
figurement. It was bad for over a month
and I really thought it was eczema.
“I tried other remedies but without any
success, so seeing the advertisement I sent
for Cuticura Soap and Cuticura Ointment.
From the first application it looked much
better. I washed it with the Cuticura Soap
using it freely and then applied the Cuti-
cura Ointment. I continued doing this
and she was soon cured and now no marks
remain." (Signed) Mrs. M, Higgins,
Apr. 24, 1914.

How to be Beautiful.
By RITA MOYA.
Tt- is not every woman’s good fortune to fee endowed by Nature with fine features and
a flawless complexion, but nowadays it is possible to make the plainest face attractive, and
that too in the privacy of one’s home, without recourse to beauty specialists which usually
involve expensive treatments and much loss of time. I shall endeavor to show you m the
following lines how much may be doue-'in this direction bv any woman of ordinary intelli-
gence When facial applications are necessary, use only the pure ingredients just as they
come to the chemist himself. Do not allow yourself to be persuaded into buying some
ch“ap ready-made preparation instead. Any chemist will gladly obtain the original con-
centrated ingredients for you. if you insist, and although he may not have them in stock,
Jou wllbe well advised to wait while he orders them for you. The improvement in your
appearance will be ample compensation for any trouble taken to obtain these simple and
harmless beautifiers.
Beauty in Breathing. —A great ileal can be
done towards brightening the eyes by system-
atic breathing for 10 minutes each morning
and evening. Breathe slowly and deeply to
the fullest capacity of the lungs. You should
stand erect by an open window. The coisets
should not be worn during this exercise.
About Shampooing.— Even the best shampoo
is somewhat drying, and if the hair is not
naturally oily I suggest that just before the
shampoo you apply olive oil to the scalp, rup-
bing it into the hair roots vigorously. Then
use pure stallax for the shampoo. Dissolve a
teaspoonful in a cup of hot water. This will
leave the hair very clean, soft and fluffy.
An Instantaneous Beautifler. —Instead of face
powder, use a simple lotion made from one
ounce of cleininite and four teaspoonfuls of
water. This lotion will tone and clear the
skin and acts as a protection against sun and
wind. A little applied with the finger-tips
instantly gives the skin a delightful “bloomy
appearance. No powder is necessary, and the
result lasts all day long under the most try-
ing conditions.
To Permanently Remove Superfluous Hair. —
It is a simple matter to remove a downy
growth of hair temporarily, but to remove it
permanently is finite another matter. It is a
pity that it’ is not more generally known that
powdered pheminol may be used for this pur-
pose. Apply it -directly to the hair. The
recommended treatment is designed not merely
to Instantly remove the hair, but also to event-
ually kill the roots entirely. Any chemist
should be able to supply you with an ounce
of nlieminol, which quantity should be suffi-
cient.
The Real Cause of Most Bad Complexions.—
It is an accepted fact that no truly beautiful
•omplexion ever «me out of jars and bottles,
and the longer one uses cosmetics the worse
the complexion becomes. Skin, to be healthy,
must breatlm. It also must expel, through the
pores, its share of the body’s effete material.
Creams and powders clog the pores, in-
terfering both with elimination and breathing.
If more women understood this there would
be fewer self-ruined complexions. If they
would use ordinary mercolised wax instead of
cosmetics they would have natural, healthy
complexions. This remarkable substance is
not absorbed by the skin; its action is just
the opposite. The skin repels mercolised wax,
and at the same time throws off all imperfec-
tions. An exquisite new complexion peeps
out. quite free from any appearance of artifi-
ciality. Apnly nightly, like cold cream, for a
week or two, washing it off in the morning.
About Hair Tonics. —Each week almost one
hears of some wonderful discovery for im-
proving the hair, and although this paragraph
may seem a little superfluous, an old-fashioned
recipe may coma as a welcome change. One
thing about it is that it will grow hair, and
also prevent it falling out. From your chem-
ist get an original package of boranium, to
this add 1-pint of bay rum, allow it to stand
30 minutes, then add sufficient water to make
half a pint. Rub briskly into the scaln with
the fincer-tips and you will immediately ex-
perience that clean, tingling sensation which
is a sure sign of healthy action. [2

The Inky Way.
There are now over half a dozen news-
papers published specially for the French
troops at the Front. The official daily is, of
course, the most important; but it is tame
compared with the others. It is devoted to
brief, official records of doings along the
whole 470 miles of lighting line, with at
times cheerful editorials by prominent men.
Then there are the Gazette de l’Oise, a tri-
weekly produced amid the whistling of the
shells at Soissons; the Democrat, published
at Dellemont, which gives most attention to
the struggle in Alsace; and the Poilu, one of
several papers circulating in the trenches of
the Champagne country and boasting a daily
sale of 10,000 copies. The most unique rag,
however, is the latest venture, the Echo Of
Shells, which appears every five days and
is edited by a poet who floods it with yards
upon yards of metred war drama.
* * * #
1
The latest competition dodge of the Lon-
don penny snippet weeklies is an offer of
big prizes—running up to £250 cash—for
the largest collection of paper “coins’"
which they print in each issue. The profit
comes in with the immense number of
papers bought by the not quite successful.
It is calculated that, in one recent competi-
tion, the £250 winner must have either
spent over £lOO in buying papers, or col-
lected vigorously from friends. Then came
prizes of £5O, £2O and 120 of five shillings.
The first of the 120 had weighed in with
“coins” cut from papers which must have
cost well over £3O. And he “won” five
bob!
* * * *
“S.D.”: Spencer Browne, whom we
Bananalanders have just sent off to be Colonel
of a Light Horse lot, is the Brisbane
Courier’s “leader,” and generally regarded
as the State’s best descriptive penman. But
lie is more than that. No old and footsore
traveller on the Inky Way ever poured his
woes into Spencer Browne’s ear in vain, and
youngsters turn confidently to him for use-
ful hints. When the “district” of the Journ-
nalists’ Association presented him with a gold
wristlet watch with the A..1.A. badge en-
graved on it, “the boys” told him all this
and lots more.
* * * *
On Rose-bushranging Day- in Melbourne
several females from the suburbs, inspired
by the highest motives, called at sundry busi-
ness places and offered to sell kisses as well
as roses. A skirted couple who invaded the
sub-editors’ and reporters’ rooms of one of
the dailies mentioned their willingness to
help on the cause at ss. per kiss. One high-
way-woman, on learning that a member of
the staff was the author of a tender rose
poem, published a day or two previously,
remarked coyly that he deserved a tender
reward gratis. But the poet was busy at
the time —writing something, as a matter of
fact, about the rise in the price of cheese.
* * * *
“Grenfell St.”: John Harvey Finlayson,
the old-time editor of Adelaide Regis-
ter, who passed out the other day
aged 72, conducted the clumsily-conserva-
tive journal for something like 20 years,
and nearly as long ago took up his resi-
dence in London as representative of the
paper, in which he held a big proprietary
interest. Soon, however, he got completely
out of touch with Australian press require-
ments, and when he came back, shattered
in health —for he was always a slogger—-
he retired finally from journalism. Finlay-
son was an up-to-date man in his day, but
was too deep in the conservative rut, and
should have given over the editorial reins
earlier than he did. Yet he was a man of
high character, and although brusque and
a trifle boorish possessed gallons of the
milk of human kindness.
» * * #
Young man, never mind Pitman. Get to
the camera. The competition for photo-
graphs amongst the big illustrated papers,
especially the cheap dailies, is very acute.
London Daily Mirror, which eats up sup-
plies of illustrations as a furnace eats coal,
is offering £lOOO for the best war photograph
sent in before the end of July. Second most
interesting picture will acquire £250 and
the third £lOO. Avoiding a swindle too com-
mon amongst publishers, the paper pays for
all photographs used. As a war recorder
the camera was first employed at the Crimea,
but very inextensively.
* * * *
The long-distance prophet of this war was
Japanese; as far back as 1907 a cartoon pub-
lished in Tokio showed a huge elephant with
the face of King Edward carrying four sol-
diers who represented England, Russia,
France and Japan. The happy family were
out hunting a tiger. Certainly the tiger was
indefinite. He may have been the Kaiser.
On the other hand, he may have been China,
or Woodrow Wilson, or the last man who
said the time wasn’t ripe.
A GOOD-NIGHT LOOK AT THE STARS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’d sat all day with my face to the wood ;
I’d scribbled all night till my eyes were dim ;
So I dropped the pen as a freeman should,
And passed from the room with the oily glim.
I opened the door to the waiting Night,
And stepped from the house with a thankful
sigh,
For, oh! I was weary of work and light
And the little low roofs that hide the sky.
As the dark closed round me, a cool wind stirred,
Like an anxious mother who comes and goes,
And I heard the cheep of a restless bird
While mosquitoes stabbed at my helpless nose.
Like the candles of God, the white stars burned,
As they still will burn when I’m dead as Troy,
And I thought of bed and a pound well earned,
And my heart within me sang for joy.
At the end of an indoor day and mean,
When the last fool-thing has been written down,
It is good to look at the stars serene,
And rest from the circus in which you’re clown.
Ere you fall to sleep in your living tomb,
Ere you bar the windows and clamp the chain,
Oh, steal out awhile where your thoughts have
room
And send them up to the stars again.

HIGHEST AWARDS AT ALL THE GREAT EXHIBITIONS. I
Phantasm.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f HUE gods of all the nations
That lean to hopes divine —
From those that pour libations
Uf sacramental wine,
To dim primeval giants,
The forebears of the race—
Went up, unseen by clients,
To gather in their place.
They came from misty ages
Receding into stone,
From all the human stages,
From every clime and zone,
From all the types and stations,
From mountain range and feu —
The gous of all the nations
Of all the sons of men.
The law that shall be nameless
And yet holds ever true;
Not errant, never aimless,
Not old, nor ever new ;
Had drawn the host together
As gravitation draws ;
The upper gods and nether,
Of endless cults and laws.
Assembled in their region
Where thought runs free of speech
Each unit of their legion,
Summed up the might of each;
For each looked on the other,
Whate’er his mien or span,
And knew him for his brother —•
A verv dream of man.
Beneath, like endless waters
Whereon no bark is buoyed,
Mankind and all his daughters
Rolled out across the void ;
Dim floods that never rested,
Despite the dark and cold —•
The ghosts of men, divested
Of countless forms of mould.
From distant vapors staring
A dim red globe appeared ;
No longer boldly glaring,
The sun-god’s eye was bleared,
Still gripped by gravitation
His planets round him hung;
Each in its dreary station
The ice-sheathed balls outswung.
Husked of its emanations
Of music, words and signs.
Trailed up the mind’s vibrations
As light through darkness shines,
Assailing that high meeting
With blasphemies sublime —
To stay its gods, retreating
Across the bars of Time.
Saying: “You gave us ever
A dream of wider scope,
A promise for endeavor,
A false ideal of hope.
You magnified our wonder.
You conjured thoughts divine
With faiths that, cleft asunder,
Our widowed souls would shine.
“You visioned some hereafter
For every make of man :
Meet not your dunes with laughter,
But aid them, if you can !
Lead on to some great portal
Of any heaven or hell,
Where harbor souls immortal,
And all may yet be well.
“We might, but would not. perish ;
We have forsworn the mould.
Out after-life we cherish
Though our old earth be cold ;
Then leave us not to groping
Through frigid glooms of space
At bitter ends with hoping
For some safe resting-place!”
As smoke, when clouds are raining,
Drifts through the drops of rain
Arose that vast complaining,
And ever rose in vain ;
The smoke of human sorrow
From homeless souls and sore.
Whose gods could give no morrow,
Nor aid them evermore;
Whose gods, in that dim region,
Were shadows out of reach.
For that phantasmic legion
Looked mutely each to each,
Each looked upon the other,
Whate’er his mien or span.
And knew him for his brother —
A very dream of man.
VICTORIA.
G. G. A. COLLES.
The Last Account.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THREE months?” Bernstein repeated
the doctor’s statement to him as a
question.
“It may be more, with care. At the
uttermost, six months. But I cannot hon-
estly hold out any hope of your living beyond
that.” Dr. Barton’s voice was quite ordinary
and matter-of-fact, it seemed to Bernstein.
He thought of Judges who snivel over pro-
nouncing the death-sentence.
“And may the Lord have mercy on your
soul!” he quoted grimly.
The doctor jumped. “I beg your pardon.”
lie said stiffly. “You asked for the truth, you
know.”
Bernstein had walked to the window and
was staring across at the motor-rank. A
blaze of scarlet geraniums in the North
Terrace flower-beds made a splendid patch of
color against the emerald green of the long
lawns outside Government House. All his
life—what remained of it—he would remem-
ber that scene, he thought.
He turned from the window. “You are
quite sure, doctor?” But there was no hope
in his voice. Barton elevated his eyebrows,
lie was a consulting specialist, and he did
not like to be doubted by a mere patient.
“You can consult any of” —he ran over a
list of North Terrace doctors—“all within
a stone’s throw; but I’m very much afraid
they will all concur with me. It’s not a
pleasant task to tell people they must look
forward to dying very soon? Mr. Bernstein,”
he added quaintly. "I hope you understand.
Your affairs”. ——- he suggested presently.
“Thanks for reminding me, doctor,” said
Bernstein. “I have a lot to do. Got to get
back to the Islands yet, you know.”
“A warm climate,” said the doctor by way
of tilling in an awkward pause, whilst Bern-
stein sorted out his fee.
The man who was to die so soon laughed
more boyishly than the six-and-thirty years
stamped on his face would have led one to
expect. . „ , . i «<p
“A good preparation, he remarked, tor
my future home.”
Then he went out in the sunlight wherein
people walked and lounged and chattered,
and the electric cars whizzed past with bells
clanging sharply, and the motor-car drivers
answered their calls, all as if the world were
going to last for ever. So Bernstein thought,
a little bitterly and a little foolishly.
lie had business to attend to in Melbourne
and Sydney, and it was close on a month
before he found himself at home again.
lie was an affectionate husband always,
so his home-coming presents were only a trifle
more elaborate than usual. Also, he was a
little less irritable with the children. That
was all.
He set his business in order, and assured
himself that there would be at least £4OO a
year for the wife when everything was
settled. She could leave the islands, lie re-
flected, and live very comfortably in Mel-
bourne on that. It would be better for the
children, too; for education at Mulwa Har-
bor was a negligible quantity. When they
grew up he hoped the boys, at least, would
feel the spell of the Pacific and return.
There would always be a friend or two
among the traders for “Bernstein’s boys.”
For the Jew had been everybody’s friend,
and no man in trouble —no man worth the
name of a man —had gone away unaided
while there was money or influence in
Judaea with which to relieve him.
Two of the three months were up before
he was satisfied concerning the future of his
wife and children.
Then he allowed himself the luxury of
looking into the past. The main thing in if
was the unsettled account of George Foster.
And it had been owing for 14 years. It was
the one bill that Bernstein, the punctiliouf
Hebrew, had allowed to stand over.
Bernstein and Foster had been trading
partners in the days of their hard-lived
youth. Foster was four years the elder, a
trifle under the middle height, and broad,
with a thick, pale skin, strong black brows,
and womanishly long-laslied, hard, brown
eyes. There may have been an unsuspected
taint of color in his make-up. The natives
called him “Fighting Face,” and shunned
him. The men, that is—the women loved
him. And the white men feared him more
or less, all save Bernstein the Jew, who
feared nobody.
Not content with the pick of the women
of a dozen islands who were his for the ask-
ing, Foster must needs poach on Bernstein’s
mats and rob him of his favorite. She was
an Ibsonberg girl, and in token thereof she
was large and very beautiful. Foster thought
lightly of her enough, and must needs think
the other man did the same. At the most, he ,
looked for a few high words and an hour or
two s sulking, the whole to be duly con-
cluded over a friendly bottle of square-face.
But Bernstein was of the jealous Jewish
nature, and, besides, had been fond of the
girl in his own fashion; and he had the
illimitable patience of his race. There was
a violent and bitter quarrel, resulting in a
breach between the two men that was never
bridged. Both had long since forgotten the
Ibsonberg girl, but the rancor she had started
grew with the years. And Bernstein didn t
kill Foster, because he couldn’t think of the
right kind of death to meet his case.
Where Foster could over-reach Bernstein
in trading he did so, and the Jew returned
the compliment with interest. The presence
of one man in a room was sufficient excuse
for the absence of the other. And the
accounts of petty insults and meannesses and
sharp dealing grew longer every day, for the
hatred became more tremendous long after
its original cause was forgotten, and as good
as dead to both of them.
Bernstein reckoned it time to balance up
now. When a man has but a month’s sure lease
of life he feels like that. He hated to die
in debt, but he had to wait Foster’s return
from a trading expedition. He bit his
finger-nails in this interesting occupation
for a week before the Hazy Star. Foster’s
tine new boat, slept on her iron in Mulwa
roadstead.
Two days after this Barry, the quarter-
breed Maori and boss trader of most of the
islands since Murray Hansen had “gone
bush,” took a farewell drink with Bernstein
in Sorrente’s Harbor Hotel.
“Good craft that,” said Bernstein, nodding
appreciatively toward the Hazv Star, rock-
ing lazily to the long, slow swell of midday.
“Yes,” said Barry. “She’s likely to stay
there a while, too, if Foster’s sciatica doesn’t
lot up on him a bit.”
“Seen him lately?” asked the .Tow.
“Said good-bye this morning. He’s pretty
bad; can just crawl round the house, lie's
sending down to Vila for his wife next week.
Flighty bit o’ goods she is.”
“I only asked,” said Bernstein, “because
he sent for me to-day, and I wondered if
he’d mentioned it to you. Reckons we’ve
been enemies long enough, or some other
damn rot.”
Barry stared at him. but he told the de-
liberate lie unflinchingly.
“He must be feeling mighty crook,” said
Barry. Then lie laughed. “You’ll miss each
other’s enmity after all these years. That
is. of course, if you’re going to make pals
again !”
“Oh. well.” and Bernstein looked a trifle
sheepish, “I think he’s about right. Frankly,
T’d like to bury the hatchet. I'll look in
this afternoon. I think.”

Why Are They Bent?
Not always on account of age, for j
many old people have straight backs
—and many young people have bent
backs. They are bent with the pains
of kidney disease, lumbago —nric
acid in the muscles of the loins, con-
gestion of the kidneys—the pains
that make the young feel old, and
the old feeble and shaky.
Rest in bed may ease a bad back,
but it cannot cure it. Only a medi-
cine that reaches the cause can do
that. Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills
are solely for weak and ailing kid-
neys. They help the kidneys to
throw off uric acid poison, which is
the cause not only of backache and
lumbago, but of gravel, stone, rheu-
matism, dizzy attacks, irregular
heart, and that heavy, drowsy feel-
ing. Doan’s Pills regulate 1 the
urinary system like ordinary medi-
cines regulate the bowels; they
release the accumulated water in
dropsy, and relieve bladder weak-
ness, which is so common in children
and the aged.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills are
pure; and cannot injure the most
delicate patient.

“That’s right,” said Barry.. “I heard
Louis and Martindale say they were going
to see him this afternoon, too. They were
to meet here somewhere near a quarter to
four.” .
He shook hands with Bernstein and
walked out to beat up his crew and put off.
"So long,” he called laconically. “See you
again in a couple o’ months.” “So long,”
called Bernstein, cheerily, but he said
nothing about seeing him again. For in his
mind’s eye an electric car sped along the
greasy-looking rails on the North Terrace of
Adelaide once more, and a red, red patch of
geranium flamed incongruously on the shore.
lie went home and waited till three o’clock.
Then, when every self-respecting person in
Mulwa was taking a siesta, he walked to
Foster’s house. He felt weak, and it was
a good half-hour’s tramp, and the shade of
the great verandah gave hint of an inviting
coolness within the great bungalow. The
western side was built up on piles, for the
ground there sloped sharply to the sea. A
spur of coral, running crosswise, barred the
waters off, but at high tide the wash bioke
over this sometimes and crept almost to the
piles themselves.
Bernstein stood a moment on the veran-
dah and pulled himself together. lie
knocked sharply, and presently Charlie, a
sleepy-looking Chinese boy, answered his
summons. His slant eyes opened almost to
the width of a white man’s with astonish-
ment when he saw who stood there, for every-
one knew of the feud.
“Your boss is in'?” It was more of a
statement than a question.
“I go see,” said Charlie. lie was evi-
dently puzzled. “I go see,” he repeated.
But Bernstein reassured him. “It’s all
right, Charlie,” he said easily. “Mr. Foster
and me friends now, topside all right. Boss’
him send word alonga me this morning. He
want to shake hands now.” He smiled so
frankly that Charlie believed him (after
Mr. Foster had said he expected visitors),
and led the way to the big room on the
western side that the owner of the house
had made his den, because it looked out over
the sea he loved.
Foster turned carelessly enough as Bern-
stein came into the room. Then he moved
so quickly that the sharp knife of sciatica
stabbed him again and again.
The Jew smiled pleasantly—he liked to
see Foster’s face twitch like that.
“Heard you were pretty crook, Foster,” he
said, “and I’m feeling none too good myself.
Fact is, I’ve got my marching orders, and I
thought I’d like to bury the hatchet.”
Foster stared at him amazedly. “Fact,”
said Bernstein again. “I’ve only three weeks
to live, I believe, on the opinion of the best
doctors in Australia, and I’d like to be pals
for the sake of old times. Anyway, she's
dead, you know,” he concluded abruptly.
After a minute’s hard thinking Foster
recollected the Ibsonberg girl. He recol-
lected a good many other things at the same
time, but, after all, Bernstein had done a
generous thing in coming to see him like
this. He held out his hand and the two
men clasped hands for the first time in years.
Charlie, hanging about in the doorway,
could not catch what they were saying, but
he saw this display of friendliness and moved
off quite satisfied.
There was the usual Jewish touch of his-
trionics about Bernstein. Ho Foster thought
contemptuously when presently that gentle-
man pulled his revolver out of his pocket and,
walking to the window, flung it far out with
a fine, dramatic gesture. “That sort of
thing’s done with between us, old man, eh?”
he said with a sigh of relief. He had watched
the weapon sink deep in the wet, oozy sand.
Already the surface of the shore was undis-
turbed again.
“I suppose so,” assented Foster. He
Avas not so dramatically extravagant as the
other. He drew his “gun” out slowly and
placed it on the table. Bernstein noticed
that he had attempted to rise, no doubt with
the intention of placing it on a shelf at the
further end of the room, but he found rising
impossible, and sat down again.
“Get a seat for yourself, won’t you?” be
remarked, and pushed a syphon and bottle
of Avhisky within reach on the Avide, bare
table.
“Thanks,” said Bernstein. “Here’s hoAv!”
It Avas surprising how easy they found it
to be friendly again. Sometimes a slight
constraint crept into the conversation, but
the JeAV talked rapidly and excitedly, with
quick, impatient gestures. The other man
thought he had been drinking.
“D’ye remember, ‘Fighting Face’ ” —he
Avas saying in a thick, uneven Amice, and
Foster, whose attention had been Avander-
ing. recalled himself Avith a start at his
native nick-name.
He looked across at Bernstein and in his
eyes read something of the truth at last.
He leaned forward, as if to pay him the
closer attention. He drummed his fingers
idly on the table. Then, quite steadily,
without any undue haste, he. reached over
for his revolver. But Bernstein was watch-
ing e\ r ery movement, and he thrcAV aside the
cloak of friendliness abruptly.
“D’ye remember?” he said again, and
snatched the revolver un himself. It was
past four o’clock, and there Avere footsteps
on the rough-eoralled pathway. It seemed
somehow that he had been Avaiting for these.
Foster heard them also, and Avould have cried
out, but the other had him covered.
Very briefly, Bernstein told him of his
interview with the doctor in Adelaide. “So
it’s only a week or two less or more for me,
anyway,” he said. “Keep still.” he added.
“Oh! I’m not going to shoot you, you prize
idiot. You always were a damned ass,
Foster! It’s only a matter of a few paltry
days less or more to me, anyhow; but it’s a
murder charge, and Avhat’s left of a long
life, most likely, to you. I was determined
on that, but I didn’t reckon on such luck as
being able to finish the game to-day. I only
meant to get friendly, for a start. I didn’t
dream you’d give me such a splendid chance,
though I half-hoped it when I chucked my
gun out of the window so dramatically.”
Foster got on his feet somehow as he heard
Louis’s Amice asking Charlie for him.
But it Avas Bernstein who shouted “Help !”
and “Murder!” with a grim smile on his
mouth before he turned the revolver on him-
self and fired. He smiled still with a thick,
scarlet froth betAveen his lips in little Avork-
ing bubbles.
There was the crash of an overturned
chair as Foster, his sciatica forgotten for
the minute, came at him.
“Quits!” muttered Bernstein, indistinctly.
The clinging red slaver dribbled about his
mouth with each pumping breath. He rocked
in an intolerable agony for a moment, then
dropped the revolver and clasped his hands
beneath his heart, where a great stain was
spreading on his white clothes. His lower
teeth were locked over the upper set in a
fashion horrible to see through his stretched
and parted lips. Then he dropped face down-
ward as Louis and Martindale and the
Chinese boy got into the room. When they
turned him over it seemed to Foster that
those lips shaped the name of the lbsonberg
lass.
LILLIAS GORDON.
On the Practical Utility of
Taking a Large Size in
Gladness.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LIKE fourpence falling on a tray
The golden bangles on her wrist
Made music at the close of day
Beneath a sleeve of lacy mist;
And it was good to sit with her
And hear the placid evening purr.
The balcony was wide and high,
The harbor murmured cool and wet,
The ferry craft went gliding by
And howled derision when they met;
And I was quite content that she
Was thirteen stone and on my knee.
It may be that I could have found
A damsel of a lighter make,
For this dear maid had grown so round
I suffered somewhat for her sake;
Yet the large dimple on her chin
Seemed worth a small man’s while to win.
There is no standard make in love,
Proportion fades quite out of sight,
The sweetness of the cooing dove
May go with tresses dark or light,
And slim romance he does not miss
Who takes the largest size in bliss.
Sweet young destroyers through the street
On glancing ankles come and go,
The smiling cruisers that I meet
Have conquered merchantmen in tow;
But it seemed wondrous good to me
To nurse a Dreadnought on my knee.
But just as love and weight combined
To crush me flatter than the snail
The garden-roller leaves behind
A-squelcli upon the asphalt pale,
My thin remaining soul was ware
Of some large tumult in the air.
Round the warm edges of my love
I saw a sudden drift of smoke,
And through the gloom some voice above
The echoes of the evening woke
With howls of “Fire!” that let me know
The building was aflame below.
My lady shrieked and swooned away.
Hot tongues of lire beneath us roared.
I felt the groaning timbers sway
As towards our perch destruction soared ;
I climbed from under that sweet girl
With all my brain and heart a-whirl.
Then I observed that far below
A mob of firemen, topped with brass,
Were rushing madly to and fro
Upon the strip of garden grass,
And that a small and sudden jet
Of water made the flames look wet.
I called aloud to ask them if
They had a monoplane or two,
Or any sort of aerial skiff
To help the entertainment through,
And whether ere the roof went wrong
They meant to send the things along.
The firemen swarmed like angry gnats,
They squirted water up like mad.
I saw the shining of their hats;
But that which made me really sad
Was that the poor folk overhead
Would, like ourselves, be shortly dead.
I mentioned this in one long yell.
My lady lay a lifeless heap,
Prone on the floor where first she fell;
I raised her in my arms to leap,
And someone very far below
Bawled “Hurry up ! Your only show !”
They spread a net to break our fall.
I staggered to the giddy edge.
My lady hung like some large sliawd
That droops across a window ledge
But heavy as five bags of coal
I found the casing of her soul.
I lifted her above the rail.
Her weight o’erbalanced and we dropped.
Through leagues of air we seemed to sail
Till with a rending bump we stopped—
I saw wild ends of string aloft
And wondered that the world was soft.
Like fourpence falling on a tray
I heard a golden bangle clash.
I gazed around. My lady lay
Right in the centre of the smash,
And I had landed like a dove
Upon the very breast of love.
For our twin souls had burst the net.
The selfsame net that broke our fall;
And there was nothing to regret
Because, my own weight being small,
I scarce had crushed the snowy lace
Upon my lovely landing place.
My lady wakened from her swoon,
Bruised from her contact with the ground ;
Her first remarks were wildly strewn
In gusts of incoherent sound :
Then looking upward she beheld
The flames that scorched and leaped and
yelled.
At once she seemed to understand.
Remembrance sparkled in her eye.
She pawed me with a gracious hand
And breathed a little tender sigh,
And glad was I my nobler taste
Ran to a generous girth of waist.
N.S.W.
PAT O’MAORI.
